CRUDE stuff, with something virile,
sturdy, human beneath its uncouth-
ness, is the latest novel of G. B. Lan-
caster, The Tracks We Tread (Doubleday,
Page, New York, per The Book Lover, Melb.).
Lancaster, whose pen-name conceals, as is
well-known, a young Maoriland girl, writes
with the most astounding masculinity. Sons
o’ Men and A Spur to Smite, her two earlier
books, were studies of Outback, swiftly
etched, starkly visualised, yet so hastily, so im-
petuously done that her work read roughly—
a hedge of prickly obstacles behind which we
caught tantalising glimpses of the thing she
sought to show. A Spur to Smite suffered
from an absurd and impossible plot—pro-
bably the' only sign so far apparent
of Lancaster’s sex, or, possibly, youth.
But behind its turbulence of phrase that
novel showed a real knowledge of the pas-
sions of simply-built men ; with the finer
nuances of more subtle beings—the appa-
rent special province of women writers—
Lancaster has little to do. A Spur to Smite,
conformed, as has here been pointed out, to a
rapidly evolving type of Australasian novel.
It is essentially a novel of action. Pictur-
esqueness of phrase, violent action, rapid
impressions of life, simplicity of character,
above all, the strength, and oftentimes
cruelty, of youth—all these are to be found
in greater or lesser degree in the novels of
Randolph Bedford, T. B. Clegg, Barbara
Baynton, and G. B. Lancaster. So it looks
as if the type—or let us say, the most arrest-
ing and characteristic type—of the Austral-
asian novel is already taking form, is
already, almost, being fixed in a formula.
But this type of the novel of action,
dealing with the primitive passions of
men and women, face to face with the
wilder aspects of Nature, is not peculiar
to Australasia. All it has of special interest
to us is its local setting, its Australasian
vocabulary. The same novel of small
subtlety and less reserve is found rampant in
the Yukon, in Colorado, in Insia. Jack
London, Stuart Edward , White, Gertrude
Atherton, and—in some of his earlier stories
—Rudyard Kipling, show us men who are
simply masculine in the grip of passions
simply primitive. It is essentially a man’s
novel—and two women are busily and
successfully writing it.
The Tracks We Tread marks no breaking
away from the somewhat limited formula of
the type. Yet this latest of Lancaster’s
works is an achievement which encourages
hopes of finer things. Here we have no im-
probable plot,, no extravagant situation, no
lush sentimentality. The Tracks We Tread
are the tracks of men Outback in
Maoriland—to be precise, in South Otago.
Lancaster takes the fortunes of a dozen men
upon a big cattle and sheep station ; she
puts them in up-country bars, in shearing
shed, saw-mill, sluicing-claim and whare ;
and women, except as small cogs in the
wheels of action, have little to do. And the
women are the least convincing of her
character studies. The men, however, are
abundantly alive, quarrelling, working, fight-
ing, galloping, shooting, dancing, drinking,
losing their souls and saving them, loving
and hating each other as man never loved nor
hated woman. And their one commandment
is loyalty, their one religion manliness, their
one duty work. It is a simple creed.
l ’A man’s not a man without a splash of the
brute in him. They are not pretty ; but they’re
tough. The bush won’t have weaklings. I bully-
dam them from the jump, and if they play up
they know it. But, bless you, if I kick them out
they come back the next year with their tails
down. It’s like to like, and no other job can hold
them for long. When the bush calls they’ve got
to answer, if it strips half their life off ’em.”
The yarn has half-a-dozen heroes, and all
of them are kept busy. FrOm this story
one would glean the impression that in
South Otago things happen. The men do
strenuous things and do them all the time.
Lancaster is evidently intent on acclimatising
in M.lji. the ferocious cow-boys of the
American wild west'; and insofar the book is
false. Broken limbs and wounds are but
casually mentioned.
Scott strained his colt’s stifle on a shingle slip
when he tried to prevent a stampede, and Megger
put out his elbow when he left the saddle in a
blind creek. But he pulled it in again by aid of a
stirrup-leather, and collared his bolting’ mob on
the slope below.
This incident seems somehow to have
strayed from our own “ Aboriginalities ”
column :
The gate swung in file wind, and Lou caught it
as Ike’s cob struck i/, full with his chest. The
rebound east Lou three yards down the track
where the pack-horse pinched his arm with a
hind-shoe as it jumped him. Lou twisted ;
snatched at a mane that blew in his face, gripped
the nostril, the mane, forced the bridle he carried
between the teeth and over the ears, and came up
astride, with the cheek-strap flapping.
Lancaster is apt to over-do the adven-
turous. The yarn, however, goes with a
swing, and the vividness of the picture com-
pensates for its exaggeration.
The style, as has been hinted, suffers
from a plethora of simile. Lancaster can
picture an incident with an admirable
economy of phrase ; but often she overlays
the plain fact with too elaborate detail.
For an example of her striking diction,
take the simile of men riding in single file
up a steep track :
Then, as buckets climb the dredge-ladder, they
came one by one to the broken hill-track, and
paused.
And how vividly and accurately Lancaster
can depict the essentials of Maoriland !
The night was cold, with grey blankets over the
hills, and a soft mist rolling along the river. . .
The rock shivered where the jet struck it, and
sunk forward in a puddle of yellowish wash.
Fysh dipped his wrist a fraction, and the water
dug out a big manuka bush, tossing it over into
the night beyond. Then the steady roar blattered
on rock again, sending a comb of white smoke
above the light-arc. . .
The moon swept out from the thick black for
two breaths. Through sleet like the bars of a
cage Ormond saw the great hump of the Lion
Mountain stripped into naked lines and sleek
with streams. Below and brought forward to the
eye, a thousand rivers galloped through scrub and
round bluffs ; spilling side-ways into the bubbling
gullies, and coasting down the Spurs with heads
of foam. Somewhere in the midst of that hell,
the mining race was going out. Somewhere, in
or below it, Bert Kiliat and a dozen more were
racing for life....
The hail shut off with the suddenness of a
beaten stick dropped from a kerosene tin ; and
the boys gasped, stood upright, shook the blood
from battered faces and hands, and took hold of
the mobs again. Round and underfoot thumped
the thunder with the earnestness of a steam-
hammer in full work, until the throb was cut now
and again by the sharp crackle that made the
boys jump,and the out-flung anger of lightning.
Far over on an unseen face came the roar that
was neither thunder nor wind, nor the roll of balls
down a skittle alley. The top of a big tree sliced
the mist for an instant as it pitched, forward.
Then the mighty groan of the parted slip filled
earth, and rang against the sky until it settled
in silence far down in a gully-bottom.
“There’ll Ire jes’ the naked skull o’that hill
grirmin’ at us in the mornin”,’ said Moody,
startled to speech. Then he shivered in the
soaked rags that clang to him. For it was quite
possible that the Brothers might also stand up
skull-bare, and grin over the death that lay hid at
the bottom.
The shingle tread where four men worked was
sloppy as the wash in a dredge-bucket ; and the
sheep stumbled on it, weighted with water, and
crying against the unwearied menace of the dogs.
And still the chain dragged forward, pulling all
life with it : and still the rain pelted straightly,
settling in behind the earth ; loosening, loosening;
and still the boys counted the ridges yet to be won
before the easy slope of the Dome shoulder gave
clean foothold of tussock and broom.
Note the force and accuracy of epithet in
these examples : — blattered ... a comb of
white smoke . . . sleet like the bars of a cage
. . . sleek with streams . . . thumped the
thunder. .... sloppy as the rvash in a
dredge-bucket. The dangers of the pithy
epithet are exemplified in the “ beaten stick
dropped from a kerosene tin.” Here the
simile has been dragged violently in, and
though the meaning is discernible, it is not
clearly expressed. But this meticulous
criticism is made because Lancaster is worth
criticism. The Tracks IVe Tread is a novel
that—within its class—may confidently be
ranked with all but the very best of that
class. Its chief and glaring fault is its in-
distinctness. It is hard reading ; often it is
impossible to see the forest for trees.
But in G. B. Lancaster Maoriland possesses
a novelist of limited range, but within her
limits of fine achievement. F2.
THE RED PAGE
THE AUSTRALASIAN NOVEL
Quite the other type of Australasian novel
—the easy-going, easy-humored, casual collec-
tion of incidents—is furnished by Steele
Rudd. Hitherto Steele Rudd has occupied
himself with the grotesque, cynical humors
of his “ selection ” ; he has moved to his new
selection, to Sandy’s selection, and once
more back to his own selection. But now
he has dropped the careless humors of Dad
to introduce us to The Poor Parson (N.S.W.
Bookstall Co.). And it is quite time ; for
Australia was getting possibly a little tired
of the grotesque and exaggerated humors of
Da'd. That old man was really in danger of
dying from over-work ; and he was too
delightful a character to bring to so shameful
a end. The poor parson is altogether a
different person, a quiet, uncomplaining,
hard-up, self-sacrificing minister with a large
family—a type to be found anywhere in
the West. The members of his congregation
are skilfully and sympathetically drawn, and
the story of hardships and humor saunters
carelessly along in the old Steele Rudd
manner, and keeps us pleasantly in-
terested. Australia—especially the Aus-
tralia of the back blocks—dikes that sort of
book; it is just the sort of book that the
back-blocks would write itself—if it had the
/ V
ability and the energy, and there weren’t so
many convenient posts to lean against.
Steele Rudd had worked that selection too
long, and with advantage it might be allowed
to lie fallow while he turns his attention to
common-place people like the poor parson.
For Steele Rudd knows his Australia. It is
curious that there seems a slight boom in
back-block parsons at present. Another
excellent study of the genus, The Man From
Gurdie’s River , by Donald Maclean, was
recently reviewed on this page. The Poor
Parson may possibly mark a transition stage
in Steele Rudd’s literary development. He
has relinquished the grotesque, and is settling
down to the conscientious study of character.
The N.S.W. Bookstall Company must be
commended for its practical encouragement
of an Australian author by its courage in
paying high prices for his work, and the
admirable production of his books.
Ethel Turner’s latest story, The Stolen
Voyage (Ward, Lock, London), shows no
falling off in her facility for writing interest-
ing, and not goody-goody, books for children.
Perhaps it is because they are Australian
children that they do such audacious things;
but the charm of childhood is there always,
and the book is more than readable by
grown-ups. It is hardly recognised here
that Ethel Turner is probably the best living
writer of children’s books ; while other Aus-
tralian writers hardly challenge comparison
with the best novelists over-seas, Ethel Tur-
ner has, in her not too ambitious niche,
made out her claim to permanent possession
of that niche. The Stolen Voyage deals
with the experiences of a lad with a fondness
for the sea, who, in order to get a voyage to
Maoriland, takes the place of a boy who
hates sea-sickness, and even sacrifices a
sound tooth at a dentist’s in order to get his
trip. Humor and adventure are abundant.
A collection of really good yarns, judged
by any standard, is Midnight’s Daughter and
Other Stories , written by W. G. Henderson,
an Albury resident, and published by Chap-
man and Hall (per G. Robei'tson, Sydney).
The yarns are all Australian, and all well-
constructed and interesting. Henderson
does not write like an amateur. “ The
Courting of Christina ” and the title story
are excellent stuff, and, like most of the
other yams, confine themselves to subjects
of pre-eminently human interest-—girls and
fights. Henderson might conceivably turn
out a good Australian novel.
And now we hail the first servant-girl in
fiction who says “ Perchance.” Broda
Reynolds, in her first novel, A Black Silk
Stocking (H. E. Reynolds, Sydney,), has
achieved that fame-. This is exactly the sort
of story that a servant girl, whose sole read-
ing was The Family Herald and Corelli,
might write. It is an affecting tale of the
tribulations of a Sydney girl, and is illus-
trated with probably the worst black-and-
white work ever seen within a book. But,
despite its sensationalism, its slovenly style,
its pervading commonness of thought, the
book shows promise. The writer possesses
a fund of knowledge about the “ shady ”
phases of Sydney life that she knows effect-
ively how to use. The heroine, who has
“ splendid shoulders and hips,” is in-
evitably spoken to in a tram by a
gentleman “ of good girth measurement.”
Subsequently this good girth measurement
becomes “a toad-like corporation.” The
owner of the black silk stocking—only one is
mentioned—becomes waitress, nurse, and
servant, and accepts situations in places not
entirely reputable ; but virtue is ultimately
triumphant, and she is able to refer to herself
as ‘ ‘ the daughter of Lord Owen Castleworth
and niece to half-a-dozen earls.” But,
despite the vulgarity, the audacious frank-
ness of her observations—“ A man in his
pyjamas does not look to a woman what a
woman in her night-dress looks to a man ” —
Broda Reynolds possesses a sense of dramatic
situation and a certain naturalness of touch.
When the husband of the niece of six earls
breaks things and .expectorates in his wife’s
bedroom, she simply goes to the dining-room
and gets drunk. It seems the most obvious
thing to do; but, so far, it is the first
natural solution of a situation upon which
novelists have expended much futile literary
ability. Broda Reynolds has power, but it
is overshadowed by her servant-maid’s point
of view.
AUSTRALIAN ROOKS
A charming Christmas gift from Australia is
the booklet titled, From Range to Sea: A Bird
Lover's Ways, written by Charles Barrett, with
photographs of bird life by A. H. E. Mattingley,
and published in dainty fashion by T. C. Lothian,
Melbourne. In a preface Donald Macdonald
points out that the booklet is modestly intended
to be just an enlarged Christmas card—or the
preface to a more complete work.
Round the World, by an Australian Native
(Herbert H. Wettenhall), from the same pub-
lisher, whose production of booklets must exceed
that of all the other publishers in the Common-
wealth, is just an informative account of a bush-
man’s trip, illustrated by snap-shots, and, appa-
rently, reproductions from picture post-cards.
A Golden Shanty, Edward Dyson’s popular
volume of mining stories, whose publication by
The Bulletin achieved a remarkably successful
sale, and which has been for some time out of
print, has now been re-issued by George Robertson,
Melbourne (Is.), with the illustrations by Phil
May. Dyson’s humor is here seen at its freshest.
Science Gossip, by “Gossip” ( The Sydney Stock
and Station Journal), is an interesting collection
of articles that might well be put into the hands
of any boy. For all boys are interested in the
little local things he writes about.
The first part of a highly-interesting botanical
publication, Illustrations of N.S. W. Plants, a
companion volume to Forest Flora of N.S. W., has
been issued by J. H. Maiden (Govt. Botanist).
The work is intended for lovers of our flora, and
not mainly for skilled botanists; and Maide«
hopes to bring into the compass of one volume the
indigenous flora (plants—not trees) of this State.
Some have never been depicted before, and others
in works of such rarity as to be practically inac-
cessible, even to botanists. In this part the
boronia, the flannel flower, the needle bush, and
other N.S. W. plants are described and depicted.
Photographs of the plants in flower, in addition
to the lithographed plates, would be of use for
identification.
“The Rise and Fall of the Mother-in-Law
Jcke” is a book announced that will be awaited
wi>h tremendous interest.
FROM IRELAND.
I lcnead and I spin, but my life is low the while,
Oh, I long to be alone and walk abroad a mile.
Yet if I walk alone, and think of naught at all,
Why from me that's young should the wild tears
fall ?
The shower-stricken earth, the earth-colored streams,
They breathe on me awake, and moan to me in
dreams,
And yonder ivy fondling the broken castle-wall,
It p-ulls on my heart till the wild tears fall.
The.cabin-door looks down a furze-lighted hiU,
As far as Leighlin Cross the fields are green and
still ;
But once I hear the blackbird in Leichlin hedges
call
The foolishness is on me, and the wild tears fall.
MISS GUINEY
(in Putnam’s).

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Nonagenarian.
He groweth old, as all men grow ;
The lion-strength is on the wane ;
His actions now are cramped and slow ;
His hands, that fifty years ago
Gript hard, are marred with pain.
Care drew deep lines about his eyes
That one time kindled with the spring •
His ears, that heard so many cries
Go up unheeded to the skies,
Are dumb to suffering.
Once grand ambitions thrilled him through;
Strong passions urged him divers ways;
On soaring wings of Hope he flew ;
But now he craves this lesser due—•
In peace to end his days.
His eyes, that long had stood him stead
To read himself into the times,
Have dimmed at length ; within his head
He joins the living and the dead—
Mixed death and marriage chimes.
Though deafness hath assailed his ears,
Perchance he hears, as on a shore,
The rising tide of time, that nears
His house at last; perchance he hears
The sapping at the door !
And yet an ancient instinct clings
Within his soul, and bids him stay—
Though Hope may spread no more its wings—
Down to the bitter end of things,
Than go the unknown way.
VlO.
O. G. A. COLLES.
Philosophy.
Enchained within a cave, mankind
Can only see, reflected
From the Realities behind,
Some shadows faint projected.
So Plato says, and Hume and Kant
Are hardly more explicit.
Small help they give to us who want
To catch the truth—and miss it.
The lips we kiss, the pipe we smoke
(According to our ages),
Considered deeply, they evoke
Thoughts which have puzzled sages.
The Moon that shineth overhead,
The steadfast Earth beneath her,
May haply be, as Kelvin said,
But “ vortex rings in ether.”
The tale that all we feel and see
Is merely an appearance,
Is difficult to grasp—for me—
And seems to lack coherence.
When Spencer writes on Time and Space,
Or even Social Statics,
I feel as if I had to face
The higher mathematics.
One Darwin unto us was given,
And Haeckel is his prophet.
Between them they abolished Heaven
And made an end of Tophet.
The old beliefs that we were taught
Are covered in confusion,
And now the Master Key is sought
By means of Evolution.
Philosophers the air may smite,
And Scientists may squabble.
I think I’ll cast my lot to-night
With the unlettered rabble ;
Or, better still, we two, apart
Prom vulgar observation,
Will study problems of the Heart—
A question of sensation.
MAORILAND.
TAIWA.
An Autumn Day.
To-day recalls an autumn day,
We fled dull city corridors,
To share with dreaming coves the spray
And silvern whispers of the shores.
Brown earth smelled sweet of autumn rain
And seeding grass, and undismayed,
Where summer-gods had feasting lain,
A solitary floweret stayed.
Calm sea and sky were silver-grey,
Soft wafters breathed the crisp sea-tang.
And singing things, in love-lorn lay,
Of summer’s stolen glory sang.
And you were exquisite and warm
With love’s desires and comradeships,
And pulsing yearnings that transform,
As lightning-fire, soft eyes and lips.
And while I sketched the cool, grey shore
You murmured little scraps of verse,
And we together pondered o’er
The sweetest-voiced philosophers.
Sometimes you lit a cigarette
And dropped the ashes on the leaves,
And laughed—and lest you should forget,
I kissed your lips as Adam Eve’s.
No thought of morrow’s irk distressed,
The day’s luxuriousness enthralled ;
Maybe the sadness never pressed,
Sea-waves foreboded when they called.
And we were happy while the sun
O’erleaped brown hills and purpling glades.
And curious star-eyes, one by .one,
Watched us amid the mist-blue shades.
Swift years soon mingle joy and tear,
Brown autumn leaves too soon decay,
And ah ! your sleep is sound, my dear •
I pondered there alone to-day.
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.

Jlorv to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin icould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin," is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. 'The person may not be there; the official
ahvays is.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 19, 1907.
THE IRON INDUSTRY.
A few days ago the N.S.W. iron and steel
industry, being in great trouble, applied to the
State for assistance, and it is pleasing to record
that it didn’t apply altogether in vain. The
Legislature rose in moderation, and recognised up
to a certain point the urgency of the case, and
rushed gradually to the rescue with limitations.
The promoter of the Lithgow undertaking had
made a great effort to get the public to subscribe
the necessary capital, and the less said about his
failure the better. In N.S.W. the public has no
faith in manufactures. The manufacturer has to
pay railway rates that the most infuriated
economist wouldn’t dream of asking from the
farmer. Being an urban person he has to pay
local taxes from which the N.S.W. farmer has
been exempt for a century. He has to
work his men eight hours where the farmer
expects ten or twelve, and to pay wages that no
one, even in a nightmare, expects the farmer to
pay, and which are, for each hour’s work, about
double those of his English competitors. He has
sometimes been grudgingly allowed a measure of
Protection which covered about half the extra
charges levied upon him as compared with his
foreign rivals, and sometimes he hasn’t been
allowed even that. When he is allowed anything
he is called a parasite and a pauper for taking it.
Whether he is allowed anything or nothing he is
alluded to by that public enemy, the blatant rich
importer, and by the tame papers which feed out
of the rich importer’s hand, as if he were a crimi-
nal of one sort or another. It is always under-
stood in N.S.W. that the manufacturer is to be
poor and struggling to the end of the chapter, and
that, while the man who imports things made by
cheat) foreign labor naturally lives in a mansion,
the man who makes things with well-paid Austra-
lian labor should, for the crime of trying to
establish Australian industry, be glad to live any-
where. So the public, which cheerfully takes up
shares when the big importer floats his business
into a company, refused to put up the money
required to set Sandford, Limited, going as the
great pioneer iron and steel industry of Australia.
Yet a vast mass of costly machinery and appara-
tus were required, for a manufacturing business
isn’t like an importer’s job, which can start with
a warehouse, an abject shopwalker, and three
months’ credit. So Sandford pawned his works,
his private assets, and apparently his shirt and
boots in a heroic attempt to make Litligow
another Pittsburg. Then, being still without
sufficient funds, he asked the State for assistance,
and it was offered to him. Provided the State
got a first lien on the whole £200,000 worth of
assets, it was willing to lend £70,000 at 4 per cent.,
and as it could borrow the money at 3g per cent,
it really asked perfect security and a very respect-
able profit on top of the perfect security. No
“Christian gentleman” who advertises that it is
his business in life to assist, not to crush, could
well have asked more. The impossible offer was
rejected, as a matter of course.
* * « •
The iron and steel business is one of the greatest
of Australia’s natural industries. Providence
placed here in rich abundance the ore and the
coal, and if Providence didn’t therefore intend
Australia to make iron and steel, it is impossible
any longer to regard Providence as a serious
institution. In this respect the iron and steel
business differs from most others. The pastoral
industry, for instance, is almost wholly artificial.
It is based upon a policy of feeding the foreign
and exotic sheep, and feeding it to a great extent
on foreign and exotic grasses. When it started
the Government gave the land free ; it gave con-
vict labor practically free ; in some cases it is said
to have given the stud sheep free. And to this
day, when the artificial pastoral industry strikes a
snag, it is coddled with “starving stock rates” on
the railways, and such like bonuses and gratuities ;
and these concessions are thrown as cheerfully to
the immensely rich squatter, who can afford to
pay, as to the poor one who can’t. The conces-
sion in this case isn’t regarded as a loan, repayable
with 4 per cent, interest and secured by a first
mortgage on the squattage ; it is a free gift. Also
if the squatter isn’t satisfied with the concession,
he just lets his sheep remain where they are and
die of thirst, while if the manufacturer was found
letting one horse die of thirst in his dry backyard to
save the costof removal to water, he would be hauled
up for cruelty to a dumb animal. The farming
industry is also an artificial undertaking. It con-
sists of growing purely foreign vegetables, often
with the aid of foreign implements, and
rearing the wholly foreign and unnatural
horse, cow, pig, and chookey-hen. It has
been boosted up in N.S.W. with railway rates
that are so cheap as to amount to a charity
given at the expense of the city worker; with ex-
emption from local taxation, or with huge muni-
cipal subsidies which make local taxation un-
necessary ; and with exemption from arbitration
laws and from all the trades-union regulations as
to an eight-hours day and a fair living wage. It
is allowed to employ semi-enslaved child labor
under conditions such as would never be tolerated
in a manufacturing industry. Also, it is helped
with gratis seed wheat, loans of public money at
low rates on poor security, and with, at times,
direct tariff Protection, to say nothing of other
aids and devices. And in addition to these two
big, artificial and coddled industries' there are
multitudes of others which are quite foreign and
unnatural, down to fishing for foreign fish intro-
duced contrary to the laws of Nature, and the ex-
porting of the unnatural bunny per medium of a
foreign ship, and the putting of foreign galva-
nised iron on the roofs of Australian buildings.
m * * *
Comparing the case of the N.S.W. agricultural
industry, which has been coddled, aided, bonused,
helped, and propped up for a century, and is still
applying daily for aid and getting it, with that of
the iron industry and its solitary request for assist-
ance, the discrepancy seems ridiculous. Sandford
offered splendid value for the aid he asked, as well
as fair security. To begin with, he offered to
employ some 1000 men on about half a square
mile of ground, which is about 500 times as much
pressure of employment to the acre as the average
farmer offers. These 1000 men and their wives
and families would probably have represented
3000 people directly supported by the business.
Then, counting the agriculturists, traders, and all
the pursuers of great and small industries from
A to Z (in other words, from the actor, agent,
apiarist, auctioneer, accountant, axle-maker,
accoucheuse, asphalter, artist’s model and aerated-
water person right down to the zinc worker) that
these 3000 folk would employ, and, reckoning in
the wives and families of all this multitude,
Sandford Limited offered support, direct and
indirect, to something like 10,000 people. And
these people would pay to the State some
£30,000 or £35,000 a year directly, in taxation, or
£70,000 to £75,000 a year in taxation and contri-
butions to the railway revenue—all this quite
apart from their contributions to the Federal
revenue. So, when Sandford asked for assistance
to the extent of £70,000, he really offered in
return these six compensations :
(1) Interest at 4 per cent, on the loan.
(2) Absolute priority as regards £25,000 of the loan over
all other creditors.
(3) Permission to place a Government representative
in charge of the works to see that the Government’s
interests were carefully safeguarded.
(4) About £75,000 a year of new revenue, equal in itself
to 100 per cent, per annum on the loan asked for.
(5) A thousand—it might be more correct to say 3000—
men who might one day be of the utmost value in the
defence of Australia.
(6) The establishment of an industry which is more
essential to the defence of Australia than almost any
other that can be mentioned—an industry which is so
essential that without it all the others count, in time of
need, for just about nothing at all.
« * * »
And, it must be reiterated, Sandford didn’t ask
for anything like the concessions that the agri-
culturist gets almost without asking. He didn’t
ask for permission to use cheap or gratis child
labor, or to employ men 12 hours a day for cocky
wages. He didn’t ask for exemption from local
rates, or for the amazingly cheap railway freights
that the farmer gets on most of his produce. If
he had got these things, and other things that are
freely given to the man on the land, he wouldn’t
have required State assistance. He wouldn’t
even have required Protection. But he had the
misfortune to be a manufacturer, and many years
of slavery to the importer has accustomed the
N.S.W. Legislature to regard the man who manu-
factures anything as a public enemy. The Labor
party, which never proposes to nationalise the
farming industry when it comes to the Treasury
door demanding the concessions that it has been
asking pretty regularly for a century or so, at
once had an idea that the iron trade should
be nationalised. The other parties reckoned
that when it came to a question of help-
ing to create an industry which alone can
save Australia from one day becoming a province
of Asia, a fair thing to ask was 4 per cent, in-
terest and £200,000 worth of security for a £70,000
loan. The stupendous importance of the bran
and pumpkin and hen-food business is recognised
by all parties, and hardly any public aid is too
great to keep them on their feet. The import-
ance of the industry which, in the day of invasion
and imminent conquest by a race of colored semi-
savages, can supply Australia with cannon, rifles,
and bayonets, with steel rails, and railway rolling
stock and armor plates for ships, is apparently, so
far as the fatuous State Legislature of N.S.W. is
concerned, recognised by nobody. The Labor
party hopes to see it started as a
State undertaking some day ; but any
day will do—or any century. The Free-
trade party doesn’t want to see it started at
all, because it will interfere with the blessed im-
porter. A dreadful idea that there is no need for
haste, that the enemy will wait till we are ready,
that we have before us all the ages between
to-day and the Day of Judgment in which to make
up our minds, pervades the soul of everybody
concerned. If there was displayed in this urgent
matter even half the energy that is put by one
party into a question relating to 2d. a day wages
on a Government contract, or by the other into
assuring the public that we really can’t make any-
thing for ourselves, and must import everything
as long as we live, the outlook for N.S.W. would
be vastly brighter than it is.
* * * *
Of course, it doesn’t follow as a matter of cer-
tainty that the newly-born iron industry is dead.
It may be taken over by some syndicate
—possibly a foreign syndicate—on terms which
will keep the business alive, but which will almost
certainly leave the plucky founder a ruined
man. It may totter along as a bank-owned con-
cern living in a state of semi-liquidation. It may
close up in N.S.W. and revive in some other State
where the local Legislature has more sense and
more patriotism. It may get from the Australian
Parliament the support and recognition that the
narrow-minded, incapable, and anti-Australian,
parish legislators of Sydney have refused. But,
even if the best happens, the disgrace of these
petty local law-makers is none the less. They
stand forth as men who are fully seized of the
importance of the bran and peanut jobs, but who
haven’t the slightest conception of the necessity
for saving their country from destruction. They
appear asahaltingcrowd with neither the energy to
start the iron industry as a State undertaking, nor
the courage to help it along as a private enterprise.
The N.S.W. Legislature goes on the general
theory that the manufacturer should pay the
highest wages on earth for the shortest, hours on
earth, that he should be subject to all manner of
interferences in this direction from which the
importer is practically exempt, that he should be
exposed to the free competition of all the cheap
labor communities in the world, that he should
pay higher railway rates than most other pro-
ducers, and that he should cheerfully submit to be
taxed in order to help the State to supply in-
dividuals like the enormously wealthy Squatter
M’Caughey with cheap imported bunny-proof
netting. The best hope for the N.S.W. manu-
facturer lies in the fact that some other States of
the Commonwealth have cultivated the good
Australian sentiment which his own State so
badly lacks.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“Ile.”
S. M. Herald occasionally realises that there is
another side to its Freetrade ravings. For in-
stance, it put the case for the British Imperial Oil
Go. regarding the 3d. per gallon duty on kerosene
in packages:—
Up till seven years ago the kerosene trade of Aus-
tralia was in the hands of the Standard Oil Trust.
Then the British Imperial Oil Co. (which draws
its supplies from Borneo) came on the scene as
a competitor. Not relishing the idea of having a
poacher on its preserves, the Standard Oil Trust—so the
opposing company’s representative alleges—strove with
all its power to crush its competitor. One device was
the “ conditional rebate ” —that is to say, distributors
received a penny per gallon rebate, on condition that they
only sold the Trust’s oil. The British Imperial Co. estab-
lished the system of bringing the oil to Australia in bulk,
and packing it locally. On behalf of the co. it was urged
that this meant the building of factories and tank installa-
tions at the principal ports at a cost of over £IOO,OOO, and
the employment of local labor in drum, tin, and case-
making and packing to the tune of many thousands a
year ; and in this connection a statement was made that
if all the kerosene consumed in Australia was imported in
bulk it would mean an expenditure on labor of £85,000
a year, but if all the oil were imported in tins and cases
the expenditure on labor would amount to only £SOOO a
year. After a seven-years’ struggle, which has conferred
great benefits on the kerosene users of Australia, the
British Co. is seriously considering whether it should re-
main in the Commonwealth. The tariff proposal is the
first measure of relief that has been offered. It will
force the Trust to bring its oil here in bulk, manu-
facture the tins and cages locally under Australian
conditions, and equalise this portion of the competition.
A vote against the Government’s proposal will be help
given to the Standard Oil Trust, of New Jersey, U.S.A.,
whose profits at the expense of the world’s consumers
(mostly poor people) during a few years have been sworn
at nearly £200,000,000. A vote given for the Govern-
ment’s proposal will be in the interests of Australia, and
will prevent a perpetual tax being levied on local oil
consumers by the Trust. The day after the tariff
schedule was laid on the table, the Trust, although it had
a year’s supply of kerosene stored in Australia duty
free, exacted the duty from consumers. This was done
to cause an outcry against the tariff proposal.
All this, however, was printed by the Herald
three days after the House of Representatives had
blown the kerosene duty out. Seemingly up till
then the Herald didn’t know there was any such
concern as the B. I. O. Co. in existence.
*
The Methods of the Foreign Agent.
Considering the exposure of business methods
that has been going on persistently in Australia
ever since the Tariff Commission first opened the
public eyes, nobody expected to find any chivalry
in Commerce. But the foreign agent, in his
anxiety to kill existing Australian industries and
to prevent the birth of new ones, is prepared to
resort to any methods. Biano-maker Beale, of
Sydney, reports cases in which tuners have filed the
strings of his pianos, so that after a little heavy work
they would break. Firms which had condemned
Beale’s Australian-made pianos as rubbish have
polished up some of his second-hand instruments
which they had taken in part payment, and, cut-
ting out his firm’s name, had replaced it with their
own, and sold the doctored implement as new.
Which does not look as though they re-
garded the Australian product as “rubbish.”
On October 19, there was held in Leipzig,
Germany, a meeting of the Piano Makers’
Association, at which the Council was instructed
to demand that the Imperial Government should
use its influence to get the new Australian duty
on pianos reduced “to a reasonable figure.”
Within a few days of the receipt of the news of
that meeting in Australia a number of agents for
foreign pianos held a meeting in Melbourne at
which a fighting fund was subscribed for a “lobby-
ing campaign.” It will be remembered that
within a few weeks an enormous petition, said to
be signed by some thousands of music teachers,
was presented to Parliament praying that the
duty on pianos should be reduced if not abolished,
as otherwise the musical education of the people
would suffer, and we would cease to be a songful
nation, and the export of Crossleys, Mf.LBAS, and
other operatic stars would cease. Presumably
this was all part of the lobbying campaign,
and, apparently, it was largely paid for by
German gold. If anything on earth should
rouse the patriotism of the Australian legis-
lator it is this spectacle of foreign agents
conspiring to discredit Australian goods in the
minds of the Australian people, while a gang
of German piano makers pulls the strings. And
this case was only one of a number in which the
foreign person’s lobbyist was in evidence. When
it was announced that Australia intended to
strike the importation of packed oil with a
duty of 3d. per gallon, an agent of the stan-
dard Oil Trust started for Melbourne at top
speed. While he was on the way the members of
the Foreign-trade Opposition talked glibly about
giving a “real preference” to the dear old
Motherland. After he arrived and had prac-
tically lived in the House for a while, they
still talked about the “ real preference,” but when
the duty on kerosene came up, it was biffed
out without even a division—it simply went on
the voices. And yet that duty of 3d. per gallon
represented a real preference to the dear old
Motherland. It would have knocked out the
U.S. Standard Oil Trust, which ships its oil ready
tinned and packed from its various depbts, and it
would have diverted the trade into the pockets of
English companies which own oil wells in Borneo
and Nigeria. This English oil would have come
in free, and by the time the grinding Yankee
monopoly had got its tank ships afloat, and its
tinning and packing factories erected in Australia,
the English companies would have got a firm grip
of the trade, and have been hard to dislodge. Of
course, the fact that the duty would have caused
£85,000 a year, that is now spent abroad, to be
paid to Australian workmen doesn’t matter. The
Australian workman doesn’t count. Neither
do the Australian business people who would
have annexed the bulk of the £IOO,OOO that
the Trust would have had to sink in tinning
and packing depbts in Australia —they don’t
matter either. The Tasmanian tin mines, which
would have supplied the material for the cans, the
Broken Hill companies, which would have supplied
the zinc, as well as the lead, for soldering purposes;
and the Australian saw-millers, who would have
supplied the timber for the cases—none of them
matter. Nothing matters but the importer. The
great, soulless Trust, that has been convicted of
every crime in the calendar, including murder,
will go on slowly crushing the English and Aus-
tralian oil industries. Oil will not be any cheaper
to the Australian, nor will it be any better—a,nd
the only people who will benefit by the abolition
of the duty will be the bald-headed John D.
Rockefeller and those associated with him.
The Trust agent was lucky in having found
a Legislature composed, like the rest of the
world, mostly of fools. But he may take
away this reflection with him, that Australia
is committed to Protection ; and if we do not
get it this year we will try again next, and the
next after that if necessary. There will be no
fiscal peace in this continent till it gets a High
Protective tariff, and the Standard Oil and all the
other Trusts are barricaded out.
Pianos, Johnson, Etc.
The debate on the piano duties in the House of
Representatives was an affair which ought to
make Australia blush with shame. It consisted
in the main of a savage attack by sundry Foreign-
trade members —principally the dreadful Johnson,
of N.S.W.—upon the Australian piano industry.
It was not alleged that the Australian pianos are
worse than the imported ones, or that they are
dearer, or that they would be worse or dearer
than the imported ones under increased Protec-
tion. But they represent an Australian in-
dustry, in which a large amount of Australian
capital is sunk, which pays good wages to a large
number of Australian workmen for eight hours’
work per diem, and which yields profits to an
Australian proprietary—profits that are spent in
Australia. This was enough to excite the fury of
Johnson and his crowd, and they waved the
blanky shirt at large. Johnson declared with
frenzy that if the figures of Beale’s balance-sheet
were “properly read”—apparently Johnson
wasn’t going by the figures in the balance-sheet,
but by those which he wanted to find there—the
year’s profits of the Beale piano-making co.
would prove to be £BIOS. He stopped to let the
horror of it soak in—the horror and iniquity of a
mere wretched Australian manufacturing concern
making £BIOS. Just a few months ago three
importing concerns, which sell their goods
in Australia but divide their profits in Lon-
don, announced that they had made over
£35,000, £55,000, and £03,000 respectively for the
year, but because they employ no skilled Aus-
tralian labor, and spend their earnings in Eng-
land, this didn’t make Johnson turn a hair.
But the shame and disgrace of an Australian
manufacturing concern making £Blos—it simply
maddened Johnson. Then there were ferocious
scoffs at “ this gr-r-r-r-reat Australian industry,”
with along drawl on the “great,” and a bitter
sneer on the “Australian.” It was alleged by
Johnson that nine-tenths of the materials used in
the manufacture of Australian pianos are im-
ported. This statement Mr. Beale briefly de-
scribed as “an infernal lie, and beneath con-
tempt,” and anyone who has inspected the Beale
factory, and considered all the processes conducted
there, and the mass of ingenious machinery therein
—much of it Australian-made, and some of it
Australian-invented machinery and seen the
making of the parts in active operation, would
consider the things Beale said about John-
son were quite moderate. But the climax
came when Johnson and Knox combined
to solemnly present to the House the
offer of a certain German individual who, it was
alleged, is willing to give £50,000 for Beale’s
business (in which £150,000 capital is said to be
involved), and to manufacture pianos without any
increase'on the present duty, “provided he is allowed
to import practically all the parts free of duty, and
provided his manufacturing process is to simply
consist in putting together these imported parts,
and consequently (though this was not specifically
mentioned) provided he is allowed to sack all the
Australian workmen now' employed in making
the Iparts. This outrageous offer was flourished
by Johnson and Knox as if it were a solid and
irrefutable argument against the Australian
piano-making industry, and again they paused to
let the serious nature of the situation soak in.
And, in addition to these matters, there
was a curious side-issue. An importer, pre-
sumably full of anxiety lest the making of
Australian pianos by well-paid Australian
workmen should get in the way of his importing


business, bad presented to Treasurer Lyne certain
written arguments against an increased duty.
These written arguments, wh'V-h apparently were
not marked “ private,” had, by some accident or
other, reached Bevle, the .lustralian mixnu-
facturer. and had been refuted by him. The
Freetraders, whose possession of Beale’s balance-
sheet was still unaccounted for, asked with
terrible intensity by what unspeakable breach of
faith the Australian maker had been allowed to
read and reply to the importer’s story, instead of
the against him by default, and seemed
to imply that Judas Iscakiot was a mere circum-
stance by comparison. It was a lamentable,
miserable, long-drawn-out business. Most of it
was Johnson ; and the savage anger of Johnson
because anybody presumed to make things in Aus-
tralia, and the rage of Johnson because an
Australian manufacturing concern had the
effrontery to make a profit of £BIOS, while
importers make £63,000 or more without aggra-
vating Johnson in the least, was a melancholy
spectacle. The Bulletin sometimes wonders if
there aren’t countries where a patriotic mob
would be a law unto itself, and chase Johnson
into the next continent.
The Hatred of Australian Industry.
The Tariff debate has been notable, amongst
other things, for the venomous manner in which
almost the whole of the Anti-Sosh members have
slandered Australian manufactures and Australian
workmen. No lie concerning the quality of Aus-
tralian goods has been too gross for these traitors
to invent or repeat; and a visitor, reading their
malignant utterances, might reasonably come to
the conclusion that these men were representing
some foreign country with which Australia was at
war. During the debate on the white-lead duty,
a few days ago, Palmer, the member from Echuca,
it is reported-
read a letter stating that pure white-lead was not
manufactured in Australia. It was a sulphate of lead,
and not equal to lead produced from carbonate of lead.
It is quite possible that the duty would have been
seriously interfered with were it not for the fact
that John Quick, chairman of the Tariff Com-
mission, Was in the Chamber. The effective
manner in which he demolished Palmer’s traitor-
ous lie is worth printing in capitals :
Mr. John Quick remarked that Mr. Palmer’s statement
was a slander on Australian white-lead. Subsequently he
read a metallurgist’s certificate as to the purity of Aus-
tralian white-lead, manufactured from sulphate of lead.
He also read a further certificate from the keeper of
stores at Newport railway depdt testifying to the quality
and covering capacity of the Australian article, showing
that it was superior to the ordinary white-lead.
Yet Palmer hadn’t even the grace to withdraw
his slander. Instead, he followed Johnson,
Edwards, J. T. Brown, Wilks and 12 others
of the slander gang, who voted for an amendment
by DugalD Thomson reducing the duty. The
manner in which the Protectionist members of the
House of Representatives have to be on guard
against the circulation of lies concerning Aus-
tralian products is humiliating to the last degree.
If there is any other country in the world where
men glory in openly traducing their own products
this paper would like to hear of it. At present
Australia seems to occupy a position of lonely and
unenviable eminence in this respect.

Drying-up—A Matter Relating to the
Australian Capital.
“The Queanbeyan River has ceased to flow,”
says a brief telegram from the southern part of
N.S.W. When that statement appeared the
drought had only lasted a few months—not so
many months as it may last years when once it
gets fairly started. Yet already the unreliable
little affluents of the Murrumbidgee are giving
out. And it is among these unreliable little
affluents that the N.S.W. State Frights party—
the party which raves against the proposed Aus-
tralian capital at Dalgety simply because the
Dalgety site is approved by the Australian Par-
liament wants to plant Australia’s political
centre. At Canberra and other places thereabouts
it is possible that enough stagnant water could be
conserved at heavy expense, to see the Federal
city through a bad dry spell, lint it is
all a matter of doubt. The affluents of
the Murrumbidgee are a rather shifty lot at
best. Meanwhile the Snowy River, on which
Dalgety stands, fed by the perpetual snows of the
great Kosciusko range, rushes down to the sea as
usual. It goes on for ever. It turns southwards
out of N.S.W. into Victoria, and there, amid
rocks and gullies and mountains and precipices,
where there is nothing to irrigate, it wastes the
good Cornstalk water that N.S.W. wants so badly.
As it roars along it carries into the ungrateful
adjacent State enough Cornstalk drink to quench
the thirst of a city larger than Sydney, and to
supply it with electric light, and electric heating
arrangements, and power sufficient to run all its
industries, and something over wherewith to
irrigate the Federal territory. All this water
and power and heat and light are Corn-
stalk property which goes to waste to make
A patch of Gippsland scenery. But against
this remains the fact that Dalgety was selected
by the Australian Parliament, and therefore, to
assert their miserable little scrap of alleged
“authority” and to show their power, the petty-
minded State Frights push of Sydney demands
that the capital shall be shifted to a site on some
of the unreliable little creeks that run into the
’Bidgee, and that N.S.W.’s Snowy River shall
still waste its splendid volume of water amid the
Gippsland scenery. And at the request of State
Premier Wade the foolishly affable Deakin pro-
poses to give the Australian Parliament an oppor-
tunity “to discuss the question of the capital
site,’’ just as if the question hadn’t already been
exhaustively discussed and settled. There is some-
thing maddening about the affability of Alfred
Deakin.
Compulsory Military Training.
The Australian Government has announced that
the key-note of its system of land defence will he
the compulsory training to the use of arms of all
able-bodied young male Australians. The idea is
one which should receive the enthusiastic support
of every patriotic citizen. In the case of a country
like Belgium, with 030 people crowded upon each
square mile, the training of a small percentage of
the young men would furnish a fairly large army
in proportion to the area to be guarded ; but Aus
tralia, with about three persons to two square
miles, with immensely powerful enemies at its
gates, and with half the globe between it and
any reasonable prospect of assistance, needs
to hustle for its very life. Even if it
does hustle as proposed, it will probably
be 20 years before there are in Australia 1,000,000
men who have passed through the training course,
and as the proposed course only consists of 18 days’
training per annum, and even that only applies
to young men in their 19th, 20th, and 21st years,
the oldest of the allegedly qualified men will only
be qualified in the sense that they had learned
something about the business 19 years ago. It
isn’t a drastic proposition by any means—in
fact, there is reason to doubt whether it is
drastic enough. But the principle is excellent;
in fact, it is the only possible principle. A
voluntary citizen defence force of any size and
efficiency is out of the question. If a young man
in a bank or insurance office joined such a
force in his enthusiasm, it would presently
be noted in the office that Private Smith was
apt to be called away at most inconvenient
times for his annual training, and the institution
would look coldly upon him as a person whose
military fervor got in the way of business. When
some responsible post fell vacant, Jones, who had
no military fervors, would get the promotion,
while Private Smith would stay in the rut. His
three years’ voluntary service, if it didn’t actually
get him the sack, would often mean three years’
loss of promotion, and he might never in all his
life catch up these arrears. But if every young
man in the institution’s employ was under exactly
the same conditions, and each was called out in
turn for his three weeks’ training, there
would be no handicap. Thus every young
man would get three weeks’ holiday per
annum, and spend it in a way that
would make him in after life a healthier, more
muscular, and more perpendicidar citizen. And
it wouldn’t make him one whit more liable to be
butchered on the gory battlefield than he is now —•
in fact, it would make him much less so. Every
Government has the inherent right to call out the
whole able-bodied male population for defence
purposes in time of dire necessity, and when Aus-
tralia is invaded by the hordes of Asia, they must
be called out. (This fact is directly acknow-
ledged in the Defence Act which provides
for the calling to the colors of all citizens in
war time.) As things are now, these levies
would go out as an almost unarmed and untrained
mob, and thousands of them would be needlessly
slain while they were learning, in the face of the
enemy, the rudiments of the military art. Under
the system of compulsory training they would go
out as men who knew enough to give them
a chance for their lives as well as a chance of
victory. There is no question of aggressive mili-
tarism in the case ; it is merely a matter of giving
the Australian citizen a show when it comes to
pass that he must go out, trained or untrained. A
voluntary regular army wouldn’t be a drop in the
bucket of such an emergency, and need hardly be
considered as a possible alternative. Such an
army always consists too largely of the wasters
and incompetents of the community—the mer-
cenaries who are willing to go Tommying for poor
pay because even that pay is beyond their earning
capacity in other walks of life. But though
the pay be poor and the hired wasters and incom-
petents cheap, Australia can’t afford defence on
those lines. It takes a large army to mate even
a pretence of defending nearly 3,000,000 square
miles of country, and 4,000,000 people can’t buy
one-tenth of the permanent and stipendiary
Tommy that would be required for the work.
The Advantage of Going Often to the
Pawnshop.
E. W. O’Sullivan writes
In The Bulletin (5/12/’O7) you have, by a tortuous
process of logic, proved to your satisfaction that the 29
new tramlines which I gave to N. S. Wales are only
paying about 2 per cent. Permit me in reply to say that
you are either looking at the subject with the wrong end of
the telescope—contraction instead of expansion—or else
you have access to figures not open to the Railway Com-
missioners or the Treasurer of N. S. Wales—the acceptable
authorities in such matters. At the turning of the sod of
the Erskineville tramway, the Railway Commissioners
(who ought to know) supplied the Hon. Ciias. A. Lee,
Minister for Public Works, with the statement that,
while in 1899 (the year I took office) the tramways were
only paying 2£ per cent., they are now paying nearly
6 per cent. As we only had 50 miles of tramlines when
I took office in 1599, and I added 133 miles at a cost of
about two millions, borrowed at and 3f per cent., I
think I have a right to claim that the increase up to
nearly 6 per cent, is the outcome of my work. To cor-
roborate this assertion of the Railway Commissioners, we
have the statements of the Treasurer in his recent
budget speech, that the railways and tramways of N. S.
Wales combined were paying about 5 per cent, it devolves
upon you now to show that your figures are more reliable
than those of the Treasurer and the Railway Commis-
sioners. When you do so, the public will begin to see who
is the writer who is humbugging himself on the
question. Take the case of the Manly tram,
which you have quoted; that is only 1J mile out
of a scheme of 16 miles, which I had planned to go to
Pittwater on one side and the Spit on the other. Even
now the Manly tram of 1J mile is so crowded that they
have had to put on steam instead of horses. In 1902-3
you say wo had a deficit on my tramlines. Perhaps, but
is it not somewhat unfair to judge a tram or railway
scheme after one or two years’operation ? Judge them
now, after six years’ operation, and you will find that the
Railway Commissioners’ report of a 6 per cent, profit on
the whole scheme of tramlines is absolutely correct.
In addition to all this, you have to consider the enormous
impetus given to building operations by my tram-
lines. You can see that in almost every suburb
around Sydney where my trams have gone. Accord-
ing to the S. M. Herald, in the year 1903, when I
was still in power, no less than three million pounds’
worth of new buildings were erected in that year alone in
Sydney and suburbs. The increase of buildings has gone
on nearly at the same rate ever since, and therefore I
think I can honestly claim that the greater portion of
this eight or nine million pounds was the outcome of my
scheme of tramway extension. Putting all other aspects
of the question aside for the moment, what an immense
amount of well-paid labor this gave rise to !
All of which is only so much further proof that
on this question the Owe’Sullivan is the incor-
rigible party that The Bulletin said he was.
Briefly—
(l) The Bulletin didn’t say the 29 tramways which the
Owe’S. “ gave ” N. S. Wales were only paying about 2
per cent. What it did say, on the authority of Coghlan,
was that in 1902-3 (which was one of the Owe’Sullivan
years) “all that the public undertakings had left over
after paying working expenses was £1,509,752”; that
“ N. S. Wales then owed Coiien £71,592,485”; and that
“ the actual return was, therefore, £2 2s. 2d. per cent.”
The Owe’Sullivan had talked about 6 per cent. ; and
£2 2s. 2d. isn’t 6 per cent., except when you look through
a magnifying glass, and Cohen won’t look through a
magnifying glass when N. S. Wales is settling up with him.
(2) If the Railway Commissioners told Mr. Lee, who
told Mr. Owe’S., that in 1899 the tramways of N.S.W.
were “ only paying 2£ per cent.,”and that they are now
paying 6 per cent.,” most of the truth got lost in transit.
Mr. Owe’S. can get much more direct information than
that. The official N.S.W. Statistical Register (1899)
shows that for that year the N.S.W. State tramways paid
3-99 per cent. ; and the Railway Commissioners’ report
for 1906-7 shows that they paid 4'93 per cent. The
Owe’S., relying upon his third-hand information, says the
improvement between 1899 and the present is 3J percent.
On the official reports, it really is less than 1 per cent.
(3) The Owe’S. thinks the increase of 3J per cent,
(which is only 0'94 per cent, and not 3£ per cent, at all)
is “ the outcome of my work.” In S. Australia the rail-
ways improved in the same time from £3 Bs. sd. per cent,
to £5 3s. 2d. per cent., and in Queensland from
£1 12s. lid. to £3 14s. Bd. in four years ; and the Owe’S.
wasn’t even so much as a plain citizen in either of those
States. Apparently, then, Mr. Owe’Sullivan isn’t the
only Providence in Australia. Further, the Melbourne
tramways in 1599 made a profit of £56,629 and in 1907
they made £137,661, so it looks as though there would
have been a tremendous increase of tramway profits in
N. S. Wales if the Owe’Sullivan hadn’t arrived to spoil
the results by building various lines that don’t even pay
working expenses—Manly, Baulkham Hills, and others.
(4) The Owe’S. glories in the fact that the spending of
his borrowed millions created a boom : “ What an
immense amount of well-paid labor this gave rise to ! ”
In other words, because the Owe’Sullivan borrowed,
others borrow'ed, and spent the money —and the bill for
it all will come in by-and-bye. The Owe’Sullivan always
forgets that blessed bill. The Bulletin doesn’t. That is
the difference between them.

Dungog and the Almighty.
“ Bill ” upon prayers for rain ;
The people who pray for rain have my most sincere
encouragement. Rain is wanted just now so much in
Australia that any means of inducing it to come here seems
fair, and if prayers can induce it to fall, that method has
at least the advantage of inexpensiveness. Only, granting
the efficiency of ram-prayers, these people do not go to
work in a business-like way. The rules of the game,
as indicated by those in the know, are based
on the assumption that the Almighty counts
votes. One prayer, like one vote cast for the
candidate lowest on the list, doesn’t seem to count for
much ; but when a majority of the electors put in their
petition the matter seems to call for investigation. Also
even a majority of prayer-votes cast at various intervals
are of no value. The thing, to have any appreciable
effect, must be done simultaneously. Apparently there
is only one polling day, and all votes east before
or after that date are invalid. So it seems as
if the Almighty, who must have known that the
drought was due, can’t be got to attend to it till
He is in receipt of a simultaneous boost from earth. And,
also, according to the accepted rules of the game, if
people go to picnics on prayer day instead of staying at
home to record their votes at the churches, the Almighty
disallows the petition even of a majority. People could
as easily pray at a picnic as in a church, and if the pic-
nickers forgot to bring along the water in the billy, they
would pray all the more fervently. The regulations pro-
vide that every vote must be cast in the polling-booth.
There are, however, regrettable difficulties in the way
of altering the weather. In the first place, if the
Almighty is a just Almighty, He will not grant every
petition that is sent up for consideration. In the
Transvaal war the Boers prayed much more fervently for
victory than the British, and the Boers lost. And if
prayers were always granted there would be no death ;
for death is a calamity that is prayed against with a
fervor beyond all comparison with that of a request for
rain. Arid in the case of a monarch, he has the advant-
age of being prayed for by an enormous electorate. If
weekly prayers by an enormous number could keep
Edward good, Edward ought to be very good by this
time. And the Almighty, considering the petition of
Dungog (N.S.W.) and other places on a small section
of an inconspicuous planet, must weigh the relative
advantages of the gain of a rainfall there with the conse-
quent loss of rainfall elsewhere. The Almighty, taking
into consideration the size of the universe, and the con-
sequent innumerable wants of the assorted species of life
scatttered throughout it, must be constantly besieged
with prayers to alter things. However, let us suppose
that the Almighty has a kindly feeling toward the earth,
even a special partiality to Australia. Then He must
work a miracle. It may seem to Dungog a trivial
thing to make a copious rainfall on Dungog and
even on the rest of Australia. But the meteorologists
know better. Rain is conditioned by certain anterior
causes. No rain falls without a physical reason. So the
Almighty, setting to work to remedy what Dungog re-
gards as a defect in His management, must upset His
whole weather scheme. If rain falls in Australia it will
affect the whole of the meteorological conditions of the
world. So the miracle that Dungog asks for is so
stupendous that turning water into wine sounds like a
mere conjurer’s trick by comparison. For the present dry
spell is casually connected with all the weather that has
happened since the creation of the earth. If there were no
miracles, and yet the rainfall that wasn’t coming to-
morrow were made to come, it would have had to be
led up to by utterly different weather conditions than
those actually existing 1 . And this different arrangement
of high and lot pressures, anti-cyclones and cyclones,
would have been caused by preceding different weather
conditions ; and these, in their turn, would have involved
different conditions to those that actually were there,
right back to the time of the Flood. And if the rain, by
prayer, is brought on a month earlier than the date
which the Almighty fixed for it to be due, then
the Flood would have happened a month earlier
than it was expected, and it would have caught
Noah with his Ark half-built, and that old
salt wouldn’t have had time to make the vessel seaworthy
—to say nothing of getting his cargo of live stock on
board—and the ark would have been swamped, and there
would have been no Dungog, and no Dungoggers. So
the people who pray for rain began too late; they
should have started their petition before the present
arrangement of cyclones and anti-cyclones was get going.
Believing in the efficacy of prayer, Dungog might
have prevented the drought long ago. If the Dungoggers
made a practice of praying for a nice rainfall
once a fortnight there would have been no drought.
However, willing to give the rain-prayers a chance,
this writer suggests two comprehensive and permanent
schemes. First, he is going to pray for a better dis-
tribution of rainfall over the earth, so that those places,
like the West Coast of Maoriland, which get about three-
quarters of an inch every twenty-four hours, might spare
some of their surplus for Australia, and the enormous
amount of rain that falls stupidly back into the
ocean might possibly he diverted ' to the interior
of Sahara and Australia. Failing this readjustment,
he will fall back upon The Bulletin's old scheme of
praying for a whopping mountain range right in the
middle of Australia. Or, if one range of Himalayas is not
sufficient, let’s pray for a do/, n. If miracles can be
worked by prayer it would he just as easy for the
Almighty to boost up a few healthy and quick-growing
volcanoes as to bring down a deluge of rain. And,
having our Himalayas, we need not worry the Almighty
any further about the weather. Where there are
Himalayas, the rain can always be relied upon.

A Fat Man’s Holiday.
The members of Victoria’s House of Fat had a
rollicking time of it the other evening, when the
Amending Factories Act came before them. As
a commencement, Labor member Evans tried to
obtain a Wages Board for clerks. But the House
fell on his idea like an avalanche, only two votes
—those of Evans and McLkllan— being cast in
its favor. Then, by 20 votes to 10, the House so
mutilated the clause providing for a Carters’
Board that it might as well have cut the lot out.
The Quarrying Board clause was amended, so as
not to apply to “agricultural purposes.” Warm-
ing to the work, Manifold, the stodgy and stupid
member for Warrnambool district, moved “the de-
letion from the clause of all mention of the occupa-
tion of gardeners or nurserymen.” Bayne rose to
support the amendment, and made a remark that
should become historical :
A garden is a luxury, and people will not tolerate
factory inspectors entering their gardens, and interfering
with their servants.
Paynf, must have been very impressive, 'as the
amendment was agreed to on the voices. But if
Payne lives a few years longer, he will find that
he will have to tolerate a good few things that at
present he declines even to consider. The House
of Rich Landlords had now got its second wind,
and the egregious Manifold rose heavily to his
feet and moved an amendment limiting the opera-
tion of the whole clause to the metropolitan
district. Said Manifold
The country could do very well without such experi-
ments. The conditions of work in the city were quite
different to those obtaining in the country, where the
relationship between employer and employee was that of
friendship.
And the amendment was carried by 18 votes to
nine. And in Manifold’s own district, where “the
relationship between employer and employee is
that of friendship,” the unfortunate farm hands
work 14 hours a day, Sunday mostly included, for
15s. a week. And Manifold, in his motor,
working no hours a day, approves cordially of the
iniquity.
The Fruit Fly—and Some Mosquitoes.
When the N.S.W. and Queensland delegates to
the recent fruit-growers’ conference in Adelaide
went through Melbourne, on their way home,
they passed the time of day to Bent’s Minister of
Agriculture, Swinburne, and some of Ma’s dele-
gates desired him to abolish his regulations for the
suppression of fruit-fly, while one of them assured
him that if he didn’t, Ma would break out and do
something nasty. Ma has now broken out. The
Victorian regulations applied impartially all
round —to Victoria as well as all other States that
needed them. The Queensland delegates, anxious
to suppress pests, made no moan; nor has any
other State, save N.S.W., kicked up a shindy.
But last week N.S.W. Minister Perry gazetted
regulations prohibiting the admission into N.S.W.
of Victorian fruit and vegetables, except under
special and drastic conditions, one of which is
that the produce shall be inspected and passed by
a N.S.W. officer to be stationed in Melbourne and
paid by Victoria. That the intention is to be
nasty to the “Victorian wolves” can be seen
from three facts :
(1) The regulations have not been applied to the Ma
State, though the fruit-fly (which the Victorian regula-
tions were designed to exclude) exists there, and does not
exist to any visible extent in the Cabbage Garden.
(2) Neither fruit-fly nor codlin moth, which both per-
vade the cantankerous State, attack asparagus, cabbages,
cauliflowers, peas, beans, and several other kinds of
vegetables, yet all these are put on the prohibited list.
(3) The regulations apply to Victoria only, though
fruit-fly and codlin moth, besides other diseases, exist in
Queensland and South Australia, and the moth at least
in Tasmania.
Minister Swinburne has already stated for Vic-
toria that there is no objection to the regula-
tions, as they are in the true interests of the
grower, provided they are fairly and honestly
carriedout; but he does protest against thediscrim-
ination that orders his State to keep itself clean,
while other States, including the chief offender
—theMaState itself—areallowed to continuedirty.
If Perry and Wade imagine they are doing a
good turn to Ma’s fruit-growers they are making
a large-sized error, too. The only possible way to
assist the County oft Cumberland fruit-growers,
and to assure the acceptance of their product in
outside markets, is to compel them to keep
their orchards clean. Perry’s action merely
encourages them to be dirty. Certainly it does
something else—it brings under the notice of a
few more people the necessity of extending the
powers of the Australian Parliament; for a
system under which such a matter as fruit pests
is left to half-a-dozen petty State authorities is
only a shade less absurd than it would be if every
householder in a city were allowed to be his own
water and sewerage board and sanitary inspector.

The People of the Slums.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Labor in municipal government had in England
removed the slums from many cities, but owing to land
monopoly the slums had been supplanted by great human
rabbit warrens many storeys high. In many of the
villages in England the material conditions were such
that the people had no moral conceptions whatever.
People were not improved by preaching morality at
them, but by giving improved moral conditions. —Frank
Anstey, M.L.A., recently returned from an inspection of
Great Britain.
In the grip of the mighty landlord, yielding th 9
landlord’s fee,
Like animals tamed and humble, behold this
people “free” —
Their verminous rags a token, their noisome lairs
a sign
Of the glory that men call England’s—O proud
and noble line
Whose hovels in Hoxton fester, whose lives all
bankrupt are;
And these are the British “ freemen ” all chained
to the landlord’s car !
As a sign of their vaunted freedom do we spank
the Empire’s drums
In praise of the pauper army—the People of the
Slums !
How long shall the parsons maunder—how long
in high conclave
Shall bishops with rubrics palter when bodies
they need to save?
They are blind to the nameless squalor, to filth
and vice and slime
That are part of the Empire’s glory. The Church
is marking time !
It mumbles its ancient story, it fishes in vain for
souls
When the bodies of men are rotting in fetid dens
and holes !
In its palsied, crumbling pulpit it champs its
toothless gums ;
How long ere the Church shall heed them—the
People in the Slums ?
Grown fat with the sloth of ages—inert, dead-
souled and tame,
The Church is the landlord’s toady and it eats
the bread of shame !
For the squire is the parson’s fetich —the lord of
the country-side.
He is the brewer magnate to whom the pub of
the Church is tied.
So the old traditions moulder, and Empires pass
away
When their creeds wax old and rotten, and their
gods are gods of clay.
The grey-haired Empires totter —and when the
death-grip comes,
They seek in vain for soldiers in the city’s reeking
slums!
Consider the Pauper Army—it marches sadly on—
And the strength that once was England’s is with
it dead and gone !
In the streets of the bloated cities, whereof she
boasts with pride,
They have slaughtered yeoman England, and
Cobden’s hands are dyed !
There the landlord harpies batten ; while the
Church with careless eye
Looks on content and silent as the pauper hosts
roll by!
And the Empire slowly crumbles; and to-day,
with muffled drums,
Are the nation-wreckers marching—The People
of the Slums !
