just heavenly. This Mount Molloy is a mining- township,
and whenever a Cobar miner comes here, for a while he
can talk of nothing- but the beautiful climate. After a
while he notices also the beautiful sunsets. If Mrs.
Molynkux Parkes could see us women running round at
election time she would know we did not lie down all day
and have a nigger to fan us.
I attended a breaking-up at the Richmond Hill
(Charters Towers) school two years ago this Christ-
mas, and talk about vitality! I would like to ask
those silly Clarke and Parkes women to let
us work out our own destiny. We northern women
and men are quite capable of doing it. Mrs. Clarke’s
ladies could find plenty to do in their own city slums,
where it is pitiful to see the cigarette-poisoned boys, and
the puny, sweated girls. Mrs. Clarke’s ladies needn’t
worry about us while they have those others at their very
doors.
lam not speaking of the coast line. That is hot, but
not worse than parts of N.S.W. Even on the coast the
men in the canefields, for whom those ladies feel such
pity, work all day, and if there is a dance on at night
they never miss it. I wonder could Mrs. Clarke’s ladies
dance all night (to the music of a concertina with three
of its keys sick)? We do that here. Come, this is a
challenge.
When we get decent land laws and better conditions
for the workers, this Queensland will be the pick of the
bunch. We have streams flowing all the year round, and
millions of acres of good land. But the monopolist is
letting the pick of it to Chows and other aliens ; and—
thanks to the crowd those Clarke and Parkes ladies
belong to—the worker can only look on and wonder when
the end of the folly will come.
Concerning Stupidity.
The Argus recently printed a letter, in favor of
land monopoly, which began with this gem :
Most Victorians are bewailing this year’s drought. I
feel sure, however, it will do good in two ways. (1) It
will show members the stupidity of the “ million-acre
scheme ”; (2) it will at last open the eyes of the Govern-
ment to the dangers of the Closer Settlement Act.
Yes, the million-acre scheme is stupid. At
present, while practically all crops north of the
Dividing Range in Victoria are more or less a
failure, every crop on the Western plains is a
success. The Aegus itself, in summing up the
wheat prospects of the State, declared that the
one bright spot was the Western plains.
These plains, only a minute fraction of which
have as yet seen a plough, will this year
garner no less than 1,500,000 bushels of
wheat, besides hay, oats, and barley. There is a
crop failure around Melbourne, and even on the
famous Werribee plains; but these Western
plains have never known what a drought is. The
Government has a closer settlement estate—
Strathkeller —on their western fringe ; and this
year the settlers are refusing fancy offers for their
growing crops, and stand to make small fortunes.
The Closer Settlement Board recently offered a
dozen farms on the southern edge of this plain,
near Camperdown, and 86 farmers battled to get
them. Therefore The Bulletin agrees over again
that the million-acre scheme is stupid—the whole
lot of these Western plains ought to be forced out
of the hands of the monopolists, and not merely a
million acres. With that done, the unfortunate
cockies who are struggling out in the Mallee, and
who this year will not in many cases put a har-
vester over their land, might come in, and make
something likean assured living.
The release of Reuben Ward, lately sentenced
to death at Dubbo (N.S.W.), on a charge of rape,
said to have been committed on a most remarkable
girl named Cecilia Smith, again calls attention
to the urgent necessity for doing something to
the very remarkable kind of girl. Reuben
Ward’s astonishing girl was cook and waitress at
the pub of Michael Grady, and in between
cooking and waiting she had attacks of hysteria.
Also, she seems to have had a habit of
going out in the evening on very slight invitation.
She went out voluntarily with Reuben Ward,
who seems to have been a raffish young railway
official. She went with him voluntarily to the
lone, dark, deserted railway carriage shed. She
climbed voluntarily with him into a dark, deserted
railway carriage in the shed. And having done
these things voluntarily, she alleged that some-
thing which is alleged to have happened after-
wards was an outrage put upon her by compul-
sion. She had, it appears, gone to all this
trouble in the dark, and taken up this compro-
mising attitude, merely for the sake of the scenery
or something. The apparent reason for this
glaring departure from all human probability was
that girl Smith took one of her hysterical attacks,
and, being delivered to Michael Grady in an
insensible condition in the small hours of the
morning by the alarmed Reuben Ward and a
scared friend, it became up to her to explain to
Mike Grady the cause of her lateness and her
condition. And she explained it in the way which,
by reason of long-continued immunity, has become
the regular habit of the truly remarkable girl.
She took the lie of least resistance. A jury, which
seems to have been the last possibility in the way
of mental incapacity, believed the really amazing
girl, and Reuben Ward was sentenced to death.
Of course, nothing came of the sentence; but the
incredible girl goes scot-free, as usual. If
she is sane she isn’t punished; if she is
insane she isn’t locked up. And next time,
by happening to be a little less incredible,
the astonishing girl may have better luck in the
way of getting someone hanged. If she doesn’t,
some other surprising girl, taking a lesson from
her failure, may have better luck. The country
is full of remarkable girls, and their constant
impunity gives them every encouragement to
keep on being remarkable.

H. E. Pratten, of the Taylor Bros, jam firm, has
been wandering through many countries in Asia,
looking for information and business; and he got
plenty of information, anyhow. Like nearly
everybody who forms his opinions by inspecting
the mild, meek, docile, industrious desirable, etc.,
Asiatic in his own lair, Pratten comes home
“with a deepened belief in the wisdom of our
Australian immigration laws.” More than that,
not only do th'e bulk of Europeans in Asia send
across to Australia a cheer of approval, but “I
found this feeling particularly marked among the
British officers I met in India,” he says. He
went north too, and studied Kiroyiro Monkey in
his house; and —
A curious thing happened while I was in Yokohama.
Two ships containing Chinese coolies arrived at Kobe,
but the Government refused to allow them to land.
Then, having shoved the superior Chinese off
with a boat-hook —the Chinese are so much
superior that even Jap firms employ them to
handle the cash—the jumped-up Jap threw out
his beautiful little chest, and threatened to punch
America’s head for adopting precisely the Jap’s
own policy; and he hinted that, after he had
flattened out America, he would deal with Aus-
tralia by way of a second course.
+.
Keir Hardie, from England, has expounded one
section of his gospel ; he wants us, by reason of
the Brotherhood of Man, or some such theoretical
mullock, to open our doors to all comers, “ irre-
spective of creed or color.” England isn’t
seriously threatened as yet with an overwhelming
Chow invasion which will snow the white race
under ; and Hardie, being truly English, doesn’t
see why Australia, however much it may be
threatened, should shut out the Chinaman when
England doesn’t. In view of that inability,
Hardie had better take his gospel back again
whence lie came. He has utterly misread the
state of affairs. Australia knows its Asiatic better
than he does, despite his recent hurried silly skip
through India, and, if it was forced to choose, it
would sooner sacrifice its free institutions, its
adult suffrage and its Parliamentary system, and
go back to despotism and feudalism, than give up
the White Australia idea. The loss of all these
things would be reparable misfortunes; history
has shown that a white race can generally find its
way back to political liberty, no matter what
temporary may assail it. But the
nation that'lias “gone nigger” and turned Eura-
sian, or mulatto, or Chow half-breed, can never
make its way back. It is damned for all time.
It loses not only its dignity as a white people—
which is something here, however little it may be
worth in the slums of London—but its capacity
for holding on to political liberty. No Asiatic
race has shown the semblance of a capacity for
popular government. The Asiatic has always been
the slave of some despot or another. Therefore
the Tory, who in his heart loves the despotic form
of government, would fain see Australia swamped
by cheap labor serfs, and in this matter the awful
Keir Hardie is his prophet—and a very silly
prophet at that.
; ♦-
I’REMiER-by-accident Philp, of Queensland, goes
to the country on a platform whereof the chief
plank and platitude seems to be “reform of the
Upper House.” Philp, as usual, proposes to
reform backwards. The Queensland Upper
House consists of life nominees, the same as in
N.S. W. Its inhabitants are in the main useless,
stupid, and Tory, but they can be rendered harm-
less. When the Queensland Assembly is ready to
abolish the House of Dodder the Ministry of the
day has merely to nominate 50 or 100 good Demo-
crats to the Council, so that they may vote their
own abolition and that of their mates. It is all
comparatively easy. But Philp aims at an elect-
ive Upper House, based on a property franchise
—a body like the immovable heap of mullock that
sits on the chest of Victoria or Tasmania—the
sort of thing that can’t be moved by any of the
ordinary processes of Government. Philp would
just love, when he goes out of office, to leave that
huge snag behind him. It might take the demo-
cracy 30 or 100 years or more to shift the “re-
formed” Upper House, while the unreformed one
could be obliterated in three months if the
Assembly really felt that way. That sort of
“reform” appeals to Robert of Townsville.

The N.S.W. State Government made an honorable
if somewhat insufficient attempt the other day to
remedy a clamorous evil. It introduced a Bill
providing that the kiddy-employee under 16, who
is now too often a gratis slave, diddled out of his
or her services on the pretence of “learning the
business,” should receive a minimum wage of 3s.
per week of 48 hours, with 6d. tea money and 3d.
per hour overtime when required to work after
b P-hl And the Assembly did a still better thing
by raising the 3s. to ss. Whereupon Minister
Wood did a shockingly bad thing by postponing
further consideration of the Bill.' Wood is a bad-
tempered man with a huge idea of his own
dignity, but he might have taken the extra 2s. as
something in the nature of a compliment—a sort
of encore, in fact.
♦
The discussion of the question of Ministerial
salaries in N.S.W. Assembly elicited very little
that was valuable in apolitical sense. The nearest
point to a constitutional view was that taken by
members who said that if Parliament left a lump
sum to a Government, Ministers would bruise and
blacken one another in trying to pull off the
largest share. The point is characteristic. The
subject did not seem to suggest to anyone the
alternative that the House might appoint Mini-
sters and fix the salaries annually. Early in
N.S.W. /constitutional history, when the ques-
tion of Ministerial salaries was before the
House, James Martin argued for an arrange-
ment whereby salaries would come up annually
on the Estimates, and he very nearly carried
a motion to that effect. He argued that
if Ministers’ salaries were placed on the Esti-
mates the House could show, by striking off a
portion of a salary, or all of it, that a particular
Minister had lost the confidence of members.
That amounted to giving the members, as a body,
control over Ministers singly, and if the power
had been obtained members could eject an objec-
tionable Minister without wrecking the Govern-
ment. The party idea was, however, strong just
then, and Martin was beaten by a small majority.
♦
Poor old Aunty Sosh is punctured again. The
awful incompetence, waste, extravagance, etc.,
etc., of the socialistic London County Coun-
cil has been accepted as a thing so obvious that
it didn’t need any proof, and it has been held up
as an awful example of the inevitable consequences
of Socialism. Finally Edwin Waterhouse, the
independent auditor who stands in the very front
of his profession in England, and is the expert
adviser of banks and commercial enterprises, was
called in to expose the rottenness of the whole
business, and various big Anti-Sosh firms under-
took, if necessary, to put up £5OOO to pay for his
investigation if the special audit couldn’t be
paid for out of the municipal funds. How that
part of the business was settled isn’t clear ; but,
anyway, Waterhouse proceeded with his investi-
gation, and just when Aunty Sosh had settled her-
self down, nice and comfortable, to listen to the
curses, he reports that the business undertakings
“indicate sound and careful management,” and
he adds that “there are efficient and accurate
records of its transactions.” And so another
horrible example of the dreadfulness of Socialism
is blown out, and quite a lot of Fat firms must be
sorry they spoke.

The Bulletin sent a Sydney musical
authority to report Melba, and got this result: —
Very doggedly determined to ovate was the crowd that
filled Sydney Town Hall on last Saturday night. And
rightly so. Melba is our prodigy ; she is the most, wonder-
ful thing we have exported to date ; she is a Marchesi
pupil (poor old Cecchi we wave gently aside). She wears
great Orders, and comes to us followed by the affectionate
regard of a dozen European Courts. She is an institution.
Fervently have Melba-lovers been proclaiming these
weeks past that the Voice is “ still there,” still as fresh
as ever it was, with all its “girlish ” quality of years back.
We are proud of our Melba, and so we protest and we
protest. But does the community at the back of its
head believe all these things which it so wants to believe ?
The writer recalls the night, nearly a decade agone,
when he saw this artist for the first time. Covent
Garden was packed from floor to roof to
hear her with the de Reszkes (Jean and Edouard)
in “Romeo et Juliette.” “On Lammas-eve at
night shall she be fourteen’’—almost the music seemed
to phrase the words, and then Juliet came on with thuds
—not Shakspeare’s Juliet, but a Juliet who was matronly,
buxom, and wore black gipsy hair about her shoulders.
Then presently the gorgeous voice poured forth, and
straightway one knew there could be no other Juliet but
this, the most gracious sweet child-woman any poet ever
imagined ; and till the curtain fell at the end the spell
lasted.
Would the Melba voice of last week compel any such
allusion ? It is divine ; but is it divine as a girl’s voice is
divine? Is it still,'for that matter, of an effortless, un-
dimmed freshness even? Melba, honest, forthright
hater of cant as she is, must .have smiled wearily
and oft at the futile stuff which has been written and
chattered about her in this connection of late. She is still
the great singer of bravura passages, though here notice-
ably a little of the old-time consummate ease is want-
ing ; still a most polished and agile vocal genius,
with an organ which in a thousand respects has
marvellously outlived her youth ; but appreciation stops
at that point, and hysteria and all fulsomeness begin.
Of her numbers the Donizetti “ Mad Scene ” im-
pressed and astounded the most. The valse song—
Arditi’s “Se Saran Rose ” appeared to give more
genuine pleasure than any other to a type of audience
whose cup of joy would have overflowed had Gounod’s
Juliette aria been on the programme. Very beautiful
was Lemmone’s flute rendering of Briccialdi’s “Wind
among the Treves”; and Andrew Black and clever Una
Bourne did notably well.
NORMAN,
The hold had man of the Bland Holt Co.
“Blue Bell,” the new pantomime, staged
at Sydney Royal on Saturday night, has one great
merit, anyhow—it serves as the grandest possible
advertisement of the Australian child. Surely so
many pretty, bright, sturdy, happy-looking,
buxom youngsters have never faced the public
before. This is a show where buxomness reigns
supreme. Blue Bell is buxom; her shoeblack
sweetheart is buxom; the Fairy Godmother is
buxom (this is putting it mildly); everyone is
buxom. Of the minor characters, the majority
look like pocket-editions of the principals in a
physical-culture school.
*-■*-**
Blue Bell is a little girl who amasses a
crust for herself and her two tiny sisters by selling
nosegays in the streets. A poor game this finan-
cially, one would be liable to say in one’s haste.
Yet Blue Bell waxes plump on it, and the infant
sisters grow absolutely bloated. A policeman
materialises (a sordid, unsympathetic rascal, as are
all his kind in the eyes of children and other
natural miscreants), likewise the Fairy Godmother
aforesaid. Later a cat and two schoolboys appear,
partly for comic relief purposes, partly to escort
Blue Bell to Fairyland. Once in Fairyland,
and at the Enchanted Castle of King
Sleepy, things hum considerably. The self-
conscious grown-ups begin to suspect the
lurking presence of an Ibsen-like symbolism
somewhere, and that the Law, or Board of Works,
or other dilatory institution of the kind is being
assailed. For, indeed, these children are cunning
enough for anything.
* * * *
After a while it turns out that Blue Bell
has only been dreaming, and hasn’t been to Fairy-
land at all—a sop plainly to the cynically-intelli-
gent modern kid, who, while patiently tolerating
now and again a fatuous parental jest concern-
ing Fairyland, refuses absolutely to have the
discredited old spot seriously handled in drama.
Blue Bell and the shoeblack are adopted ulti-
mately by an immensely wealthy collector of in-
fan tile avoirdupois or something, and theshow ends.
It is finely mounted, and the youngsters —mere
babies some of them—do amazingly well. The
frantic earnestness with which they take them-
selves and their art is a thing of abiding joy.
Rosie Fitzgerald ( cetat 13) plays the name part, and
does it‘like the accomplished little actress she is.
Lily Moore—sister of our own Carrie —is Rosie’s
sweetheart —a shoeblack, who, if trade languished,
could take on the Infant Hercules game at a
moment’s notice. The chorus won’t appeal to the
typical baldhead, and yet a sweeter lot of little
maidens this scribe has not yet seen on any stage.
The show will run every night till next Saturday,
and then there will be matinees afternoonly.
Julius Knight—Julius of Rome, who used
to make legs at Berenis in her litter, and fold his
arms aggressively at Ahenobarbus Caesar —will
rise at the Royal on Saturday night and produce
“The Scarlet Pimpernel.”
** * *
“Lady Madcap” closes her reckless
career at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) to-night, and
will be succeeded on Saturday by “The Girls of
Gottenberg.” On Friday the big theatre will
take a well-earned rest.
* * * *•
Sydney Operatic Society deserves several
approving smites on the back, and one or two
cautions for its production of “Les Cloches de
Corneville ” at Sydney Criterion. The writer
has searched the programme in vain for the
prompter’s name. This is unjust. At one
stage of the proceedings, the other evening,
two performers stood silent on the stage
whilst the prompter spoke both their parts.
Any person who says two parts at one time is
entitled to have his name on the bill. The ladies
—as is usual in these things—did most of the good
work. Miss Gladys Boyd made a dainty
Serpolette. Miss Winifred Birch, as Germaine,
acted and sang with great spirit; but one had the
impression she would have done even better
but for a little nervousness. Mr. S. Grainger
did fairly well as Henri, Marquis de Corne-
ville; and Mr. R. B. Orchard (Father Gaspard),
like the curate’s egg, was excellent in parts. The
Grenicheux of Kenningham was good ; but he has
the advantage of long experience behind him.
Messrs. W. D. Loughlin (the Bailie) and P. A.
Pawley (Gobo), as a rule, did their best. Some-
times they did their very best. Now and then
they almost succeeded. But before it is too late
someone ought to write a text, “Consider the
Prompter,” and hang it up in a prominent position.
Either that or he should be given some sort of a
costume and allowed to prompt at largeon thestage.
Finally, the chorus singing was splendid, and the
show was in aid of the Home for Incurables—
which should be sufficient excuse for the most
carping critic.

This Christinas, Johnny Sheridan will
leave off being the widow of the lamented!
O’Brien, and will blossom out into a baroness in
“ Cinderella’’—the pantomime whose burden he>
and W. Anderson bear on their joint and colossal
shoulders. The Palace will house the show, and
the stage has been stretched and rolled out till,
at one part of the proceedings, it accommodates!
129 people with a squeeze. The production is>
home-grown, and is hatched in a toy shop, which;
is raided by youngsters representing all the-
suburbs, from Woollahra, where youngsters are;
scarce, to Camperdown, where the bailiff is in:
possession. The leg of the ballet will swing for
the first time on Saturday night.

West’s Pictures continue a varied pro-
gramme of interesting films, humorous and
scenic, at Sydney Glaciarium. “ A Naval Re-
view at Spithead ” is accompanied by realistic
cannon roar, but no waves splash on to the audi-
ence, though, this hot weather, they would be
welcome. Other instructive films show “ Sea
Bird Colonies,” “Carl Hagenbeck’s Wild Animal
Park,” “The Slate Industry in France,” and
“ Motoring on U.S.A. Racing Tracks.” There is
the usual filling-up of humorous and colored films.

Price and Revost have come back at
Sydney Tivoli, and they make the solid founda-
tions for a holiday bill. Revost (if it isn’t Price)
does nothing except fall over things and off
things ; and until he arrived nobody could have
had a notion how humorous the Fall of
Man may have been. The other item that
deserves the ink expended on it in the programme
is the concertina-playing of a blind bellows-
blower named Henderson. Lots of lonely ’men
start to “learn the concertina,” but desist when
they see the hostile demonstrations of their
fellow-men and the agony of their fellow-women.
Henderson couldn’t see, so he went straight
ahead, and learned how to make the machine
imitate bag-pipes, cats, church-bells, mosquitoes,
cheese, and other insects.

advent in a different dress was hailed as a fresh
achievement. Surrounded by an atmosphere of
“guff,” and presenting a kaleidoscopic appearance
of theatricalism on an uncongenial platform,
Blanche Arral is a difficult subject for criticism.
She offends one’s sense of the eternal fit-
ness of things whilst compelling admira-
tion for her method of voice production,
and, in a less degree, for her fine, melodious
voice, which seems to have gained strength
and lost a little of its suppleness since she first
sang in Melbourne. It was always more admir-
able even in the production than in the execution,
perhaps. At any rate, the artiste who sings the
Mad Scene, from “Hamlet,” and other things,
within a week or two of Melba’s warbling them,
must expect her trills and runs and shakes to
suffer by comparison. They are brilliant enough,
but they lack perfection of finish. To be severely
truthful, one may say of Blanche Arral’s art that,
though obviously fine, it is conspicuously
tawdry; and she is never an excellent per-
former save in the light comedy and broad
comedy ditties that accord with her personality.
True, she is “great” as compared with the
average public singer in her vocalising of such
florid pieces as “Ah, Tors e Lui ” from
“Traviata,” but in these, as in “ Sancta Maria.”
she sings without sentiment. A mechanical
gesture of an arm, or a prayerful folding of the
hands, gives no expression to the music.
Histrionics merely assist a stage illusion when
they are artistic. Mrs. Arral is not a clever
actress, neither-is a bare-backed platform a stage
illusion.
* * * *
Except for some long waits between turns,
and a protracted half-way interval, the Arral
concert was not dull. Dr. Brice played an organ
solo, and took part in a mixed accompaniment to
“Sancta Maria.” An “ obligato of instrument-
alists,” nine in all, included a violinist (who
swayed with his music as though his fiddle were
his infant and he was rocking it to slumber),
Stoneham on the flute, and a young harpist —Mr.
Torzillo—with a masterly pair of hands and a
Davidian soul. A second concert was announced
for Tuesday, and a third for Saturday, but this
last fixture will presumably be shifted to a more
inviting date.
* * *
Melba has booked Saturday for her own
at the Exhibition building, and the second concert,
on Boxing Night, will be her farewell. Andrew
Black, also a full opera orchestra conducted by
Slapoffski, are engaged for the two events, and
the world at large, which has thus far waited to
“hear Melba” on easy terms, will be able to
gratify its craving at half-a-crown a head. Re-
served chairs at ss. each, and some still more
reserved seats, at half-a-guinea, in the vice-regal
district, were thrown open for selection last
Monday.
** * *
On Saturday likewise the Williamson
panto, at the Melbourne Majesty’s will break out.
Its name is “ Humpty Dumpty ” —for even a
panto, needs a name—and the managerial pro-
mise of “unexampled completeness” is backed
up by an impressive list of people who will figure
in the medley. Phydora, Florence Young, Harry
Shine, and others of the “ Mother Goose ” co. will
be there, together with a number of new girls and
a pair of freshly-imported comedians, both of
whom are familiarly addressed as “Bert.” One
of these is Bert Gilbert, the panto humorist of
yore, who used to stand unsteadily on his dignity
and hiccup, “ How dare you, you blackguard ! ”
*# * *
Wirth’s Olympia re-opens on Saturday,
but not for the purpose of circus. The educated
horses and the odorous menagerie will be away
from Melbourne for a long time, and Philip
Wirth is just off to Europe in quest of astounding
feats for the arena. J. T. West has leased the
Olympia for nine months, and during that period
he will present hundreds of miles of cinematograph
films to local notice. The West Pictures in Mel-
bourne will work in conjunction or alternation
with the show at Sydney Glaciarium, and the bill
— i.e. the pictures on the vast expanse of sheet-
will be changed every week. Olympia has been
floored, and otherwise refitted for the accommoda-
tion of 4000 fellow-citizens, and West hopes that
by strict attention to business, and the aid of a
full military band, he will sometimes attain the
happiness of standing room only.
*# * *
And the Bijou re-opens on Saturday, at
the bidding of Anderson. The last Melbourne
season of the Anderson co. concluded with a long
innings of “The Squatter’s Daughter,” and when
time was called that amazing drama was not out.
The innings is about to be resumed on a smaller
pitch than before.
** * *
A few remarks re Melbourne Princess’s
and Royal are being held over for next issue,
seeing that the first said house will not return to
life until Boxing Night, and the Royal has not
yet exhausted the resources of “The Midnight
Wedding.” A while ago the “Wedding” began
its Last Nights, since when they have seemingly
been growing in number, like the vultures at a
banquet.
** * *
Following the example of the Marshall-
Hall Conservatoire, the Melbourne University
Conservatorium gave its first operatic perform-
ance on Saturday at the Princess’s. There were
scenes from “ Der Freischutz,” “Romeo and
Juliet,” and “Faust”—and, of course, the inevit-
able Spinning Chorus. On the whole the work was
pleasantly mediocre, no one being viciously bad
in acting or singing; but in every instance save
two —both of them masculine ones—the performers
showed a certain rawness and uncertainty. The
“ Romeo and Juliet ” scene gave Sydney Monk an
opportunity to exhibit a charmingly lover-like
manner and a ringing tenor voice. His Juliet,
Miss Treweek, also looked young and amorous,
and sang very brightly. What regret the comely
couple aroused, though, for their own vocal
deficiencies, and the unloverlikeness of great
singers we have heard ! What a sad thing
it is, that great artistry usually entails a
paunch, and that youth can seldom sing like
passionate angels ! The best performance of the
evening was the Kermesse scene from “Faust,”
in which one Henry Thomas sang and acted
Mephistopheles with subtlety and fire. The
choruses were very creditable to Miss Harrington,
who.taught them, but over all was a chillness and
a repression that made for tediousness.
Latest comers at Melbourne Opera House
are Seeley and West, whose clever musical comedy
business has been improved during their four
years of absence from these shores, and Rhodesia,
the comely juggless. The average male professor
juggles more deftly than Rhodesia, but in the
matter of sex the frequent and common
man is not to be compared with the
rare and occasional lady. Other items in the
Rickards show are mostly the same as
they were a fortnight ago—Hayman and Frank-
lin, Finglass, Bluett, Terry and Bentley,
Miss Lottie Kay, Majorie Bray, and the various
human portions of the First Part. They continue
as before. Also La Martine —whose “La ” belies
him —spins a steel baton between his thumb and
fingers until it looks like a four-spoked wheel,
which is a feat more exciting in the observance
than in the cold, hard statement of it.
* * *
“Tom Moore,” at Adelaide Royal, has
resulted thus, after a spirited contest: Andrew
Mack 1, Melody 2, Sentiment 3. The placed
horses were closely attendant on the winner
throughout—in fact, nothing was going stronger
than Sentiment at the finish—but Mack won
handsomely, with his mouth open. Coincidence
was always prominent, with Arrah and Begorra.
Also ran : History and Probability; the former
received several nasty bumps, and was hardly re-
cognisable at the finish, while Probability, after
being jumped on by Coincidence, took no further
part in the event. There have been various other
excitements. The old real-ice palace (the first Aus-
tralia knew) has modestly re-opened as a roller-
rink. The Tivoli continues, though the Hippo-
drome has closed. The railway-travel illusion,
doing great business, is now open 12 hours a day,
and at present takes travellers about Europe,
including a thrilling journey on a single overhead
rail. And Clara Butt, possibly stimulated by a
recent Bulletin statement that she had “ drawn
the last dollar out of Adelaide’s pocket,” has re-
turned for one low-priced evening at the Exhibi-
tion, and drawn the city’s last half-dollar also.
A good New Year resolution —to send
The Lone Hand to a friend abroad, so that he
may think well of Australia. Send the subscrip-
tion money to The Lone Hand office, and we’ll
pay the postage.*
S. Australia has a Royal Commission at
work considering the dirty cow-man and the con-
dition of his produce. It did a good day’s work
last Monday inquiring into the animals, etc., that
are found in cream when it arrives at the factory.
They have been known to include grass, rushes,
ants, blow-flies, maggots, mice, rats, and a jew
lizard over a foot long. It was a very old lizard,
too, and fell to pieces when picked out of the
can. S. Australia now thinks of trying some
beastly State interference with the liberty of the
subject, in the shape of a Dairies Supervision Act
Dead at Bendigo (Vic.), aged 82, Wooten
Lansell, brother to the late George. Wooten
came to Victoria in 1852, and moved on to
Leichhardt, where he spent about 30 years making
the surface of Australia smile by tickling it with
the plough. Like George, he speculated some,
and when he died was a director of something like
20 concerns.
“ Uloola ” : Be statement (B. 28/11/’O7)
that Italian miners were employed rock-chopping
on the Cataract (N.S.W.) contracts. If so, they
must have been there before the job was let to
contractors Lane and Peters. I went to Cataract
with the contractors, and, as timekeeper, knew
every man on the job. All the rock-choppers
then were old sewer men. Some were badly
“ dusted,” and regarded by their mates as doomed.
The mates would calmly remark that they
“ ’Sposed they’d be the same some day.” How-
ever, the rock-chopping at Cataract was not
nearly so deadly as the same work in the sewers,
being mostly in the open on the surface, and not
at the bottom of a narrow trench.
One of those seasoned sinners whom the
new Vic. Vagrancy A.ct is being used to suppress
met trouble the other day through playing tricks
on about the most persistent man in Melbourne.
The lady has the reputation of being an accom-
plished pickpocket, and when she happened on a
stranger in a Collingwood street at about midnight,
what time he was lighting his bike lamp, it was
the work of a moment to pass him the time of
night, whip a purse containing £4 out of his
pocket, and step delicately away like Agag
treading upon eggs. Missing the purse the bikist
followed the lady to a house in a neighboring
street, and entered at her heels. He demanded
his property, and, though the lady scornfully
denied all knowledge of him, he insisted, and
threatened a disturbance, with the police at the
end of it. Foreseeing trouble the house-
holder assumed the r6le of a disturbed
and outraged citizen, and ordered the pair
off his premises, so the lady 'left, and
so did her victim. They traversed several
streets together, the lady putting on an air of
virtuous indignation, while the gentleman at
intervals cordially invited her to come with him
to a police station and argue the matter out. The
dame presently wearied of her escort’s company,
and started to scale a handy fence, but the other
ungallantly grabbed her by the leg—and felt a
purse in her stocking. This only made him a
more determined citizen ; but the lady still denied
all knowledge of his purse. After much argu-
ment, she offered to allow him to search her, and
to that end removed her skirt in the main street.
He perceived the why and wherefore of that
tricky move when she started to scream and
threatened to charge him with assault and several
other crimes. After that there was more argu-
ment and much walking to and fro, till finally the
pair happened on one of Melbourne’s scarce con-
stables, and the trio adjourned to the watch-house
at 2.30 a.m. There the feminine was recognised
as an old offender, and passed in. Next day he
got his money, and she got her twelve months.

“ J.A.C.”: Good old Ben Leveson has joined the countless
caravan of the dead. Although not occupying a very high
rank in “ the business,” nobody was better known, or better
liked. He was connected with the Australian operatic
profession for upwards of 40 years, and was a member of
W. S. Lyster’s first opera company, singing in the
chorus and minor parts. His cheval de bataille was
the King of Spain in “ Mantana,” and thereby
hangs a tale. Ben had a slight nasal twang.
Another Hebrew, who used to dabble in theatrical
management, and who had a twang like an elephant
breathing through its nose, on one occasion complained :
“ I heard Bed last digh.t as the Kigd of Spaid, add, do
you kdow, Bed calls it Baritada. Yes, I distigdly heard
hib say Baritada—dot Baritada, but Baritada ! ” Ben
had one of the gentlest natures I have ever known. In
the course of an acquaintance of over a quarter of a cen-
tury I never heard him say a harsh word of anyone. He
was a born gentleman, quite different from being a
gentleman by birth. His last theatrical “ shop ” was as
stage-doorkeeper at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, but I think
his manner was too suave to suit the management ; at
any rate, when the theatre was rebuilt after the fire, he
was shunted. Ben, who was horn in Sydney, must have
been about 70 years of age. He was one of a numerous
family. He had a sister who was an excellent pianist,
although born deaf and dumb. She was sent to London
to consult an eminent specialist, and died there, at the
age of 22. His brother John, not very long-dead, was
editor and owner of a Masonic journal published in
Sydney, and was about the first to run theatrical adver-
tising programmes in that city. Ben’s real name was
Solomon, and his father, Philip Solomon, was a Cockney
Jew, who came to Sydney some time after Captain Cook.
Old man Solomon built a house at the corner of Erskine
and Clarence streets, which he called Solomon’s Temple.
For the last few years Ben' was accorded the hospitality
of the Montefiore Jewish Home.
Abiding at the Australia just now are the only Julius,
also the Elbert-Orton lady, likewise sweet Miss Beatrice
Irwin, and, moreover, Ola Jane Humphrey. In addition
an Unpleasantness has taken lodgings at the hostelry.
There is a rift in the relations of these beautiful peoplel'
Some do not play speaks with others, and the Baleful
Glare is liable to pass at any moment across the public
dining-room and turn the intervening milk sour, or
ullage some unoffending person’s whisky and soda. The
whole trouble is connected vaguely with the harassing
problem of whether Julius has or has not allowed himself
to be married recently ; and it is all most lamentable and
diverting.
The rumors as to Jack Wren’s far-reaching designs on
theatrical management in Melbourne have boiled down to
the fact that, firstly, he is “presenting” Mrs. Blanche
Arral (the somewhat mysterious “ prima donna of four
continents ”) in two or three concerts at Melbourne Town
Hall. Last year the lady presented herself —or her
Yankee husband did it for her—at the Princess’s. In
Wren’s more important character of theatre-lessee he will
presumably not make his debut until next midwinter.
He has contracted with Meynell and Gunn for a sub-lease
of the Royal for some months (four or five) in each year
until 1912, when their existing lease expires. George
Musgrove will be associated with him, and will direct the
director, even though his—G.M.’s —name doesn’t figure
at the head of affairs. As for the new theatre planned
by Architect Pitt, in Russell-street, it will be Anderson’s
spec.—not Wren’s. Seemingly the profits from Wren’s
Tote, plus the profits of his City Tatt.’s Club, were almost
as great as they were reported, and, notwithstanding his
alleged heavy losses in backing his own and other people’s
’osses, he is still full of money. Incidentally he has let
himself be lured into the cheap tailoring trade, and his
scheme for clothing Melbourne’s manhood in 30s. suits is
said to be going to involve him in the ownership of forty
shops, city and suburban. But forty, even if you speak it
slowly, sounds a large number to begin with.
Hiphen ” : At a cinematograph entertainment, the
tiickergraph wa,s ruining our eyesight with pictures of
rugged mountains and witching lakes. “ Oh, these Nor-
wegian fiords ! ” exclaimed a lady next me, clasping her
hands ecstatically. “ Aren’t they simply too lovely ! If
we only lived at Home and could run over there in two or
three days.” “Pardon me,” I interrupted, “ these aren’t
fiords; they’re the Maoriland Sounds, and you can get
there in three or four days.” “The Sounds!” she ex-
claimed disgustedly. “Pooh!” She jumped up and
went out and asked for her money back. No, she didn’t;
that’s a lie. But she certainly did snort contemptuously,
and seem to kinder lose interest in the entertainment.
“N ”: Melba feels an itching on the instep of the beak,
the new soprano has appeared in London—Mrs. Tetraz-
zini, from South America, an overwhelming success at

Melbourne Age made open confession, in a recent lead-
ing article on the theatre : “ Newspaper criticism is
largely responsible for the desertion of the cultured
theatre. . . . There is very little genuine newspaper criti-
cism now. .. . Censure has been practically banned from
the columns of the press.” This is lamentably true, and,

demonstrations in its own treacly theatrical notices
David does badly, but, with true Scottish unction, he lets

“Bloggs”: Had 2J hours’ of edification at Sydney
Hippodrome t’other night. An allegorical play—“ Dare-
devil Dick”—which deals absorbingly with the warfare
that rages ever between the powers of Light and Dark-
ness, is running there. On the one hand is ranged
Daredevil Richard (Virtue); on■ the other Don Manuelo
(Vice). Richard’s supporters are the ladies of the
cast, a sheriff’s officer, an Irishman, and a parson,
who looks as Hamlet would have looked had
lie (Hamlet) attained middle age and contracted the
drink craze. Manuelo leads a gang of Indian scene-
shifters and cowboys and (most wonderful) a monoeled
British dude—who is probably the first theatrical dude
ever found on the side of Vice. The worsting that Vice
gets at the end of each act is most cheering, and the joy
with which the audience greets the series of paralysing
rebuffs dealt out to Manuelo reflects
credit on its moral character. Judkins
should visit the Hippodrome and see for
himself whether the drama can’t be
elevating when it tries.
“J.D.”: Herewith mv unbiassed esti-
mate of the Melba voice and Art, as con-
sidered after a lapse of six years :
Noticeably the voice has lost in compass,
freshness, and sympathetic quality. The
high notes are failing. Melba, neverthe-
less, is not cultivating her medium
register, while the free use she makes of
the chest register tends to rob her tones
of their old liquid sweetness —attributes
that rightly should stand to them for a
very long time yet. Patti, whom I last
heard in the Albert Hall over 12 years
ago, was an example in point. Her head-
notes had left her then ; but her singing
in the lower register was an artistic
treat; and her mezza voce a thing not
to be forgotten. Melba’s voice in the
late ’nineties had de diable.
Particularly was it remarkable then for
its even quality, and purity of attack.
Both of these were lacking last week
—markedly so in the Bach-Gounod “ Ave
Maria,” concerning which item an
amount of humbug has appeared of late
in the local press to make the judicious
grieve. Of yore Melba’s “ Hamlet ” scenas
were astounding—if only for the singer’s
entire absence of effort in vocalisation.
That is so no longer. Her method now
lacks a little that unstriving vocal charm
(witness those ascending scales of hers)
which is really the only raison d’etre
for bravura passages. In the “Hamlet”
scena the artist’s singular lack of mezza
voce was hard to explain till one ana-
lysed and realised that the excessive use
of the chest register (killing to a pure
mezza voce) was responsible. A great
charm in the Melba art, and one which
seems to have gone unnoticed during the
present tour, is the great singer’s im
proved diction. Time was when Melba’s
pronunciation of his tongue induced
pangs of sorrow in the worshipping
Gaul. To-day her French is delicious ;
which is a reason why one prefers her in
French numbers rather than in English.
“When Knights Were Bold,” the piece that Frank
Thornton will shortly stage in Sydney, would have been
qualified for production at the recent Women’s Exhibi-
tion in Melbourne. It is claimed to be woman’s most
successful attempt in farcical-comedy writing thus far,
the authoress (“Charles Marlowe ”) having divested her
pen-name of its pants after the comedy had caught on.
This device of sex-alias is a strictly one-sided practice.
Now if a man named John Jones were to write anything
under the name of Letitia Brown he’d be snorted at when
the fraud came to light, but a she gets honorable men-
tion for calling herself Charles Marlowe or George Eliot.
Said Sara Bernhardt, with a quite British smugness,
last month, apropos the suggested wiping out of the
censor’s office in the United Kingdom : “ The public is
the true censor and the only censor. In Paris we have no
censor, but if a piece is obscene or truly immoral, it is
hissed out of the theatre.” Which is probably the richest
thing, this lady has got off her bust for some years.
Putting aside the extremely steep productions at the
Nouveaut.es and the Grand Guignol theatres (both quite
fashionable), Paris still has its Palais Royal. All
the world goes to the Palais Royal. A first
night there is quite an event. The acting, taken by
and large, is classes ahead of anything to be found in
a London theatre (French people act as naturally as
they breathe), and the lines are humorous and witty to
a degree, but also they are obscene—obscene as any to
be found in a Paul de Kock novel. The conventions of a
Palace Royal farce change slightly from time to time (for
instance, nowadays, it is mostly the husband who
triumphs in the third act, and not the lover), but the
indecencies remain fixed and immutable. Last of these
shows I saw was two years ago. All the old props I had
known eight or nine years previously—the double-bed
scene, the unclothed heroine, the night-shirted hero—
were on hand. The oply—to me—wholly new jape was
where the well-meaning aunt of the newly-married couple
who are staying with friends at the sea-side accidentally
dopes the entire house-party with what the audience
learns, amid shrieks of laughter, is an aphrodisiac.
Writer has seen a fair number of Paris shows in his time,
but, not yet has he encountered the one a Paris audience
would hiss because of its obscenity.
“ D.” : Picked up the World’s News last Saturday, and
in half-a-column concerning mummer Kingston unearthed
the following:—(1) “He was educated at St. Augustus
Benedictine College, Ramsgate.” (He wasn’t; for there
is no such place, nor any Saint of that name in the
Roman calendar. St. Augustine’s is the name of the
Ramsgate education-factory ; it’s as well-known in Kent
as cricketer Hutchings.) (2) “ Kingston early attracted
the notice of William Terris.” (The murdered melodrama
star did not spell his name that way, otherwise the
item contains the truth-germ.) (3) “Under Frohman
Kingston played in ‘ Two Little Vagrants.’ ” (Never. No
such play exists.) (4) “In mentioning his most success-
ful parts we must go back to Sydney Carton in ‘The
Old Way.’” (Presumably “The Only Way” is meant.
Perhaps the writer had some hazy idea re Besant’s
Herr Paulus, and his gibberings about the Ancient Way.)
The piquancy of the above lies in the fact that the Daily
Telegraph (which is guilty of the World’s News) has
ever been loudest of the anti-tariff press in its talk of
the educative influence of dirt-cheap newspapers.
John Wren (of Collingwood), now a full-blown entre-
preneur, employs the same literary expert to fashion his
advts. re, Mrs. Blanche Arral as was turned loose to set
forth in glowing adjectives the stupendous prowess of
Boshter Bill, and the pugilistic virtues of Micko Plugug
and Ginger Thickear. There is no mistaking the flam-
boyant style, the swelling adjectives, and the superh
disregard for the laws of syntax. This is how one
breathless burst of eulogy is wound up :
“The Strange Song-bird, who entered with success
that famous aviary wherein has nested so many brilliant
rivals. “ Yours faithfully,
“JOHN WREN.”
Another opens with the following gem :
“ Blanche Arrals’s Opening Programme of her farewell
series will contain many of those charming little chef
d’oeuvres, breathing in their every note the gaiety and
mirthfulness of those climes of which they are charac-
teristic.”
That sounds so fine that it’s really a pity it doesn’t mean

MRS. MELBA’S MOTOR OAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I heard a mighty tooting on the wind,
I saw the people scatter wide and stare.
The streets were rocking wildly to and fro,
I also felt a twisting in my hair.
I hit the roadway hard and saw the stars,
My brainpan gave me notice of the jar,
And as I raised my eyes I heard them screech,
“ It’s Mrs. Melba’s lovely motor car.”
“ Great Scott! ” I said, “ her rotten motor car ! ”
I bought an ev’ning paper going home,
And saw a big black-lettered heading line ;
I read along, and then began to laugh,
1 found it was an extra motor fine.
As I crept on I thought I guessed the end,
And, bless my soul, I hadn’t gone so far,
When hip, hip, hip hooray, I found, my oath,
’Twas Mrs. Melba’s beauteous motor car—
Hip, hip, hooray, her smellful motor car.
I went to bed, and started dreaming fast.
And, bet your boots, it was a motor dream,
For, all day long, in suburb or in town,
I listen to that horrid racking scream.
It’s thumping through my poor head all the day,
At night I’m ill, and so it troubles Ma.
I’m looking now for peace, I’m full right up
Of talk of Melba’s 1-o-v-e-l-y motor car—
God help us all, her fiendish motor car.

The more we master the forces of nature, the
more do our chances of accidents multiply, even as the
tamer’s dangers increase in proportion to the number of
wild animals which he “puts through their tricks” in
the cage. Formerly, we avoided the contact of these
forces as much as possible ; to-day, they have gained
admittance to our household. And so, notwithstanding
our more prudent and peaceable manners, it happens to us
more often than to our fathers to look pretty closely upon
death. The South British Insurance Company of 12
Bridge Street, Sydney, issues policies covering all classes
of accident and seventeen (17) specified sicknesses for
£2 2s. per annum upwards—special policies for your
special needs.*

PENEOLD'S
WINES
Sydney city council has at last got
authority to tap Cohen for £160,000 to pay for
land in Hay and Engine streets on which to
build new produce markets; so the squalid,
smellful old muddle through which people have
to pass when they are poured out of the Owe-
Sullivan’s railway station will disappear in course
of time. Between his breakfast and his work,
the man who walks into the city from the so-
called Sydney railway station gets almost as
many smells as tourists pay heaps of money to sniff
up through their handkerchiefs in, say, Canton ;
for after he has picked his way through the rotten
fruit that has been thrown out on to the streets,
he must still take a fill of the Chinese stinkeries
and a dozen cheap doss-and-liash houses, from the
Salv’army “Palace” onwards, and they all seem
to boil their cabbage under the gratings that open
on to the street. The Fat person who trams and
cabs doesn’t notice these things; but the shop
and factory person, who can’t do either, must
often reach toil in a very agitated frame of
stomach.
* * * *
Rupert Clarke is taking a sporting in-
terest in Lieutenant Shackleton’s Antarctic Ex-
pedition, and is understood to have given it some
financial impetus. Regarding this dash for the
South Pole as an international race, with the
French and Belgian parties as the other competi-
tors, Rupert reckons that if Shackleton can get a
fair run with his experimental ice-motor he will
win handsomely. Also, he may do the trick with
a Siberian pony if the motor breaks down. Any-
how, the Britisher’s mount is worth backing.
The folks of Broken Hill publish strange
“narky” advts. about each other, and it is an
everyday occurrence to find Smith advertising
that he won’t be responsible for his wdfe’s debts or
his daughter’s conduct, and his wife replying that
she always paid her own debts and also bought
her husband’s beer, and the daughter stating in
print that if her pa’s conduct could bear inspec-
tion as well as her own, he wouldn’t be the loafer
he is. As for the advts. stating that if Mrs.
Johnson, of Opal-street, doesn’t stop saying
things about Mrs. Ferguson, of Potch-road, she
will be prosecuted to the very threshold of the
High Court; and that Mrs. Jones will be
arrested if she doesn’t apologise ; and that Mrs.
Thompson must keep her goat at home or take the
consequences ; and that the brolly must be re-
turned at once as the person is known—they are
like the sands upon the sea-shore for innumerable-
ness. But the Broken Hillite also rushes his
gratitude into print on all possible occasions ; he
even thanks by advertisement the people who
“ kindly inquired ” when his wife with the kettle
in her hand fell over the dog in the yard and
broke her neck. And out of all the thankful
notices, this, from a recent Miner, is surely the
limit:—
THANKS.
T BEG to tender my sincerest Thanks to Dr. Setzke
for his successful treatment, of my Wife, who suf-
fered for 17 years with running sores of both legs.
My Wife has been cured within a fortnight.
, Argent-street,
Broken Hill.
Only a man here and there would publish his
wife’s legs in that reckless fashion. But what a
time the press would have had in Galilee, if there
had been a press in Galilee, when the local in-
habitant rushed into print to thank Barnabas,
the apostle, on the ground that his daughter used
to go about naked and possessed by 49 devils, but
was now quite well and looking after the house-
work in a perfectly rational manner; and when
the other inhabitant stated in the agony column
that the old family leper was miraculously healed,
thank you, and was open to take ringbarking
contracts on Lebanon !
* * * *
“ Hassan ” : Recently the boarded-out
and unwelcome infant had a bad fortnight in
Melbourne. From the 16th to the 30th of Novem-
ber the district coroner inquired into the deaths
of 10 boarded-out babies. Five more small
corpses were discovered dead in various parts of
the city and suburbs, and one kiddy was over-
lain. It almost pays nowadays not to be born at
all in Melbourne. The Argus had a long article
in which it attributed the whole trouble to a
bad milk supply. The milk inspection is certainly
shockingly inefficient, but it doesn’t seem to
explain the deaths of such a large percentage
of boarded-out babies. Do these small unfor-
tunates get all the bad milk, or what? And bad
milk doesn’t explain the infant who is found dead.
As a matter of fact, until illegitimate children get
the same care and attention and love as legal
babies, they have got to die in large numbers.
And they won’t get the same care while the birth
of an irregular child is considered (by the parson
and the Dorcas Society and a large section of
society) an indelible stain upon the mother. As a
matter of fact, if the State can’t find the father,
and make him pay adequately for his offspring’s
upbringing, it should take charge of the mother
and child itself. An illegitimate baby is of much
more importance than a lot of things the State
does take care of.

The N. S. Wales police count up Black
Brother every October; but it is an irregular
reckoning, Black Brother
having a habit of wandering
over boundaries without
giving notice. One thing is
certain, though—he is drop-
ping out to the extent of from
60 to 150 a year ; and there
are fewer full-blooded children
(under 20 or thereabouts) than
there are adult males, so he
will drop out still faster later
on. In some districts the
piccaninny is just about un-
known ; scattered over the
Milparinka district, in the
droughty far north-west,
among 77 all-blacks there are
only two youngsters. The
half-castes, however, don’t
diminish ; last year more than
two little social sins arrived
to every all-black piccaninny.
There are nine or more State
schools where the colored
youngsters are taught, and
these schools are ‘"inspected ”
like the rest. The reports
don’t vary much—the pupils
can imitate, but reasoning is
painful ; so their writing,
when they have a line at the
top of a copy-book to guide
them, is their star subject.
All told, there are over 7000
aborigines in the State, of
whom only 2500 are now
reckoned all-blacks.
• » ■*.
It is probably the ex-
traordinary luck of Ander-
son and Meynell and Gunn that tempts John
Wren (late Jack) into the theatrical profes-
sion. Anderson rushed in where angels fear to
tread, and scooped riches and glory. Meynell
and Gunn followed in the same game, and oof and
honor are theirs. Why should not J. Wren, pur-
veyor of popular pony sports and pugilistic
comedies, succeed, too, as an entrepreneur? He
has only to take the rope off the ring, and call it
a stage, and there you are. Still there is a larger
scope for the burning of fingers in playing the
plays than there is even in playing the pugs or the
ponies. Australia is a fair hog for its bit of drama
while it has its trousers full of shillings, but the
moment the pinch comes the theatre is the first
item it economises on, and then the theatrical
manager becomes a pale martyr to the cause of Art.
The deadly hat-pin nearly settled the
account of a man who fled into Melbourne
Hospital, the other evening, and desired the
house surgeon to remove a spike from his chest.
After the doctor had probed for and withdrawn
about six inches of broken head-skewer from the
region of the heart, he asked how it happened ;
but the victim declined to be more explicit than
that he had been “ larking with a girl.” Evi-
dently he had got hold of the wrong sort of girl.
It really seems as if the abolition of the
British Peerage would be a desirable reform, con-
sidering what a lot of unnecessary turmoil is
caused by the Earl of Mountararat and Earl
Tolloller, and their kind. For years the relations
of a party by the name of Druce, who used to keep
a fancy-goods shop in London, and who, so far as
is known, died and was buried 43 years ago, have
been trying to prove that the deceased was iden-
tical with one Cavendish-Bentinck, better known
as Duke of Portland. Portland is said to have
gone into the fancy business under the name
of Druce, and worn bogus whiskers, and treated
himself to a mock funeral for no reason that any-
one can allege. There seems to be no doubt that
both the deceased Druce and the Duke of Port-
land were real persons, but there is much doubt as
to whether they were the same person. A curious
part of the business is that two joint-stock com-
panies have been formed to push the claims of
various members of the Druce family to the Port-
land title and estates. As a result of the clash of
interests, one Druce has been prosecuting another
in a London Police Court for perjury, and the
directors of the latest-formed company ai’e suing
each other in the Court of Chancery, where it was
stated the other day that the shareholders were
mostly artisans and domestic servants. It is not
easy to understand the state of mind of artisans
and domestics who risk their money in an attempt
to get a peerage for a total stranger, and on such
evidence.

A N.S.W. Public Service panjandrum of
tremendous inconsequence lately laid a series of
charges against another big public servant, and
the Public Service Board has been engaged in
hearing them in the full panoply of war. One
charge was that the big official had been pur-
chasing quantities of high-priced liquids with
Government funds, and using them at his own
table. Before this rumpus accused and accuser
had been bosom friends and frequent partakers of
each other’s hospitality; and when before the
Triumvirate the accused upset the gravity of the
Board by declaiming in fierce anger that his
accuser had drunk most of the alleged misplaced
liquor, and by asking the Plank’s candid opinion
of a man wbo would go and drink his brother
man’s whisky, and then pop up serenely and say
it was stolen ; and he wanted to know why didn’t
the panjandrum charge himself with receiving.
The N.S.W. Public Service is gloating over the
whole business, and its sympathies are all in one
direction.
*»* * *
Things are getting mixed under the new
Act for the suppression of certain pubs in N.S.W.
The special courts which are to decide what pubs
shall be shut, in accordance with the local option
vote at the last general election, have begun to
sit. In the metropolitan district one Judge and
two Police Magistrates decided that proceedings
would have to be hung up till a short amending
Act filled up a hole which Costs had observed
somewhere or other; yet out in the Far West
another Judge and two other Police Magistrates
threw scorn at the 217 (or thereabouts) .alleged
flaws that were held up for inspection, and in the
Broken Hill electorate gave half-a-dozen pubs
from six to 12 months’ notice to disconnect the
beer pump. Mostly the pubs that have to go are
barns built in the days when, so long as the beer
was wet and considerable, nothing else mattered
much ; and in some cases even the best of conduct
hasn’t been able to save them from the blue pencil
of the pub sub-editor.
WINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Unfermented wine,” was the subject of a paper deli-
vered by Mrs. Downing. Most of the unions had been
able to persuade such of the local church authorities as
were open to conviction that it was humane and scriptural
to use pure and unalcoholised juice of the grape when
celebrating the Holy Communion. Footscray was, how-
ever, the only union reporting all churches in the town
to be using unfermented wines. Mrs. Press (treasurer)
said she had herself made many experiments in the manu-
facture of unfermented wines, and hoped she would soon
be successful.— Christian Temperance Mission item from
Victoria.
Alcohol is in the air !
Alcohol is in the clay 1
You will meet it everywhere—
Everywhere, and every day—
Go, be buried ’neath the wave—
Alcohol is in the grave !
Other people, Missus Press,
Ere you, made experiment—
Savages of scanty dress
On a new distraction bent—
Wine fermenting for a drunk,
Oozing from the palm-tree’s trunk.
“ Wanities,” oh ! Missus Press !
Are the drinks of blacks and whites ;
“ Wanities ! ” no more nor less —
As the Reverend Stiggins writes—
Unto Brick Lane he did come
Full of unfermented rum !
Full of unfermented rum,
Smote he Brother Tadger’s spleen—
Brother Tadger did not come
Up to time—nor was he seen—
Where the cold tea nods its head
At the crumpet in its bed..
Once I had a drink of juice
Which had not fermented been !
How I went upon the loose !
Ask my brother saints who’ve seen—
Since then I’ve adhered to gin,
Finding it the lesser sin 1
Better devils that we know—
Better beer whose bite is sure—
Than the fluid you’d endow
With the virtues you secure
From a faith that sure prevents
Chemic changes and ferments.
Prayer and unfermented wine—
Law of gravitation dead—
Mercury, the thermal sign,
Works no more, but snores in bed.
I admire your certainty—
“ Prayer doth conquer chemistry.”
Meddle not, oh ! Missus Press !
With the thrice accursed drink !
(Saints do always make a mess
When they walk too near the brink).
’Ware the yeast that circumvents,
’Ware the microbe that ferments.
vie.

S. M. Herald is not more than half
satisfied that the British navy is really all it is
cracked up to be; so she has been round among
all the naval men she knows, begging them for
pity’s sake to say whether it is true that “none
of the ships of the British auxiliary squadron now
in Australian waters are seaworthy.” The Garden
Island authorities were “as dumb as oysters”—
all but one, who said “Rubbish !” Which was
very natural, since there is no auxiliary squadron
in existence. Still, one genius, though he told
the Herald that it was not permissible to discuss
service matters, said :
The plan of constructing a ship that could withstand
the explosion of a torpedo at close quarters is not yet
discovered.
That is a startling statement for even the least
reliable naval expert to make. The Dreadnought
is supposed to be torpedo proof, and here’s what a
better expert than the Herald says about it: —
This “proofness” resides in part in solid bulkheads,
but these are new only in our navy. The Pallada and
Bayan, torpedoed at Port Arthur, kept afloat by virtue
of the same thing.
So even the information that the Herald did get
landed her farther away from the truth than she
wa3 at the beginning.

A PUBLIC NEED.
“ Why in thunder don't you get a wireless clothes-line, Maria?"
The Home of Compassion, in a Wellington
(M.L.) suburb, run by the Rev. Mother Mary
Joseph Aubert, wants a reservoir. There is plenty
of hill to cut down to make a reservoir, but the
problem arose how to pay for the removal of the
soil. Mother Aubert mentioned her diffi-
culty to the Governoress, and Lady Plunket
said: “Lend me spade and wheelbarrow,
and I’ll dig the reservoir.” Accordingly
she organised a Government House working
bee, led by herself and Lord Plunket, and
they went to the site of the works and
shifted several barrowfuls. Now, ever
since, the populace of Wellington has
been getting in its own road at the Home
of Compassion, and the Trades and Labor.
Council rivals the Chamber of Commerce
jn digging out that hill, or what is left of
it. Mother Aubert is able to sit down and wear
a happy smile. As she said to a reporter : “Our
reservoir is as good as built.” The moral of this
is that the Government ought long ago to have
persuaded Plunket to put in half a day’s graft on
the Main Trunk line. That undertaking was
commenced 24 years ago, and it isn’t finished yet !
A lawyer writes
“The Crown” has fallen in again over its attempts to
convict Mrs. Scales, a reputed Sydney fortune-teller, for
an offence under an Act of Parliament passed in the
reign of George IV. Mrs. Scales’ fate was closely allied
with that of one Quon Yik, convicted originally as a
pakapu or fantan player. The Chow appealed to the
High Court, where the conviction was quashed on the
ground that the old Act of George IV. did not apply to
N.S.W. This ancient Act gave persons convicted under
it the right of appeal to a Court of Quarter Sessions.
Counsel alleged that when that Act was passed Quarter
Sessions were not known in N.S.W., therefore things
didn’t fit; and the Court accepted the story, and let
Quon Yik go.
Now, as a matter of fact the advice given to the High
Court was quite wrong. So when Mrs. Scales came up
before the Police Court, the N.S.W. Crown Law Office,
which had discovered the error, informed the police
magistrate, and he nodded sagely and said it was a
deplorable fact, but he must obey the High
Court. So he dismissed the case. “The Crown”
trotted Mrs. Scales off to the Full Court, which said the
same thing as the P.M. Thence “ the Crown,” still with
its expensive barrister in tow, confronted the High Court
and demanded a conviction of the clairvoyant.
The Chief Justice admitted that the previous decision
was wrong on the one ground the Court had given.
He then read the old Act of George IV., and pointed out
the penalties Mrs. Scales was liable to. For the
first offence she was to be whipped and taken to the
place where she was born. For the second offence she
was to be whipped again and a piece of gristle removed
from her right ear, and for a third offence she was to be
hanged. As Mrs. Scales was charged with telling quite a
heap of fortunes, it seemed as if all these things were
liable to happen to her at once—the two whippings, the
ear-operation, the removal to her native town, and the
hanging. Chief-Justice Griffith, however, added briefly
that what might seem ill right in 1827 was barbarism in
1907, and refused on general principles to allow the
appeal. And it'really seems as if “ the Crown ” might
have saved a lot of Costs if it had read the Act before it
appealed so much. Surely it didn’t really hope to get
Mrs. Scales whipped twice, and then removed and muti-
lated and hanged !

A most expensive half-sovereign’s worth
of law is debited to an Adelaide dentist, who, in
the course of a series of nagging cross-actions with
another citizen, found himself obliged to sign
recognisances to avoid being arrested. He signed
all right, one Saturday morning, but objected to
the ten-shilling fee tlie Court charged. That
afternoon, just as (he says) he had changed his
mind and wanted to pay up, he was arrested, and
it was Monday before bail could be arranged. He
reckoned the Sunday in gaol was worth £lOOO to
him, and sued the police-magistrate for that sum.
Action (as anybody could have foretold) dis-
missed ; and with Costs “ as between solicitor and
client”—which is a horrid form of the animal,
with specially long claws and teeth. And all
this over—originally—a ten-bob fee !
Sydney City Council wants to hear from a
man with a scheme for turning an elephant into
pork or some other brand of payable meat. Back
in 1891 the ancient market buildings that were
begun in the days of Governor Bourke were
pulled down, and a large ornamental elephant was
raised in its place, and the large ornamental
elephant has in nine years cost something over
£75,000 more than it has earned. Things are not
getting any better, because of the growing cost of
keeping up the vast buildings with the inside like
a tomb—which is the elephant previously alluded
to. So the Oouucil wants suggestions for turning
the whole concern into something that will
pay for its keep, and it is prepared to give £lOOO
to the man who can tell how it is to be done.
Apparently, however, it isn’t prepared to spend
more than £75,000 on the reformation of the beast;
so if anybody has a scheme that will cost more
than that, he had better boil it down before sub-
mitting it for the inspection of the Board that
the council has appointed to consider all the
bright ideas, with plans attached.
That School for Scandal, the Wallace
divorce case, continues to give lessons on the
manners and customs of Melbourne sassietv.
There is one change, though. After the few first
days of fierce struggle for front seats, when the
court cleaners swept up buttons, laces, belts,
satchels, bits of muslin, and sundries in the
morning, the police kept order in stern fashion.
Now the leading lady in the tragedy enters and
leaves court by the little door near the jury room,
passes out into the courtyard guarded by con-
stables, and enters the hansom where her sister
waits behind drawn blinds. The pair don’t look a
little bit like the part that the other side assigns
to them. But little nurse—she who wept all
night because of “Ruby’s” alleged naughty
threatenings—little nurse looks just what, she
ought to—quiet, cool, fresh, and womanly. So
far, the most artistic touch in the drama has been
the dressing of the leading lady : mostly of black,
but seldom the same costume two days running ;
relieved often with touches of white, or toned up
with a shimmery gossamer. The sombre hue was
deepest while witness after witness painted her
in colors to correspond; then, as her witnesses
threw an atmosphere of gay but quite proper tint
over the proceedings at “Novar,” “Ruby’s”
gowning grew quite cheerful.
# W * *
“ E.D.” wrestles with the problem, and
gives it up :
The interest the Wallace divorce case has excited in
Melbourne is unprecedented so far as my experience
goes. I remember many celebrated cases, but not one
that took so intimate a hold of the people, that hit them
where they lived so emphatically. It does not appeal to
“ society ” alone, but to all classes and conditions, and is
discussed as vehemently on a workman’s tram as in a
loaded motor. It has given an enormous impetus to
newspaper sales; and “off” journals which went
frankly into details were simply rushed, second-hand
numbers being saleable at fancy figures. At
home and abroad, in theatre, office, railway,
restaurant, and church, one got nothing but Wallace
case. The court was rushed daily by swarms of well-
dressed people, many of them “ladies.” Some of the
women dragged children with them. Facts and specula-
tions were greedily exchanged and discussed, and people
were ready to tear down houses only to get a glimpse of
the respondent. It all beats me. There is nothing very
exceptional in the case. The lady is a beauty, but nothing
like as distracting a vision as the hysterical squeak of femi-
nine scribes would lead us to believe. The details of the
alleged sins and wickednesses are the same old sordid
details that have done service in procuring a thousand
divorces. The trials and tribulations of the hus-
band, the vicissitudes of the wife, the vagaries of the
“ c 0.,” as set forth, and contradicted, in the sworn testi-
mony, are all tale as stale as “Old Mother Hubbard.”
But the greedy interest does not pass.
The avidity with which the Melbournian fastened upon
the smallest, driest details of Wallace v. Wallace ex-
tended even to bricks and mortar. “ Novar,” the house
in which most of the boorish and bibulous gaieties
described by Wallace’s witnesses were said to have taken
place, was besieged by crowds every day of the trial, and
on the intervening Sundays thousands made pilgrimage
to the place, and stood in the roadway, staring stupidly
at the front of the building, or in the right-of-way, staring
fatuously at the rear. “Novar” is in an out-of-the-way
part of St. Kilda, but these people thought it worth a
long tram journey merely to look steadily for half-an-
hour at the bricks and mortar, inside which the “gay”
dullards are alleged to have thrown chicken bones at each
other and emulated the refined exuberances of the bar
parlor in a snide pub ( vide press reports).
Melbourne Paunch, with a fine, fatuous capacity for
divining the tastes of fatuous people (this is sometimes
called “ gauging public opinion ”), catered for the needs
of the silly multitude by printing a picture of “Novar,”
front view, and a picture of “Novar,” back view. The
“back view” is mainly corrugated iron fence, but it
includes a wooden gate—just an ordinary wooden gate,
but the gate through which somebody is alleged to have
slipped in and out on divers occasions, and that makes it
thrilling. Now we anxiously await pictures of the tram
the lady used, and the hansom she was seen in, also the
corner she turned round on divers excursions, and the
spot of ground she is said to have sat upon on the mo-
mentous occasion when she turned away her head.
And why not a photo of the bottle that “ Hec ” carried
the whisky in ?
* * * *
The scandal of the long divorce case in
Melbourne seems to have had a legal as well as a
social character. Even Brother Madden, the
learned Chief Justice, who is regarded as a judicial
whale for petty details of domestic disturbance,
made some candid comment on the protracted
cross-examination of the petitioner. “We have
been here nearly three days, and we have not
touched the question of actual misconduct yet.
We are hardly on the fringe of it,” said the pre-
siding Wig. Later on he put the matter in a
financial light: “All this is not only irrelevant,
but costs a great deal of money .” Which suggests
that the walls of every law court should be hung
with illuminated texts from the Unholy Bible of
the Learned Brethren. For instance, “ Money is
the Root of all Irrelevancy,” “Time is the Thief
of Costs,” “ A Long Purse makes a Long Suit,”
and “ Circumstances alter Cases.” In the Court
of Dirty Linen cynics always chuckle over the
self-evident proposition that there is more law for
the rich than for the poor.
Somebody is looking for trouble in emula-
ting the thriftiness of that Bluenose skipper who
lately had much argument with his crew, and
was shown to have sailed his ship without lights
so as to save kerosene. The other night the
Peregrine passed a full-rigged ship, churnirg
along outside the Barrier reef without a single
light showing fore or aft. That made the fourth
ship so reported lately. The punishment for that
sort of thing ought to be the drowning of at least
one of the owners—with Costs.

if n Varraville (Vic.) “ temperance ” meeting, a

The carter with the horny hand,
The leader of the German band,
All sorts o’ chaps —why do they stand around in cursing
bunches?
Ah, why ? The horn o’ plenty ’ll pour
Corn’-beef and sanwidges no more—
They’re waging now a holy war against free counter*
lunches!
O, this is funny, more’n most—
It soon will be the good man’s boast
That Bill is off anchovy toast; it’s only beer be crunches
These joyful days ! Ber-ruther, shake !
And let us have, for goodness’ sake,
“ Temp’rance ” in everything we take —including counter
lunches !
At lunch, wild flies arrest their flight
On lettuces with Chinese blight,
And foreign bodies flop and fight. . . . The warm crowd
simply munches,
No matter what queer things appear !
I must abjure the pony beer
I used to drink at times—for fear it leads to counter-
lunches !
No jokin’, now T —it’s serious, chaps !
Good gosh, I’m nearly starvin’ —p’r’aps
it’s joy for some, if we collapse without our humble
hunches
O’ food —our snacks o’ meat and bread !
Free victuals ? Well —one must be fed !
We’ll pay —if we can get instead free, long beers with our
lunches 1
VIC.
FRANK HENTS.
MADRIGAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What needs it then we stand so long- a-gazing,
And do not our lips mingle,
Since our hearts, so long single,
Have married, as if in a dream amazing?
Our lips in such a joy should follow suit,
And on each other feed as on Love’s fruit.
N.S.W.
R. CRAWFORD.
THE PURSUIT OF PEACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Proceeding, the Kaiser said he hoped that history
would acknowledge that in his foreign policy he had
unswervingly pursued peace. —Edifying cable item.
In ancient days on the Attic shore,
And where the waves round Lemnos roar.
They grew the olive and curling vine
To the glory of Greece and the Argive line ;
And the glad earth yielded her increase
To the Kaee that shone in the arts of peace.
High thought, high art, high aims were born
In the Islands under the Golden Horn—
High eloquence and elegance ;
With the silver lyre went the graceful dance,
And after the dance the joyous feast—
The worship of Beauty never ceased.
They followed Peace and followed Joy,
The Race that had conquered sacred Troy ;
All the pleasant arts beneath the sun
They came to perfection one by one
In the halcyon days of Immortal Greece,
And life was spent in pursuit of Peace.
Alas ! it passed, that Age of Gold,
And now, when the world is getting old,
Although we follow Peace to-day
We do the thing in a different way—
Our rulers hasten in her tracks
In a battleship, with a battle-axe!
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.
