The WILD CAT
COLUMN
For pretty well three years Sydney has been
watching the old softgoods and shipping firm of W. and
A. McArthur, Ltd., and wondering whether it could pull
through. The marvellous recovery made by Australia
after the drought set one or two other rocky concerns on
their feet; but the McArthurs had plunged too heavily in
S Africa to reap the same advantage ; moreover, though
there has been profit in most things in Australia during
the last few years, this co. managed to strike some of the
few duffers. The co. was formed in 1898 to take over the
business of the old firm, which had then been running
57 years. The price was £300,000, half in fully-paid
shares. Altogether, the public has put up £175,000 for
preference shares; and after the first lot of ordi-
naries (15,000 at £10) were issued, 10,000 more were
sent out, £1 paid. As the ascertained losses amount to
about £235,000, all the ordinary capital is to be
wiped out, and 7s. 6d. in the £ of the pref. capital.
Creditors are to be paid partly in 5j per cent, preference
shares in a new co. to take over the warehouse business,
which is said to be profitable. There is a liability of
£90,000 on the last issue of ordinary shares ; but the
receiver, according to the cables, is “ unable to hold out
any hope that they will realise anything from the un-
called capital.” That is a matter that ought to be
probed. People are encouraged to give credit to concerns
on the strength of uncalled capital ; yet again and again
creditors have been compelled to suffer because no ade-
quate attempt has been made to recover this capital.
People are shy of giving credit to no-liability concerns
because they know that the shareholders 5 liability
for uncalled capital is almost wholly a myth ;
and after some Anglo-Australian experiences, they
will be presently coming to the conclusion that
the liability in a “ limited ” co. is to be relied upon
just about as little as if it were the other sort of co. In
the McArthur case a meeting of shareholders in London
has adopted the proposed scheme of reconstruction. The
scheme is really the liquidation of the old co., the
forming of a new co. to take over the warehouse business,
and the winding-up of the other disastrous part of the
concern as soon as possible.
* * * *
When the Sandford iron people appealed to the
N.S.W. State Government, it was proposed (and the
Commercial Bank of Sydney agreed) that the State
should have a first mortgage to the extent of £25,000,
but that, as to the other £45,000, it should rank
on ev;en terms with the bank. The bank is a
creditor for about £130,000. The Assembly, how-
ever, said that the State should have a first
mortgage for the whole £70,000. Of course, the bank
wouldn’t consider such a thing ; if somebody was to have
a first bite to the extent of £70,000, the bank itself might
just as well be the party to have that first bite. Parlia-
ment, in fact, made a monumental ass of itself; it
spent two long days deciding upon terms which
any banker could have said had not a hope of
being accepted; and when it made an offer
which any bank would have been prepared to make, it
hemmed it about with conditions—the appointment, for
instance, of a director with power of veto—which no
banker w«uld have had the effrontery to suggest. It
offered help, in short, to the indispensable industry on
ludicrous terms ; and it posed as quite a public-spirited
benefactor while it arranged those terms. The bank
thereupon took possession, putting accountant Borchard
in charge ; and only the blast furnace is now at work.
Negotiations with Premier Wade are understood to be
still in progress.

Considering its limited opportunities this bank is still
making very good profits indeed. Progress in Tasmania
is, however, wretchedly slow : only about 30,000 have been
added to the population in the past 16 years, and pro-
bably two-thirds of that increase has been due to the
opening of the West Coast mines, for which outsiders
were mainly responsible. As to agricultural and manufac-
turing population, the increase has been inuclT less than
the “ natural ” increase; and a bank which has to battle
along under such handicaps can’t very well escape stag-
nation. That profits in the past two or three years have
shown a great improvement is mainly due to the wonder-
ful metal and wool prices ; but the bloom is off the
unstable metal markets now, and already the profits fall
away. The bank’s detailed figures, however, still show
no change for the worse :
Nov., ’O6. Nov., ’O7.
Notes in circulation.. £63,167 £66,089 Inc. £2 922
Bills, etc 8,784 14,908 Inc. 6,’124
Deposits at interest.. 245,624 250,382 Inc. 4 758
Free deposits.. .. 287,252 314,542 lne. 27’290
Liquid assets .. .. 201,651 155,173 Dec. 46,478
Advances .. .. 567,775 662,281 Inc. 94,506
Premises, etc 27,932 28,268 Inc. 336
The advances are unusually heavy, wherefore liquid
assets now represent only 24 per cent, of the debt to the '
public. Nobody knows very much about the current ac-
count (free), money except the people who are inside a
bank. This free money is sometimes of as much use to a
bank, no doubt, as fixed deposits—it depends upon the
depositor. Yet, at the best, it does seem as if this
bank were taking very considerable risks, the proportion
of current account money being large and the proportion
of liquid assets very small. Assuming that the advances
are justified, what the bank needs is more fixed-deposit
money ; it could very well stand some more, because at
present it has £1 of shareholders’ funds to every £3 7s. of
the public’s money, and, with reasonably sound manage-
ment, the public is justified in lending a bank a consider-
ably larger proportion than that. As for the small holding
of liquid assets, no doubt it is much more profitable to
lend money at 6 or 7 per cent, than to keep it in the
vaults ; but there must be limits to that sort of thing.
And very serious things have happened through those
limits not being recognised.
On the basis of total shareholders’ funds the
profits have lately shown the following return

The co. formally writes £30,000 off freehold and leasehold
premises this year. Something may have been written
off in previous years—it certainly should have been—and
this £30,000 may be in addition to the ordinary writing
off. If, however, something- has been written off in pre-
vious years and charged direct to profit and loss, and
nothing except this £30,000 has been written off this
year, then it would not be fair to compare the disclosed
profits for this year with those for former years. As a
matter of fact, in connection with a business of this sort,
which deals in properties and securities liable to violent
fluctuations, one can’t judge the figures without also
judging- the management. Take these following figures
for example:—
1897. 1906. 1907.
Perpet. 4J debentures.. £620,000 £620,000 £620,000
~4 „ .. 1,643,210 1,643,210 1,643,210
Termin. 4 „ .. 279,070 468,380 431,080
„ 3J „ .. 15,500 Nil Nil

Premises, etc 219,809 614,045 728,894
The advances, almost all no doubt on the security of
pastoral properties and rural products, total over 5£
millions ; and nearly half a million of that has been
advanced during the past 12 months. In transactions of
that size, in connection with such an erratic industry,
obviously a vast deal depends upon the amount of current
“ profit ” used to write down assets ; in other words, set
aside internally to provide for probable future losses—so
highly probable as to be practically certain. Well, what has
the reputation of Dalgety’s management been, so far as
this matter goes? About the best available test is the
dividends paid during bad times : only by making large
provision in good times, can a co. honestly pay divs. in
bad ones. This co. started in 1884 ; it began with 7 p.c.,
soon advanced the div. to 8 p.c., and was still paying that
8 p.c. when the first list printed above opened ; and some
of the intervening years had been shockingly bad from a
pastoral point of view. In the very bad years of 1903-4,
the div. got down no lower than 5 p.c. For a concern of
this kind, that looks like very prudent management in
the past. There is absolutely no way to tell what it is in
the present; all that can be said is that the personnel
has undergone very little change. This year, as last,
£5OOO has been deducted from profits and put to the
credit of a staff benevolent fund.

This little office’s connection is largely marine (it does no
life business, by the way, in spite of its title) ; and marine
losses have been heavy all round during the year. Con-
sequently results are only middling
(he- Ex- Loss Expense

Considering what a little office it is, the expense rate is
unusually low. The loss rate figures are erratic—neces-
sarily, on account of the smallness of the business : one
or two claims more or less make all the difference. For
this reason it isn’t much use taking notice of a single
year’s profit figures. In fact, the surplus of revenue over
outgoings in any fire office is only profit in a
loose lhanner of speaking. This co.’s position is re-
markably strong, taking the size of its risks (as judged
by premiums) into account. Of capital and reserves to-
gether there is £591 for every £lOO of annual premiums ;
while for 1906 the average for 55 British offices, tariff and
non-tariff, was under £155 for every £lOO of premiums.
The fact is that in proportion to risks undertaken the
Australian insurance office almost invariably puts up a
vastly greater amount of capital than the British office
has paid up; and that of course means that the
Australian office offers greater protection for the man
who insures. What this co.’s investments are earning
can’t be said positively, because it lumps fixed deposits,
which are investments, with agency balances, etc.,
which are not investments. This sort of lumping
ought to be dropped. The bulk of the assets are
mortgages, which represent £85,452. No doubt they
yield a good return ; but they are not the sort of quickly-
realised security that a fire insurance co. ought to load
up with.

1904 .. 32,390 4 „ „ 23,400 16,000 178,810

1907 .. 69,342 5 ~ ~ 29,250 14,500 146,677
The book-keeping methods of this co. have undergone
considerable changes. For many years, down to 1901,
the proceeds of the sale of timber, after paying working
expenses, were treated as profit, and nothing was written
off the value of the assets. In other words, no fund was
accumulated out of profits to take the place of the assets
that had been chopped down and sold ; so, to make the
balance-sheet balance, and on account of a provision in
the Victorian Companies Act, this item had to appear in
1901: “ Accumulated assets depreciation, £264,920/’ in
addition to a paper asset, “Debenture expenses account,
£16,037” —total, £280,957. Against that there were
reserves and undivided balances totalling £95,717, leaving
a visible deficit of £185,240 at the end of 1901. as shown
above. Since then an effort has been made to get rid of
some of this paper stuffing by replacing it with un-
divided profits; and the paper has been reduced to
£219,920, against which there are reserves of £73,243,
leaving the present deficit at £146,677. Meantime, the
current destruction of timber is being more or less pro-
vided for. Apparently nothing was written off on this
account in the year 1905-6, but last vear £5OOO was pro-
vided ; the other £9500 (making up £14,500 shown as
written off) was for depreciation of real estate, including
mills. The movement can be put in another way
1901. 1906. 1907.
Liabilities (me. debentures) £327,610 £195,191 £129,387
Liquid assets 148,342 143,061 177,437
Deficit £179,268 £52,130
Surplus _ _ £48,050
Thus what may be called the cash position has improved
to the extent of £lOO,lBO during the year. Obviously
this has not been through profits (the figures, by the
way, are taken as they stand in the balance-sheets, before
the payment of dividends). A large part of the explana-
tion lies in a reduction of standing timber, stocks, etc.
from £439,458 to £382,509 ; there have been heavy realisa-
tions of stock or sales of other assets. Since the close of
the year a further £30,000 worth of debentures have
been paid off ; and this lightening of the interest bill will
be a relief in dull times. As to the big “ assets deprecia-
tion account” mentioned above, the directors say that
kauri forests have gone up in value, wherefore “a careful
revaluation is now in progress,” apparently with the
object of writiug-up the remaining assets and thus
wiping out some of the deficit. But the shareholder or
investor, before he accepts any such revaluation and
writing-up, ought anyhow to satisfy himself that the
spring in value is permanent, and that the new
Australian timber duty was taken into account
when it was made. The capital is £585,000;
150,000 shares are paid to 335. and 450,000 are paid to
15s. ; 7s. per share was written off in 189 S. Prior to that
year the co. had for some time paid no dividend, owing
mainly to the heavy debenture debt (over £300,000), re-
presented mainly by trees which weren’t spinning-any
visible profits to help ray the interest.

As shown in the table, a large amount of capital was
written off in 1903, when tw'o £1 shares were issued to
replace each £5 share. The properties were written
down to match ; and steady realisations have since taken
place. Hitherto no profit made on these realisations has
been taken into account; the transactions have no doubt
been on a time-payment basis, and, to cover the risks
that necessarily hang- on to the tail of that plan, as well
as to provide for further possible shrinkages, the surpluses
have been used as an internal reserve. Now-, however,
the year’s profit on realisations is shown ; the directors
explain that, since the writing down, the properties that
have been sold have averaged 7 per cent, above the
reduced values, so they conclude that the need
for special provision to cover probable losses doesn’t
exist. But, though disclosed reserves will now
have a chance to mount up, there can be no dividend for
shareholders yet a while. The society held a good
deal of Scotch money; and in 1899, when a re-
arrangement became necessary, the old creditors accepted
debentures, half carrying 5 per cent, and half 4 per cent.
The 5 percent, debentures have now been paid off ; but
£26,616 is still owed on account of “B” (4 per cent.)
debentures, and under the rearrangement no dividend
can be paid till all this debt is wiped out. In four years
£18,176 of “ A ” debentures have been paid off ; mean-
time, however, debts apart from the debentures have
been increased from £28,169 to £33,66o—mostly over-
drafts and deposits. The greater part of that newly-
borrowed money has, however, gone into the society’s
Collins-street freehold. But it may not be safe to assume
that the realisations will go on as satisfactorily. Seasons
are not likely to continue as good as the last three or
four, so sales will probably ease off ; and then, as the
properties age, upkeep will increase. Still, as far as
things have gone, shareholders ought in the end to get
something substantial out of the wreck. Judging by the
fact that properties when sold bring more than book
values, it might be assumed that they have been written
down low enough. Yet, reckoning the debentures in as
capital, the return, after paying interest on the com-
paratively small deposits, etc., was only about 2| per
cent, last year ; and that is low enough to raise some
doubts.

The Coastal Farmers’ Co-operative Society,
Ltd. (Sydney)
Net Bonus to To Re- Re-

1907 .. 3731 3731 4631
This purely co-operative society makes its distribution at
the end of its financial year, in March. The business is
still growing, and the profits (to be shared by the people
who supply the business) grow with it. The half-year’s
turnover was £272,414. The capital is only £8587.
Almost of necessity, therefore, the book-debts ap-
pear very large in proportion to capital. Yet,
though they amount to £33,045, they really represent
only about two-thirds of a month’s sales ; consequently,
the risks are probably not what the size of the book-
debts would suggest. Still there must be some risks, and
with the growth of the turnover (and consequently of
the book-debts and the risk of bad debts) there ought to
be an increase of reserves. Private Enterprise in this
business appears, however, to fight the co-operative en-
terprises with all sorts of weapons, and many of the men
who would gain from co-operation in the long run—who
would save for themselves the profits that Private Enter-
prise has hitherto pouched—can only be kept loyal by
stretching the bonuses to the last possible penny.. Later
on they may get educated up to a better understanding.
Meantime (still on this question of book-debts and risks
and reserves) the co. has this in its favor : that of its
capital of £8587 only £599 is sunk in fixed assets, such as
store, plant, etc.
* * * *
The lead market has been doing the same sort
of gymnastics that the copper market was guilty of a
while ago ; there was a fall of 225. 6d. (down to £l4 10s.)
in one day. Thousands of people were lately inveigled
into Barrier stocks when lead was up at £2O, and as they
have that figure stamped on their minds, they naturally
think that £l4 is surely about the worst the lead
market can do. Unfortunately it is nothing of the sort.
Here, half-year by half-year, is what the market averaged
after the 1900 boom :
Sept., 1900 .. £l7 6 9 Mar., 1904 .. £ll 8 9
Mar., 1901 .. 15 1 11 Sept., 1904 .. 11 15 8
Sept., 1901 .. 12 3 3 Mar., 1905 .. 12 11 6
Mar., 1902 .. 11 4 6 Sept., 1905 .. 13 8 0
Sept., 1902 .. 11 5 9 Mar., 1906 .. 16 0 3
Mar., 1903 .. 11 8 1 Sept., 1906 .. 16 17 9
Sept., 1903 .. 11 11 10 Mar., 1907 .. 19 12 10
It is true that the average level of prices is higher now
than it was a few years ago, whether in consequence of
increased gold production or something else doesn’t
matter. Therefore lead will probably not fall now to the
depths to which it used to descend. Which is a mighty
good thing for even the best of the Broken Hill mines. In
1894—the bottom of the slump which followed the mild
boom of 1888-1890—the average for lead was £9 11s. 6d. ;
and, with the high returning charges which now prevail,
that price would shut up every mine but two or three
on the Barrier.
* * * *
When lead tumbled to £l4 10s. the other day
S. M. Herald printed a comparison showing how Broken
Hill stocks had come back compared with prices as at
December, 1906. That was a very useful comparison, only
it ought to have included another column giving prices
as at December, 1903, just to show what these stocks can
get down to when a bad market hits them. Here is the
Herald’s list, with the other column that should have

The prices in the third column lasted, with no great
variations, for about a couple of years.
Curses come home to roost again. Years ago
the Broken Hill eos. had an agreement with the miners
which was terminated without warning just when it
suited the cos. to terminate it. Since then the miners,
whenever they fix things up with the owners, stipulate
for an agreement running a stated time. In November
last year, an agreement was made about wages. A
sliding scale was suggested ; but the miners pressed for
what meant a rise of about 12 per cent, for two years
certain ; and as the lead market was then booming, the
owners gave it. Now' lead has slumped, and the owners
are face to face with a high-wage agreement which won’t
run out till the end of 1908. It looks, however, as though
some of them will either have to sue for mercy or shut up
shop long before then.
Jfr ■Jr Jfr Jfr
In 1902, when lead slumped to £lO 2s. 6d., the
mines on the Barrier which kept on working had to pick
the best ore out, and some of them were then making
only Is. a ton profit. To-day, in order to place those
mines in a similar position, lead need fall no lower than
£l3. The difference is brought about by the increased
returning charges, which next year will be £5 ss. to £5
10s. per ton of concentrates instead of £4 ss. ; and the
higher wages and other costs will account for the balance.
* * * *
It is just about certain that, unless lead prices
harden again, Broken Hill Block 14 won’t turn the mill
wheels after Christmas. Up to the time of the fire the
mill was running only two shifts daily, and it is pretty
evident that not more than eighteenpence a ton was
being made out of the sulphides mined, even at the good
metal prices then ruling.
* * * *
South Blocks are struck hard ; but a big slice
will be taken off working costs by the stoppage of develop-
ment W'ork at Block 5 shaft. Still, even so, if things don’t
improve in the metal markets, this mine may take a very
long Christmas holiday. It is almost wholly dependent
on the lead market ; its silver production amounts to
next to nothing.
» * * *
Broken Hill Junction North is finding the drop
in lead inconvenient. Already hands have been short-
ened underground, and the mill is taking things easy.
The mine has this advantage : the stopes are now well
opened out, and manager Palmer could keep on hauling
for three shifts a day if required. The character of the
ore will, however, make low working costs difficult.
«• » » *
It didn’t take a very big wave of depression to
swamp the Broken Hill Junction. Bad steering was not
so much to blame as the fact that recently the co. got
into shoal waters. The 900 ft. level, upon which hopes
had been built, failed in the time of greatest need.
Rhodonite appeared in the north end, towards the Junc-
tion North boundary, and the south end pinched and
made again, flickered for a while, and then almost went
out. Then there was a lot of poor ore showing in the
other levels, and stoping was so unprofitable to con-
tractors that it was difficult to induce them to undertake
the job. But those are only temporary difficulties, and
should disappear if development work were further
advanced. When Browne’s shaft reaches the 1000 ft.
level, and King’s shaft is down below’ 400 ft., and the price
of lead is high enough to provide sufficient water, the
Junction will float again. It is a habit it has got into.
• * * *
The Sulphide Corporation’s pref. shareholders
get 10 per cent, again for the year, and ordinaries once
more get a shock. In 1900 the ordinaries got 15 percent,
(on 155.) ; since then, nothing at all. There are 550,000
shares of each sort, originally £1 paid ; but an inadequate
ss. was written off the ordinaries on the failure of the
Ashcroft process, which the co. was formed originally to
work. It did look as though the ordinaries would get a
taste this year, on account of the high price of metals.
It only takes £55,000 to pay a 10 per cent. pref. div. ; and
the profits in the 12 months to June, 1906, were
£136,364, though lead in that year averaged only £l5 11s.,
while for the following two half-y'ears the averages were
£lB 7s. 2d. and £l9 17s. Id. The Sulphide Corporation
has, however, about £79,000 of first debenture stock
maturing at the end of this year; and very likely it is ■
preparing to repay that without further borrowing,
intending possibly to write down works, etc., to corres-
pond. As mentioned above, the sum written off capital
originally, after the failure of the Ashcroft process, was
utterly inadequate ; and shareholders have had to pay
since for the old lack of courage. For the assets had gone,
whether the co. was prepared to acknowledge it or not.
* * * -K
One of the few bright spots in Broken Hill
mining affairs at present is in the north drive at the 800 ft.
level of the British, between Howell’s and Thompson’s
shafts. At first this drive was thought to be skirting the
eastern vein ; but when it plunged into the heart of an
ore body, that theory was queried. For 25ft. or 30ft. the
drive has been in what would be first-rate ore if it were
not cursed with so much rhodonite. As it is, the ore is
much better than that developed in the eastern
vein by the north drive from Blackwood’s crosscut.
In the face, overhead, underfoot, and on either
side the ore is showing well; but whether the drive is
crossing the body at an angle or following the strike, has
yet to be determined, so nothing can be said about its
size. When the drive is finished, steps will be taken to
ascertain whether the ore belongs to the eastern vein or
is an independent shoot. In any case, the development
adds something solid to the ore reserves.
* * * *
One of the humorous features of the daily papers
is the country correspondent who, after reporting the
big pumpkin and the accident to Muldoon’s cow, finds
himself suddenly up against a prospector with a tale of a
marvellous local discovery. The S. M. Herald lent its
ear to one of him last week, and had a beautiful yarn
poured into it. “Samples sent for assay have returned
140 oz. silver to the ton, with 50 per cent, lead, 33 per
cent, zinc, and 17 per cent, copper.” In other words, the
stone was 100 per cent, lead, zinc, and copper, so Heaven
only know's how the 140 oz. silver found room to crowd in.
“ There are vast quantities of this stone in sight,” says
the S. M. Herald. Hooray !
* * * *
After writing off 10 per cent, for depreciation
of plant and works that have been charged up to capital
account, Mt. David (N.S. W.) gave a profit of £2419 for the
year, which ended on October 31. Liquid assets at that
date, including stores, amounted to £2016 ; liabilities,
£146; leaving a surplus of £lB7O. Complaint has been
made in a vague way that excessive administration costs
eat up any profit this mine is likely’ to make. As usual,
however, the working expenses account sets out the items
in great detail—Directors’ fees, £260 ; secretary, £156 ;
travelling and escort expenses, £5B ; mine rent, etc., £lB3.
As the accounts are for a year, the figures are by no means
staggering. No estimate of ore is made —in fact, none of
much value is possible—but the manager is justified in
saying that prospects have greatly improved, and he
adds that crushings during the current year are “more
than likely to amply recoup shareholders for previous
disappointments.” The present co., a reconstruction of
the co. formed in 1901, which in its turn was a recon-
struction of an old co., is in 120,000 10s. shares, on which
only 3Jd. is uncalled.

The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by W. & J. Lempriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleicherb & Co., of London, the largest firm in the world
for the manufacture of transport appliances.*

One set of Improved Plunger Pumps, Cornish
type, is at present being constructed for the Berry United
Gold Mines, to the order of Messrs. Bewick, Moreing and
Company. The set will consist of 3 lifts, each of 200 ft.,
the total lift being 600 ft. The Plungers are 26in. in
diameter and 10ft. stroke, and the discharge 2,500,000
gallons per day. The valve boxes are made in separate
pieces, instead of forming part of one H piece, and the
valves are of the compound flap type. The total weight
is 110 tons. The Austral Otis Engineering Company have
this work in hand. This Company has constructed and
erected most of the big volume, deep alluvial plants now
working in Victoria."

The old Mt. Hope (N.S.W.) has been resur-
rected, mostly by Coonanible people, who early last year
formed themselves first into a syndicate, and then into a
co. (capital £lO,OOO, in 20,000 shares, of which 15,000 are
fully paid, and the other 5000 are paid to 45.). The accounts,
for nearly 18 months, to the end of August, are only just
issued; but how things will pan out, in view of the
slump in copper that happened after that date, isn’t yet
certain. As the accounts stand, the syndicate and the
co. show a profit of £3134, after paying £1975 for new plant
out of proceeds of sales ; but it is possible that the co.
will find it has overdrawn against shipments. Mt. Hope
has a big ore body, but most of the ore needs concentra-
ting, and, partly because the mine is miles at the back of
nowhere, costs are-heavy.
7C * * *
Things are a good deal unsettled at Newcastle.
Last week it looked as though all the arrangements
which ended the recent strike would break down on
account of the Hebburn difficulty ; but, after some delay,
the miners agreed to work the electric coal-cutting
machines. There is, nevertheless, a feeling of unrest
among the men, and on the other side there is a feeling
that the men really want more than they say they do
(that explains why so much stress is laid upon the re-
sumption of work with coal-cutting machines at Heb-
burn) ; so the whole situation will still have to be handled
very delicately to avoid trouble.
* -a * *
Nymagee copper mine (N.S.W.) has had a
very brief run under its new English owners ; word has
already arrived to shut down. The property was only
this year taken over from the Great Oobar people. Costs
have always been heavy at this remote mine.
Queen Bee (N.S.W.) had used up all its fresh
water, and practically every other outside mine in the
Cobar district was in the same trouble, when heavy
thunderstorms arrived at the beginning of this week.
The rainfall will relieve the anxiety at Great Cobar,
where preparations had been made for a famine water-
supply ; but it will wipe out an excuse that some of the
outside shows badly wanted for closing down.
* * * *
As expected six months ago, the value of the
Westralian gold production for 1907 will be half a million
less than that for 1906. The total output since 1886 to
the end of November this year was £19,606,598,
valued at £77,349,406. Dividends have t0ta11ed£17,244,321,
of which £1,507,156 was paid between January and
November 30 of this year. The December distribution,
so far, reaches £130,000.
* * * »
Golden Horseshoe Co. (W.A.) has purchased
the adjoining Ivanhoe South Extended for £35,000. How
this value was arrived at no one seems to know ; and, as
the sale has not caused any disturbance, no one appa-
rently cares. For some years this property was worked
in a half-hearted way, but on the strength of the drill
cutting 4ft. of 35dwt. ore at 1920 ft. from the surface, the
main shaft was recently taken down to 2000 ft. So
far as can be gathered, results from development
work at this point have proved disappointing.
This, coupled with the fact that not a single ton of
payable ore has been raised, makes the mine look dear at
the figure quoted. Still the possibility of one or more of
the Ivanhoe lodes underlaying into the property at depth
gives it some speculative value. It is intended to test
the Horseshoe lodes by means of a long crosscut from the
Extended’s 2000 ft. workings, and this will rank as one of
the most interesting and important pieces of deep
prospecting carried out within the borders of the Golden
Mile.
# * * #
South Kalgurli’s • estimated ore reserves show
little alteration compared with what they were a year
ago. Then the figures stood at 112,060 tons of 7Jdwt.
ore ; now they are given as 111,039 tons, worth 7jdwt.,
with 73,527 tons of “ probable ore,” assaying 6fdwt. This
looks all right on paper ; but, judging by recent returns
from this tricky mine, it seems as if mill returns are going
to fall short by a dwt. or so per ton of what the sampling
results led the management to expect. As the ore now
going through the mill is harder, more expensive to
treat, and lower-grade than that previously handled,
shareholders had better prepare themselves for increased
costs and smaller profits.
* * « *
The financial position of the Golden Ridge Co.
(W.A.) is getting sounder : £2OOO is now lying at interest,
the bullion reserve for equalising monthly outputs stands
at £3500, while, after paying the forthcoming dividend of
2s. 6d. per share, the co.’s current account should be
£2OOO or so in credit. Next year, the profit from ore
treatment should exceed £25,000 —equal to 16s. per share.
At the recent half-yearly meeting of shareholders, a pro-
posal was brought forward to split the shares into 10s. or
ss. scrip, and open an Adelaide office ; but as to both pro-
posals it was decided to wait a while.

The Ivanhoe Junction crowd isat it again. This
time shareholders are informed, per medium of a rambling
circular, that while mill results have proved the mine to
be worthless in the top levels, good assays, ranging from
lOdwt. to 35dwt., have been obtained at a depth of 340 ft.
Something is also said about the development of payable
ore at this point giving new life to the property, and for-
feiture of shares is threatened unless the current call is
paid. So far, some £60,000 has been squandered on this
life-like image of a wild cat. Any more money that
may be raised seems to have no better chances.
* * * *
Ivanhoe Junction’s request to the Westralian
Government for a subsidy to sink another 100 ft. ranks as
about the cheekiest of its kind. It is not so long since
this mine was alleged to have 9000 tons of 15dwt. ore,
besides vast amounts of 9dwt. stuff in sight; yet the ore
passed through the mill only averaged about 6gr. per ton.
Local papers, which were booming this failure when The
Bulletin wasn’t, now profess astonishment that anybody
should have wasted time and money in breaking, raising,
and treating stuff worth less than Is. per ton. '•

During its,financial year Burbank’s Main Lode
(W.A.) made a profit of £14,030; but after writing off
£9704, representing the whole of the cost of development
to June 30th, 1906, and after allowing for London
expenses and for plant depreciation, etc., there remains
only £2069, which with the amount brought forward gives
a disposable balance of £6257. Out of this sum a
10 per cent, dividend, absorbing £3OOO, has been declared.
Ore reserves, which now stand at 24,000 tons, show a sub-
stantial increase compared with what was in sight twelve
months ago, and big type is used to emphasise this fact
in the report. So far, the property has been opened up
to a vertical depth of 480 ft.; but as the shoot is 700 ft.
long at that depth, and as it looks well in winzes sunk
below, the proposed new level at 600 ft. has decidedly pro-
mising prospects. A fact to be remembered, though, is that
the reef went wrong at or about that depth in the neigh-
boring Burbank’s Birthday Gift leases.
v ** * *
When the accounts presented at the half-yearly
meeting (November 11) were made up, the Phillips River
Gold and Copper Co. (W.A.) had a credit balance of
£46,210, which it was decided to carryforward. In arriving
at this result, the directors valued stocks of ores and
matte and shares in other companies at “ present market
quotations ” ; and there has probably been a fall since
then, in which case the profits are less than the accounts
allege. Additional plant is on order, and general manager
Klug is at present in Broken Hill, making tests with the
Elmore oil process on 2 and 3 per cent, copper ore.
State mining-engineer Montgomery (W.A.)
thinks well of Ravensthorpe prospects. He tells the Minis-
ter for Mines that as they go down the shoots get larger
and often longer, and that the present depression is due
mainly to the restriction of operations because the owners
expect better working conditions when the railway is
completed and smelting works are-improved.
• * * •
Some excitement has been (caused in Westralia
because about 30cwt. of Mulgabbie ore has given 600 oz.
of smelted gold and 400 oz. of telluride and gold specimens.
One lot of 300 oz. of almost-pure gold, taken from Cable
and Kelly’s claim, has been purchased by the Government
for show at the Anglo-French Exhibition. Mulgabbie is
situated near liurnalpi, and is the only place outside
Kalgoorlie where telluride has been discovered in W.A.
The metal, however, only occurs in dabs, and, like the
rich patches of gold, takes a lot of finding.
* * * #
The Lady Miller, Norseman (W.A.), is appa-
rently in trouble. This is a cast-off of the once-boomed
Lady Mary Co. Lately there was a crushing, since which
all has been silence. And the West always knows what
that means.
• * * *
The Mararoa reserves (W.A.) are largely hypo-
thetical, owing to only a small percentage of the so-
called ore in sight being blocked out and ready for
stoping. This means that a lot of development work has
yet to be done before the official estimate given recently
is justified. It must be borne in mind, however, that ore
worth 70s. per ton is showing in the bottom level; and,
although the known length of the shoot in the workings
above is only about 240 ft., the outlook for the Mararoa
turning out a small but first-rate mine at depth is very
promising, contrary to all that past experience of the
field might have led one to expect.

Another returned prospector’s opinion of Mool-
yella tinfield (W.A.): —“ Moolyella may have been fairly
good for tin-getters while the late sky-high price pre-
vailed, but the few (about 100) men who have not cleared
out now make little more than food.”
• * * *
The long-neglected Two Mile end of Gvmpie
(Q.) is on the eve of developments. Great Northern, the
mother property, has been developing the Empire Reef
during the last couple of months, and has got out 100
tons of quartz for a trial crushing. There looks to be a
safe ounce per ton in it. Considering that there are not
as yet any stopes on the reef, and the reef has only been
opened 40ft., an ounce would be very satisfactory, for a
start. It is only 14 months since work began." Great
Western, adjoining the Northern, and floated in Sydney
last August, has a shaft down over 70ft., and winding
machinery is now going up. This property, of 25 acres,
floated at 6d. (48,000 half-crown contributing shares and
12,000 half-crown shares fully paid up), has now a market
valuation of about 2s. 6d. a share. With five or six reefs,
and slates at a shallow depth, its prospects are bright.
Great Northern Extended, east of the parent mine, is
down some 240 ft. Unfortunately, it has struck heavy
water, but the engine is beating it; sinking, though
under more expensive conditions, is being continued.
Slates in this mine lie at a depth somewhere near 1000 ft.
The Two Miie, a new co., is re-testing a previously tried
area. With a start of some 680 ft., it now has a shaft
845 ft. deep. The position is close up a mile north of the
Great Northern. At a depth of 730 ft., it proved a band
of good gold-bearing country, called locally the First
Bed. On Nov. 30 the manager reported cutting good
slate and plumbago at 842 ft. This represents the Second
Bed. The importance about this co.’s work is that it
brings that end of the Two Mile area right in line with
the rest of the field, in so far as the position of the slate
beds is concerned. The Peter and Paul reef, when pros-
pected in these lower beds of slate, should give payable
results. A 25-acre lease to the south of this property,
called the Peter and Paul, includes the reef of the same
name ; pot holes 70ft. deep on the reef gave lioz. quartz
in 1899.
* * * *
Mt. Morgan (Q.) has refused to exercise the
option over Peak Downs, so that very misguided concern
is now in a hopeless mess ; and the worth of the tales
told concerning the mines resources, and its slag
dump, and its thousands of tons of basic ore lying at
grass, ought now to be clear to shareholders. The
Bulletin was officially slated because it dared
to expose the true position at Peak Downs,
while a crowd was doing its best to get the
public to take shares at high prices. Fortunately
one section of the public was warned off ; but another
section went in, and now finds that Mt. Morgan will not
even take over the mine as a flux quarry. Yet the co.
has provided itself with a smelting plant in the wilder-
ness, and a fine, fat overdraft in the city. It
is not likely anybody will shift the furnace, but the bank
will want the overdraft shifted. Where the money is to
come from is a mystery, so far. Had the money which
has been spent foolishly on smelting equipment been put
into the mine, something might have been found good
enough to influence people like Mt. Morgan to take an
interest in the concern. As it is, the gamble at Peak
Downs could only be attempted by a co. which could get
the property for nil.
* * * •
“A Shareholder”: It is true the capitalists
who had an option over certain Peak Downs (Q.) shares
did not take them up ; but it could not be on account of
size or quality of the lode, as the assays are not yet com-
pleted, and no report has been issued. It is understood
that the assays made by Mt. Morgan practically con-
firm those made by Mr. Rands in 1899. As for size
writer saw the lode at the 270 ft. level, continuous for
600 ft. in the drive, varying from 2ft. to 10ft. in width.
Underneath, there is a winze 110 ft. deep on the underlay
showing the lode to be 6ft. wide at bottom. The Peak
Downs ore, although rich in iron, contains in the upper
levels a certain amount of silica, of which Mount Morgan
has a superabundance. But if the big co. is not prepared
to take the crude ore, it is very likely that it will be
glad to buy:the Peak Downs matte for the iron it con-
tains, and that would suit the Peak Downs Co. best of all.
* * * *
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers, shows a
1 old front to a dejected share market. On the half year’s
output of 23,450 tons, it made a profit of £5032 ; but
practically all of this was paid away for plant and in
other capital expenditure, so there has been no chance to
reduce the overdraft. Costs were 355. 9d., but the
new plant is expected to lower that. The manager
reckons cyaniding can be done for 3s. 9d. and milling
for 4s. 9d., and that the 255. it costs to mine the stone
will be “reduced in proportion.” It is tantalising for
the Extended to have 65,000 tons of stone dead on the
payable limit—enough to last for, say, 18 months. The
future depends, therefore, entirely on the development
of a rich shoot in the deep ground; the prospects
although capricious, are fair. But there is an overdraft
to be paid off, and the end of capital expenditure is still in
the distance.

Gilmore (North Queensland) directors soon shut
on the practice of putting up their tin to compe-
tition. In fact, they tried it only once, with the result,
it is alleged, that the shareholders gained £6O on the
aeai. ihe co. has purchased a carrying outfit from the
Endeavor and Leslie Co. (which, alas! has no more stone to
carry). The Gilmore ore will be carried, as hitherto, to the
Gurrumba mill. An offer was made lately for the ‘ ‘ dirty ”
tin, but the directors considered the price too low. Im-
purities consist of copper and sulphur ; there is no zinc
or arsenic. Wolfram, which is considered detrimental in
Australia, is, under certain conditions, an asset in Corn-
wall, -where magnetic separators are being used with
success. The Irvinebank district needs a calcining plant
and a separator, as the penalties imposed for impurities
are very heavy.
* » * *
The Chillagoe Co. still wants to send the
Etheridge railway via Charleston ; the Queensland
Government is understood to favor the Georgetown
route i and so a curious situation has arisen. The
Chillagoe Co. has to provide the money in the first place ;
the Government will no doubt <have to provide it
later on, when it takes over the concern ; and meantime
the Government guarantees some of the interest. So each
party says it is the proper party to decide the route.
The matter being hung up, the Etheridge Development
Co., which was on the stocks, has been hung up also,
though there are probably other reasons at work in
connection with that.
* » • •
20 miles of railwa V line to Mt. Molloy
(N.Q.) will be open for traffic early next year ; a shipload
of rails from England arrived lately. Sleepers are still
hard to get, particularly ironbark ; Australia has had the
importing mania, and to pay the bill it has exported
things it badly needs itself. Molloy’s smelting costs will
be reduced when the railway is through, and, with
cheaper fluxes and coke, the co. will be able to compete
for customs trade. Coke is rather dear, having to be
shipped to Cairns and railed up; but the discovery of
coal at Mt. Mulligan, near the Hodgkinson, may after a
time provide a, local supply of coke. Over-capitalisation
and the floating of wild-cat shows has made investors
sceptical about North Queensland, but a land of such big
resources is sure of a great future.
* * * *
Glad noises are again being heard from Mary-
borough (Vic.) over deep-lead shows. Once the Main
Leads, Main Leads North, Duke United, and North Duke
all flourished like the green bay tree. Now the drum can
only be beaten over the Duke and Main Leads and the Burnt
Creek, and neither of these deserves much noise. Burnt
Creek is, in fact, rarely mentioned ; but a good deal of
fuss is made over the Duke and Main Leads
and some of the two-penny-ha’-penny quartz shows. The
yarn pitched about Duke and Main Leads is that from
No. 2 shaft (whence pretty well all the gold comes) the
outskirts of the Duke lead have been entered. Wash and
mineral are said to fix the identity of the gutter. But
how does this fit in with the belief that the Main Leads
North had worked the Duke gutter at this point, and
proved it so unpayable as to lead to the shutting down of
that mine ? This is a matter one would like to hear some-
thing about from the old management of the Main Leads
North, now defunct.
* * « *
Prentice and Southern, Rutherglen, is being
rebuilt by Bewick, Moreing and Co. into a company of
200,000 shares of £l, paid to 18s. Peacock, telling how
funds have gone, says there is no desire to push out old
holders. Ha, ha 1 Quite likely 1 Everybody, from his
office-boy up, knows that no one is shouldered out of
deep leads just now. The trouble is to keep them in, so
as to get calls out of them—he he ! But really the
little bill showing how the cash has gone at the
Prentice absolves the directors and everyone else
from any thought of the kind that Peacock suggests.
The co. began with £30,000, earned £4643 from gold ;
total, £34,643. The expenses have been : Development,
£11,117 ; plant, £2813 ; stoping, £1513 ; pumping, £7487 ;
and, among other items, “ General and London,” £5368.
Therefore the co. has spent all its capital to get £4643 of
gold, and one-sixth of the total expenditure has been on
“general and London.” No, there isn’t much fear that
anybody is being pushed out. What they no doubt fear
is that the beggars won’t stop in.
* * * *
Minister McLeod has displayed the wisdom of
Solomon in the way he has handled the Burnt Creek
application for the forfeiture of the Duke Junction
claim, which is wedged into the Burnt Creek leases at
Maryborough (Vic.). Duke Junction was loafing, and Burnt
Creek wanted the ground to work from its old shaft. On
the Junction Board was one of the Burnt Creek directors,
also an M.L.A., also some Ballarat men. Altogether a
smart and strong set had to be faced. Evidently torn by
conflicting emotions, McLeod finally cut the Junction
claim into two sections. One of these he flings to the
Burnt Creek ; the other the Junction crowd has to bore or
work, or off the ground 1 goes to the Burnt Creek !
The point in it all is that if Burnt Creek pumps at No. 1
shaft, it will drain the Junction claim. Now-, will there
be any pumping?
• * * *
First Chance, Ballarat East, is well named, for
its called-up capital of £6333 has been returned, and a
bit on top. Still, the return is poor considering that it
took £68,000 of gold to do that little. Grade of stone
crushed is about 6dwt. This does not mean that all the
quartz developed will average that much. What with big
breaks and little breaks, cross-courses and slides, and big
bodies of barren quartz, it’s always uncertain whether a
crushing will pay. However, W. J. Pearce, the manager,
has the pluck to say that this half-year will see better
times. Ballarat needs them.
* * * *
At the much-boomed Electro-Hydraulic con-
cern, at Carngham (Vic.), the attempt to work the
ground by gravitation has failed dismally, and now
gravel pumps are to be given a chance to realise the
golden hopes of investors. At the next half-yearly meet-
ing it will not be surprising if some of the amateurish
persons concerned with the management should apolo-
gise for the failure of the gravel pumps, and promise that
everything will go smoothly when a battery is utilised.
The stern fact is that the ground is too hard to be profit-
ably broken up by the highest water pressure available,
and this should have been realised from the start—or
very soon afterwards.

The chairman of Mt. View (Yic.) has his
own opinions about it:— I “Re remarks (B. 5/12/’O7).
The mine has been in the hands of the present co. for the
past nine years. When the co. was formed all the work
done was a shaft sunk many years ago to 90ft. This
shaft has been cut down and continued to 350 ft. and is
now connected with a tunnel nearly 900 ft. in length. It
has been proved that a gold-bearing reef exists at tunnel
level 280 ft. ahead of present workings ; a winze sunk on
this reef, which is from 4ft. to sft. wide, disclosed payable
gold 18ft. from tunnel level. This was continued to 60ft.,
and a drive put in north 55ft. and south about 60ft. Reef
maintains its size and value the whole distance. Three
trial crushings at a local battery and one.at Footscray
clearly demonstrated to the directors that they were
fully warranted in putting up a battery and compressing
plane, which has been done ; and when water is available
crushing can be started. The winze on reef has been
sunk 120 ft., carrying payable gold the whole distance.
The shares are held by men who know the value of their
property, not as a speculation but as an investment.
There are no sellers of any moment; an offer to purchase
1000 would meet with no response. If all the mines in
Maldon strove to emulate the perseverance and pluck of
the Mt. View Co., Maldon ventures would stand in better
odor with investors.”

Peter’s Gully, Heatlieote (Vic.), is reported to
have 18in. to 2ft. of stone worth £l5 a ton. This
is quite a new make, being further west than the
original Peter’s Gully reef, and an eastern crosscut at
200 ft. is going out to cut the old reef, supposing it to be
there. As this reef was shown by the old shaft, which
the present co. unwatered, to be living strongly below
150 ft., the prospects of the new workings hitting it are
good. A little ring of bears hits the stock whenever it
goes above 4s. or ss. ; but it still has strong local
support.

“ S.F.” on the emu’s slit windpipe :
In answer to “ Sallad ” (B. 5/12/’O7). The emu (Dromceus
Novce Hollandiat) is normally provided with a tracheal
diverticulum of great interest. There is a natural defect
in the front of the windpipe at a variable spot between
the fiftieth and sixty-fifth ring: of cartilage. The deficiency
may involve six or more rings. In the emu chick the
defect is scarcely noticeable, and the extremities of the
rings are almost in contact. As the bird grows, the
mucous membrane lining the windpipe becomes gradu-
ally pushed through this opening, till it forms a large sac
between the skin of the neck and the trachea. The adult
emu inflates this bag when it produces its peculiar boom-
ing sound resembling the noise made by blowing across
the mouth of a large bottle. Concerning the function of
this pouch nothing is known save that it seems useful to
boom with. It sometimes becomes inflamed and dis-
tended with mucus.

“ T.A.W.” : Be “ Ham O.’s ” inquiry
(B. o/12/’O7) as to origin and meaning of the ex-
pression “No good to gundy.” “Gundy” is a
corruption of a Welsh word meaning to steal,
shake, pinch, or hook, and the expression simply
means that a thing is not worth stealing.
“William V.”: “HamC.” (B. 5/12/’O7)
asks for the origin of the expression “ No good to
Gundy.” Gundy is an abbreviation of Gundagai,
and the phrase originated way back in 1852—the
year of the big flood, which caught up Gundagai
in its embrace, and scattered it over Riverina, and
threw bits of it as far as Thargomindah. On the
eve of the catastrophe a bulloeky pulled into
Gundagai, and camped at the old crossing on the
Murrumbidgee. After tea, he had a look round
the town to make sure that it was all there, and
then turned into bunk in the waggon. He woke
at daylight, feeling sea-sick, and found himself
afloat. Mistrusting the seaworthiness of his
craft, he made haste to get from under the tar-
paulin, and with a plunge and a splash, was safe
among the branches of a gumtree. After disen-
tangling a few codfish from his whiskers, he looked
towards the blank that had been Gundagai, and
remarked sadly, that “’Tweren’t much good to
Gundy.” In confirmation, I might mention that
the tree is still there.

“ J.P.” : Here at Barcaldine (Q.) we are
having drought again, but, thank Heaven ! we
have got last year’s flood to talk about; and the
stories grow whiskers and other ornaments as
they get older. During that flood the towns of
Stonehenge and Jundah, on the lower Thompson,
were threatened by famine, when some bright
mind conceived the idea of carriage by water. An
order was given to a Longreach firm, and shortly
afterwards the pioneer boat was launched. She
was christened the Storm King, and, after re-
ceiving her cargo, started down stream amidst
enthusiasm. She arrived safely, after battling
her way over the tops of trees and through a
wilderness of logs and wreckage. Other liners
were built; then came the Lusitania of the
West, viz., the Great Eastern. She was a
beautiful-looking craft with hurricane decks, and
classed A 1 at the Longreach pub. When
she left port and sailed gracefully out to sea
her captain and crew received an ovation.
The first day or two out they experienced excep-
tionally dirty weather, the seas running shrubs
high. Eventually the wind abated, and the crew,
dead tired and knocked up, anchored in a shel-
tered bay to get a rest. When they woke in the
morning the craft was high and dry on a mud
bank, half-a-mile from water of any kind. A few
days later the sailors rolled up their swags and
tramped back to town. There’s good prospect of
getting the vessel re-floated about the year 1920
that is, if the law of averages holds out.

“ Poleaxe ” : “ Agricola ” (B. 28/11/’O7)
describes the hunting of a ’possum by a gohanna.
That go. must have felt very hungry, else he
wouldn’t have been so violent. The oil-producer
usually gets his ’possum by sprawling on a
limb in the sunlight and mesmerising the little
animal out of the dark hollow it inhabits by eye-
traction pure and simple. Have repeatedly seen
this done, and the ’possum’s laments are painful
as it crawls up under the influence, to taste death
in its most revolting form.

“ P.C.K.” : “ Gip ” (B. 14/2/’O7), praising
the pluck of “ willie wagtail,” does not praise it
enough. Never have I seen such eagles as were
on the Upper Macleay, 40 years ago
—eagles, mind, not eagle-hawks—and
I have seen some big ones on the
Bathurst side, and bigger on Monaro.
Yet, big as were those of the Macleay,
many a time I have seen them abandon
their majestic soaring flight, and, with
unusual flappings of mighty wings,
ungainly downward plunges, and
furious jerkings of fierce heads vainly
endeavor to shake off the maddening
assaults of willie. That fearless little
bully always got himself somehow
between the eagle’s wings, and always
kept above him, no matter how fast
or high he flew. From his refuge be-
tween the giant’s wings he would
dart at the top of the head and the
eyes. Magpies, leatherheads, and
dollar-birds will often follow an eagle
for miles, and sometimes in the nesting
season will venture to attack him;
but seldom single-handed, as willie
does always. Other birds, too, alter
their cries when an eagle appears, but
willie does not alter worth a darn.
* * * *
The complaint of “ W.H.M.” :
Is “ Agricola’s ” story of gohanna and ’possum (B.
28/11/’O7) a temperance lecture, or what? I am not
satisfied with the fight put up by “ Agricola’s ” ’possum.
Also I am willing to back the ’possum to do the drop act
any number of times without coming to grief, and, if
necessary, to lose any gohanna in a contest of speed at
even weights.
As a kid I used to go ’possuming with other kids along
the thickly-covered gum flats of the Murray, near Echuca
(Vie.). We depended on a company-owned pack of mon-
grels and a rusty “tommyhawk” or two to do our
execution. When a ’possum was located in a favorable
sapling we set to work to demolish the foundations of
his home. The dogs knew their work, and would rush
the head of the tree from all sides the instant it hit the
earth. It was exciting sport, but we got few ’possums.
Often a too eager dog rushed too soon and was laid out,
and sometimes there were casualties among the hunters,
but the quarry escaped to the next tree nine times out of
ten.
After one experience the ’possum never waited for the
crash, but took a flying start from the falling timber.
Now, I cannot reconcile the extreme alacrity of those
’possums with the slothfulness of “Agricola’s” beast.

“J.P.” (Blackall) : Be John Fry’s shear-
ing of 828 sheep in a week (B. 11/7/’O7). It’s a
fair performance, but no record. The Australian
record belongs to Jack Howe, of this town,
who shore 1437 sheep in a working week
of 5| days, at Alice Downs, in 1892. His
best effort on that occasion was 321 for the day.
A good performance was that put up a few weeks
ago at Tocal, down the Thompson. A team there
averaged 159 per man per day from start to finish,
one day getting as high as 182. These men are
known as “ Pyper’s guns”—Pyper being a Long-
reach shearing contractor who travels around with
a portable plant. The leading “gun” in the
show is Billy Lynham, who one day cut 225 ; he
exceeded the 200 in 12 out of 18 full working days
in the shed just referred to. May remark that in
the search for really big shearing tallies you’ll
have to come to Queensland every time.

“ Bdyson ” : I was travelling in a waggon
when I struck the tightest of all the tight town-
ships in my wide experience. The man who
owned the waggon was selling dry goods, tobacco,
and sly grog to the peaceful agriculturists, the
butter founders, and the navvies of the camps up
the line. The township was a new one, and com-
posed mainly of navvies, splitters, and their wives,
who had settled down about theNativeDog, a way-
side shanty, and induced a speculator to run up a
paling, iron, and sugee-bag store. The men went to
work up the line on trucks worked with levers.
We drove into an empty township. The waggoner
tried a few homes, but all were silent, fireless,
desolate. The Native Dog was bolted and
barred, and the sugee-bag store bore this legend :
“Up at the accident, back in a month.” The
waggoner picked a camp and unharnessed. We
built a fire and made tea, and presently the in-
habitants of Cobb’s Corner began to drift home.
They were capable of drifting, no more. In point
of fact they were an unspeakably drunken public,
and too full for utterance. They surged down
the track from “ up the line,” fell into their tents,
huts, and houses, and slumbered where they fell.
We made earnest inquiries, but elicited nothing,
and conjecturing that some mad philanthropist
had thrown open his illegal still to a grateful
people, I started up the route on a voyage of dis-
covery. I walked three miles. The way was
littered with slumbering drunks, and as dead
centre was approached I heard the murmurs of a
mad jamboree. An amazing spectacle met my
gaze when I turned into Humble’s cutting. Here
a locomotive was turned on its side, and the
wreckage of a train was piled against one rocky
wall. About a big pool in a rock-hole to the right
were gathered half the inhabitants of Cobb’s
Corner, and divers men from other places. They
were lying on their stomachs, and when they felt
disposed they refreshed themselves at the dam.
The mystery was explained. There had been a
truckload of beer on the wrecked train. The
barrels had been burst in the concussion, their
contents had drained into the receptacle in the
rock, and a reservoir of beer was thrown open to
the simple bush dwellers. When I arrived a
committee was holding down two men who wanted
to swim in the public beer, and the policeman
from Bealiba was preserving order. Never was
order more sedulously preserved. He was tucked
away under a truck, dead to the world. *
“ Mallarapoonie “ M.M.” (B. 14/11/’O7)
tells how some incautious person pushed a walking
stick through an ant-eaten State school. I once
accidently shoved an umbrella through the bottom
of a life-boat off the Westralian coast. The
harbormaster at the port of clearance had spoken
disrespectfully to our skipper about carrying
inadequate life-saving apparatus, and firmly re-
quested that increased accommodation should be
provided. The owners supplemented the usual
battered dinghy with a dry-rotted deathboat, and
we went to sea in the height of the monsoon
season with the man-trap riding bravely at the
port davits. I was coxswain of the corpseboat
when landing mails up the coast, and the insignia
of my office was a gamp to stave off rain and sea
spray. Had occasion once to throw down the
brolly and take hold of the tiller with both hands.
The gamp stood erect on the bottom planking.
The Japanese seaman on the stroke oar grinned.
When I withdrew the gamp a small live geyser
started to operate on board. I looked serious.
The Jap seaman grinned some more, but said
nothing. Replacing the gamp I corked up the
geyser. When we returned to the vessel, and the
boat was on its way to the davits, our skipper’s
eagle eye detected the orifice by the escape of
bilge water. Then he surged a lob about some
passengers practising pistol shooting at gulls
reckoned they couldn’t hit a haystack, and would
be scuttling the ship next. The carpenter started
to put a patch over the orifice, but the bottom fell
out of the tomb-boat and saved him the trouble.

“Brad”: Be “Douglas T’s” query (B.
31/10/’O7) as to why the average dog goes cranky
when a crow approaches his private apartments.
Knew a tame crow at Hamilton (Vic.) which was
on excellent terms with the house-dog (a mongrel
fox-terrier). The only time “ Foxy ” showed any
dislike to his black friend was when the latter
attempted to dine with him.
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” : During my Outbacli
exile I wrote a par. for The Bulletin describing
how a well-known Outbacker had been badly
bitten on the nose, cheeks and chin by a cod in
the Paroo, while lying flat on his stomach to have
a drink ! I pointed out that the bitten man was
so badly scarred on the cheeks and chin that he
had to grow a beard to hide the serrated souvenir.
Unfortunately he couldn’t grow whiskers on his
nose, where the worst part of the trouble was.
Incredulous folk (like “ Wun Hi'’ and “Wun
Lung” and “Wun Man Wun Vote”) sniffed
incredulously. How comes a well-authenticated
story of the biting of a Wagga professional mau
by a Murray cod in the Murrumbidgee. I attach
tile Hekali) par. as an unimpeachable authority.
“F.H.R.”: While quite agreeing with
“ Janor” (B. 3/10/ 07) in his attack on food fad-
dists, I would be very thankful to him for some
further information re those 20in. logs he and his
mate timbered their rise with. Given green
timber of, say, 6ft. in length, these logs would
weigh about lcwt. per foot, or at least Gcwt. in
all, for anything less than a 6ft. rise is super-
fluous. Now, despite that 421 b. of meat, could
“Janor ” and his pal pull 6cwt. up a one-in-forty-
five? Of course, I’m not such an ancient as
“Janor ” admits to be, but I helped to put up a
rise in bad country out North Gippsland way a
few years back, and we used 6in. by 12in. timber
(bush logs), joggled. We had a block and tackle,
and we found it hard enough to keep the timber
within cooee of a treacherous back at that. We
were forced to fire an occasional shot in a hard
substance which ran through the ore channel, and
when we did the face used to have a nasty habit
of climbing 10ft. or 12ft. out of reach and drop-
ping short tons on us while we bullocked the
timber up a wet, crooked pass. Time meant life
there, and the small timber spelt time. If we
had used “Janor’s” timber, death would have
overtaken us before we got the trees in position.
* * * *
Melbourne Age lately reported a queer
accident that happened to Mrs. Clingan, of
Camperdown (Vic.), whose husband threw a
scythe at a cow he was bailing up—or, rather,
wasn’t bailing up. The weapon bounced off the
beast’s horn and cut the lady’s cheek open. This
habit of persuading a harmless, necessary cow
into a bail by means of a scythe is highly
irregular. There are several recognised ways of
accomplishing the feat. One is to get a small
boy after school to step down the paddock and
round up the milkers; the small boy—dog-tired
and disgusted—has ways and means that the
grown-up knows nothing about. Another wky is
to sool three or four healthy mongrels on to
Strawberry’s heels; you won’t get much milk
that way, but you’ll get the cow, with luck.
Another is to set the women-folk on to the job
while you stand off and pray; but you have to
pray very hard. Probably the simplest plan,
though, is to shift the bail and put it round the
cow.
* * * *
“ Gr.S.K.” : It were a crime, when con-
sidering utterly dead N.S.W. towns, to overlook
Milparinka. It is the climax and the extreme
limit of Outback dreariness. Milparinka was
prosperous in the days of Mount Browne. Now
its scores of buildings, which were once banks,
hotels, stores, etc., are falling to pieces. Two
pubs remain, and their efforts to ensnare custom
afford the only amusement that comes in the way
of the mournful inhabitants.
THE ORDER OF THE BATH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This Sunday bathing, unfortunately, is only one of the
many forms of Sabbath desecration that are attaining
abnormal development at the present time, and not a
‘ "V of our own young people are being carried away by
,l. Two or three young men and women, members of a
suburban choir, resigned from the choir so as to be free
to go bathing on Sunday. —Extract from religious paper.
We had some comely maidens in our choir, but the best
Was pretty Dorcas, dainty and demure ;
She brought a congregation that no other church
possessed,
And every one salvation reckoned sure ;
We’d hundreds of Endeavorers, who kept each godly
rule—
Each mission-evening won us some recruit;
And scores applied as teachers at the little Sunday-school
Where she taught the young idea how to shoot.
But, now, in awful discords are the famous anthems sung.
The sermons fall on transepts desolate,
The Missions all have stopped, and the Endeavorers are
bung,
And, worse than all, quite empty is the plate;
And with the younger members that we want so much
to reach,
Two-up, I grieve to say, has taken root,
And not a single youth is there who’ll volunteer to teach
Where Dorcas taught the young soul how to shoot.
For the surf has whispered to her, and our Dorcas has
resigned,
Obedient to its sweet, seductive call;
To-day her dainty figure in a costume is confined
That fits her like the paper on the wall.
While the church is vainly praying for the converts she
can make,
Her missionary zeal is bearing fruit,
And every Sunday morning where the biggest rollers
break
Is Dorcas teaching Bondi boys to shoot.
N.S.W.

“Jimmie Pannikin ” : I agree with “ Full-
lup” in his protest against the use of “yer”as
bush vernacular. During my three years of Out-
back exile and exciting experience, I carefully
studied speech peculiarities. “You” is invari-
ably called “ya,” not “yer.” The “a” is ex-
tremely short. Readers of “Pannikin” pars will
have noticed my adherence to “ ya ” as the correct
bush vernacular. In the plural, “yuz” or “yz”
is much more common than “ yez ” or “ yous.”
* * * *
“Human” : “The Prooshan ” (B.
28/11/’O7) oversteps the truth. Some bushmen
still wear doormats on their chins and sufficient
head foliage to stuff a mattress. Know one my-
self, intimately. He’s a real old whaler, and has
humped Matilda for years and years—between
the top of Little Bourke-street and the bottom
thereof.' Sometimes, greatly daring, he strikes
out for the desolate wastes that lie N. by N.W.
of Menzies’. But not often. He is getting on in
years now, and has lost a little of youth’s dash
and recklessness. He carries a swag, billy,
damper, bowyang, stringy bark, and buck-
jumper—all the regular properties of the true
bushman. Withal he cadges—ye gods, how he
cadges ! When he strays into the wilds of St.
Kilda road he grows uneasy; and once, when a
cruel deceiver lured him, by means of lying talk
re beer, into Albert Park, a search party had
to be despatched. He is the most convincing
sample of sundowner I have ever seen. A grand
type which—under the Yag. Act—is unfortunately
becoming extinct.
* * * *
“Broadaxe”: “F.H.R.” (B. 7/11/’O7)
mayn’t know all about those “sleepers” either.
Have found that a sleeper’s capacity to absorb
moisture depends more on the density or other-
wise of the timber than on the mode of producing
the sleeper. It is possible to prevent a “quar-
tered ” or free sleeper from split-
ting at the end when the “ dog”
is driven into it. Helped to saw
many thousands of redgum sleepers
for Junee-Hay and Narrandera-
Jerilderie railway lines, and as both
Department and contractors were con-
vinced that timber was too valuable to
be reduced to sleeper choppers’ chips,
thousands of sawn “ quartered” sleepers
were taken. But the saw-millers had to
bore and bolt the ends of all “quartered”
sleepers. I saw many of the old original
bolted ends doing service 20 years after.
Writer was not arguing re the comparative
durability of sawn or hewn sleepers, but
protesting against the wholesale waste of
timber by sieeper-squarers as compared with
the saw-millers’ waste in the same industry.
Along the lines above referred to where
sleepers were cut by saw-mills, it is safe to
say that millions of feet of sawn timber were
produced from thoseparts of the sleeper logs
that “ choppers ” make chips of ; and those
millions of feet helped to build the towns
of Wagga, Junee, Narrandera, Hay,
Wentworth, and most of the Darling
River towns, to say nothing of countless
villages, woolsheds, and wells; also, I
contend that it is better for the Railway
Commissioners to provide work for bolt-
makers at our ironworks than encourage the
sinful waste of timber, which only provides
feed for bush-fires to ruin our growing forests.

“Garry”: !“ G. Mac’s” bridle-breaking
cure reminds me. This was my cocky’s plan to
cure a horse that would not pull. He would take
the beast down to the river, where it was narrow
but deep; stretch a rope from bank to bank, hook
the “jib” to one end, then get his favorite horse
Boxer on the opposite bank and hitch him to the
other end. The next move was to start Boxer,
and back would come the jibber until he dis-
appeared with a splash in the river. My cocky
would then,turn Boxer down stream, and tow the
other out. In this way he had cured dozens, he
said —used to buy them cheap, cure them, then
sell them as pullers. When I was with him he
brought home a splendid-looking horse, which in
harness refused to budge an inch. “ We’ll take
him to the river,” was all he said. He started
Boxer as usual; but when Boxer pulled the
other pulled harder, till Boxer landed in the
middle of the river. Then the trouble started,
for at this point the jibber stopped short and
would not pull Boxer out, and we had to manage
it somehow between us. We did it; but the
language haunts me still.
THE LOST ONE’S RETURN.
“ Why, look here, there’s yer brother Bill coming along that yer heard had pegged out up in
Queensland last year.”
“ Yes, that’s him all right. And he ain’t got that old kangaroo slut that he took away with
him. My word, but the old man’ll rouse if he’s lost her.”
Apropos of the renewed discussion con-
cerning the artesian water supply in Australia,
Dr. Jack, who was for years Queensland’s geolo-
gist, holds that the sponge-like rock that he
christened Blythesdale Braystone, is the absorbent
which maintains the supply. In all artesian
country, he says, there is such a source.
Taking that view of the origin of the underground
reservoirs, he regards it as highly improbable that
the stock will give out, unless rain leaves off fall-
ing as a permanent thing. In Cheshire (England),
where the artesian area is limited, it has been
found that the greatest flow is six months after
the heaviest rains, and that fact is one of the
pillars on which Jack built his comforting theory.

“P. Winkle”: “J.G.”is quite correct
about Murray cod and perch being found in many
unlikely places since the big Campaspe flood in
September, ’O6 (B. 5/12/’O7). But what has this
t 0 do with the quick growth of our native fish
that run to size ? My experiences are based on
dams dug and filled from the Campaspe irrigation
channels. If “J.G.” knows anything about the
channel country, he must be aware that wherever
the channel crosses a road the inlets are covered
with wire netting, with an inch and a half (or
l ess ) mesh, to keep out the rabbits, so
sma ll fry (or ova) only can reach the
dams . Most of the tanks or dams on
Restdown are like sieves, and owing to the
eternal soakage have to be partially filled three
or four times a year. Now, there has been no flood
for 15 months, and the tanks I speak of as holding
fish have been dry for a spell since last Christmas
(’O6) owing to the channels being washed away by
the Campaspe September overflow. When the
channels started to run in July the dams were
filled, through the wire-netting meshes necessary
to exclude bunny—yet there are now decent fish
to be caught, and this is December !

“ The Prooshan ” : I once struck an old
miner prospecting for a deep lead under the
basalt. He was 40ft. down, and had driven 70ft.
from that level without employing assistance. As
soon as his sft. x shaft reached the slinging
limit, he erected a platform of logs 4ft. high, put
on a windlass, and fixed a wooden shoot leading
from the edge of the hole, with side cleats to
keep the bucket from sliding down when tipped.
He fastened a short, light tail-rope to the hoisting
rope to avoid greasing that with mud at the
bottom. When his bucket was full, he pulled on
his “ endless belt ” rope, and up went the stuff,
and, adroitly swinging it out, he dumped it on to
the head of the shoot, then tipped it with ease,
and, reversing his pull, brought it back overhead
and down again for refilling, He shovelled the
tip out twice per day, lunch time and knock off
time. Some miners think it’s impossible to sink
a shaft on your own, but it can easily be done. A
winder is idle three-fifths of his time except in
soft country.
* * + *
“Agricola”: Re “Elmore’s” caterpillar
(B. 24/10/’O7). Instead of four bunches of yellow
flowers on its back, it has eight, and it is with
these that it does the mischief. So long as peace
reigns, the flowers lie flat, but when touched the
creeper raises them and jabs them into the in-
truder. In the bush the caterpillar is known as
the “ Spiteful Jack.” The smart from its sting
does not go away for about five days ; and always
becomes more painful after washing. Strange to
say, it cannot sting the palm of the hand, and I
have seen a child carrying seven of the brutes to
school in her bare hand. Yet these same stung
me on the back of the hand when I went to in-
vestigate. The Spiteful Jack is the larva of the
Mottled Cup moth, and its smooth, oval chrysalis
case, with a circular cap off the top, is exceedingly
common on the bark of the stringy-barks and
messmates.
* * * #
“Crow”: I was running a farm down
Gippsland way, and had an old mare (named
Bloss) within a week of foaling. One morning I
threw her a sheaf of hay, but she refused to eat,
which struck me as uncommon, she being usually
a mighty feeder before the Lord. I jumped at
the conclusion that she was pretty bad, and rode
into the township for the vet. He treated
the mare, and she seemed a bit easier. At
this stage, as we were watching Bloss, and just
intending to go in for a feed, about a dozen crows
flew down, and lobbed on the fence. I remarked
that they were the first lot of crows I had
seen for months. The horse-killer seemed to
like Bloss’ chances of recovery less when he saw
the crows. However, we went in and had a bit
of “scram,” and on returning found that Bloss
was the mother of a dead foal, and the crows were at
dinner. Now, how did the black flying prophets
know that Bloss would slip her foal ?
LAZY SUMMER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the summer-time, and now that summer’s on the
spot
It’s up to us to speak of how “ the weather’s gettin’ hot,
And, on the windy, dusty days, to curse the breeze from
Bourke,
And ponder (over beer) new ways for dodging summer
work.
This is the summer-time wherein strangle Sunday fish are
caught
In quantities, by men of sin —when boating-beer is bought
On Saturday, at eventide, by people who appear
To think the fisher-fates deride the man who brings no
beer.
Quite so ! And, summer nights, it is the thing for us to go
And watch the breakers foam and fizz all round the girls
we know ;
To see cool symmetry afloat among’ the crimson weeds,
And watch pink, paddling toes, and note white knees
across white steeds
That race along the sunset wastes of ocean. Here’s the
clime
In which the torrid thirster hastes in droves to “see tha
time ”
Between the acts ; who comes in late, chewed cloves
upon his tongue,
And leaves, betimes, to seize his plate of bivalves while
they’re young.
Here is the summer-time, all hot; and we are heated, too.
It’s pleasant, now, to find a spot beneath the staring blue,
Wherein to spend an hour ill thought, and where the
distant swarm
Of grubbing bustlers matters naught; where beer is not too
w’arm ;
And where stacked ice in mighty blocks salutes the
thirsty stare
Of men who chase the beer that mocks, in sleevers rich
and rare.
Yes, now’s the summer-time, red-hot, when Brown and
Johnson lurk
Behind long beers, to hatch a plot for dodging summer
work.
VIC.
