PERSONAL
ITEMS
The Bulletin is informed that the late
Mr. Henry Clarke paid over the Hoskins legacy
(of which he was trustee) to Mr. Hoskins’ son
within a few months of his (Mr. Clarke’s) death.
Joey Carruthers, ex-Premier of N.S.W.
(who, by the way, is becoming something sizeable
among squatters), will reach his half-century on
the 21st.
Theodore Francis Bevan, who took the
home trail from North Sydney recently, aged 47,
spent part of his life blazing the White Man’s
track in Papua, and in 1887 discovered two
rivers, the Aird and the Purari, the latter being
the second largest trickle in the Possession. His
book, “Toil, Travel and Discovery in British
New Guinea,” tells much of what he saw in that
yammy country.
PAINTING IT RED.
Lieutenant Shackleton said that lie spoke in no spirit of
Jingoism when he said that if they could even put a
little more red on the map, if they could paint a little
bit redder this small globe of ours, the Antarctic
expedition would not have been in vain.— Sydney
TELEGRAPH.
The start of the Wallace money, now
being poured out in Costs, was in a Victorian gold-
fields grog-shop, in the roaring ’fifties. “Johnny”
coined £l2O a day, it is said, for one pretty long
spell.
During the 12 days that 40 South Austra-
lian legislators (including three Ministers) spent
sailing in gladsome fashion down the Murray in
the steamer Trafalgar, only five bottles of whisky
were consumed on board the vessel, and there
were no other intoxicants carried. A large pro-
portion of the Parliamentary party were Metho-
dist local preachers, like Price, and nearly all the
Labor members are teetotallers.
Robert Laidlaw, a Victorian pioneer dating
from 1839, died at Heidelberg, last week, aged
over 91 years. He was Scotch, and was all his life
on the land. His first venture was a squattage at
Boroondara ; he bought the land for £2O, and the
sheep thereon for 365. per head. The progeny of
the sheep he sold later on at'4s. fid. each.
Vere Goold, the Monte Carlo murderer,
seems to have had a passion for order. To this
craving he set down the mutilation of Mrs.
Levin’s body. The remains, said the neat and
tidy Thug (what a title for a poem, by the way—
“ The Tidy Thug ” !), looked confused and irregu-
lar in the box, and he cut them up to make them
more uniform and symmetrical.
Frederick Lassetter, of the Sydney uni-
versal providery, entered upon his 79th year on
Friday. He came to Sydney with his parents in
1832, then went to Tasmania, then across to Mel-
bourne, where he entered the office of William
Easy, an early-day auctioneer. He married a
daughter of Lancelot Iredale, an ironmonger who
had established himself in Sydney in 1820 ; and
after that he “bogged in ” commercially and grew
to greatness.
Died last month at Craigie (N.S.W.),
.T. Gr. Gough, whom J. C. Watson ousted from the
Young seat in 1894. Gough entered Parliament
in 1889, and was re-elected as a member of the
first N.S.W. Labor Party in 1891, but his refusal
to sign the Party’s pledge in ’94 brought Watson
into the field against him as the official candidate.
Gough was a builder and contractor by trade,
but was reckoned to know as much as the average
about mining, and did a lot in that line.
Bruce Smith and Janet Mrs. Clarke and
the other people who reckon that the North is
only really habitable by Chunder Ram and Co.,
and that the white person up there can’t be ex-
pected to do anything more energetic than fan
himself, ought to have been in Brisbane while the
Englishmen were playing cricket, so that they
could have seen Hutchings. Fresh from the Kent
hop-fields, he batted vigorously for over an hour
in each innings. And he did it bare-headed,
though the sun was streaming down in its best
Brisbane manner.
Man that is born of woman never looks
much more ridiculous than when his pet name is
read out in court during the divorce proceedings.
In the course of the Wallace divorce case in Mel-
bourne it transpired that some of the men who
foregathered at “Novar,” answered to the pet
designations of “Sossa,” “Fatty,” “Bunny,”
“Viv,”and “Jam-pot.” When a man begins to
answer to the name of “Jam-pot,” there is only one
lower depth—he might be called “Towzer,” and
get the bone on the mat. By the way, in every
divorce suit of any note in Australia there is men-
tioned someone named “ Bunny.” Why ?
“ Auld Lang Syne” : Fifty years ago a
Rev. John Reid thumped the pulpit cushion of
the Presbyterian Church in Elm-street, Hotham
(Melbourne). The shepherd, after a long time of
vacillating conduct,
'gave such offence to
his hearers that they
adopted the extreme
measure of locking him
out. The holy battler
was not dispirited. He
rented a disused hay
and corn store at the
corner of Queensberry
and Errol streets,
Hotham, and went on
grind i ng out th e blessed
Word as before. After
some months’ strenu-
ous endeavor, he found
that the nimble three-
penny bit did not fall
into the plate in suffi-
cient quantity to pay
exes, so he renounced
Presbyterianism to be-
come a Congregational
pastor in Sydney. The
Lord gave him two
sons. One of them,
George, has always
ignored his days in
Hotham, and affects to
have forgotten that he
passed through the
doors of the North Mel-
bourne Grammar School
many times before he
proceeded to the Scotch
College to finish his edu-
cation. The writer won-
ders if the Hon. George
recollects playing the
truant from Hotham
school, and going to the
Saltwater River to
bathe. Probably the
sole of his foot still bears
the trace of a scar left
by a broken bottle at
the back of the old
grand stand on the Flem-
ington course.
Auctioneers are getting a mortgage over
tbe Mayor’s cupboard in Adelaide. T. Bruce has
been followed by Frank Johnson, the talky
Adelaide-born son of an old Parliamentarian.
Ihe new man has to call in a married sister to say
the nice things at the Mayoress’s end of the table.
Ihe McArthurs, whose big “reconstruc-
tion ” is a bitter Christmas pill to a lot of people,
hawe for years tried to mix softgoods and politics.
Vy A., born in Sydney 50 years ago, is pretty
high up in the Liberal party in England, and was
-J umor Lord of the Treasury from 1892 to 1895.
His father, Alexander, was in the N.S.W. Parlia-
ment, and afterwards member for Leicester in the
Commons. McMillan, the best known of the
directors in Australia, is a nephew of Alexander’s.
Parkes made him Treasurer.
Wi Pere, who for years represented a
Maori constituency, got tossed out of his seat in
Maoriland Parliament at last elections by a young
Maori B. A. Wi was a fightable old rangatira. He
once smote the nose of an interpreter for refusing
to interpret some unpolished words to the
House, and, when pulled up by the Speaker,
threatened to call out the militia. But he
is a shaky old man now, and has lost his sting.
Lately they lifted him up to the Legislative
Council. But since that he has made only one
little speech, protesting that he couldn’t fight
among the lords. And as he couldn’t fight he
sat down and went to sleep.
PROOF POSITIVE.
“ Pat, I’m surprised to see you knock a man down like that.”
“ Well, yer Rircrence, ’e called me a loiar, and, begobs, I made 'im prove it!”
George Yesey Allen is back in Sydney,
as manager of a team of Fijian cricketers this
time. Allen is putting up a record. First he
was known to the public as Secretary to the Royal
Commission on Railway Commissioner Eddy.
Later he was appointed to the N.S.W. Public
Service as shorthand writer, which job he threw
up after a year or two. The conventions of the
official grind annoyed Allen. Later still, he was
Secretary to the Bendigo Jubilee Exhibition.
Between times he has faced many troubles, and
either beaten or bluffed them all.
The public can tell now why William
M'Millan, Parkea’s Heaven-born financier, left
politics. M'Millan’s firm—W. and A. M‘Arthur,
Ltd. —had an “ ordinary ” capital of £160,000 and
a “preference” capital of £175,000. The co. left
off paying “ordinary” dividends in 1903, and,
on account of a number of ghastly investments, it
is now admitted that the whole of the ordinary
capital is lost, and will have to be wiped clean
out, while 7s. 6d. in the £ of the preference
capital will have to go the same way, and
various creditors will need to take shares to see
the thing through. And William M‘Millan (who
would now have a lot more sympathy if he had
been a lot less bumptious) belonged to the party
whose chief shriek is that Private Enterprise
can always do the thing better !
One of an ancient name out Leichhardt
(N.S.W.) way died with the month—George
William Hearn, aged 56 years. The original
Abraham Hearn, who lies ballasted with a sub-
stantial monument in Ashfield cometery, came to
N.S.W. from Gloucestershire in 1790. He settled
on a farm at Camden, and afterwards kept the
Spread Eagle Inn on Brickfield Hill. Afterwards
he bought land from Captain Piper on the Parra-
matta-road, and built the Australian Hotel in
1831. He was a witness in the famous Newtown
ejectment case. In 1849, a younger Hearn took
over the business and called the inn the Bald
Faced Stag, by which name it is still known,
and going strong. It is marvellous how the old
pioneers ran to pubs. It is hardly possible to
reminisce over one of them without mentioning
which hotel he opened, and which other hotel he
moved to later on.
“ S.S.” meets an agitator :
The red ties flocked in thousands to give Keir Hardie a
reception when he arrived at Spencer-street, Melbourne.
Red ties, red badges, and red neckerchiefs were very
much in evidence. Then Hardie stepped out of the
carriage, looking not a bit like the frenzied iconoclast,
but very like a Wimmera farmer down for Christmas,
and a gasp of disappointment went up from the
multitude. “ Whj', ’e ain’t got a red tie!” snorted
a disgusted gentleman on my right. Hardie was
not sporting the red badge of courage, and I fancy
he slumped in popular estimation as a result of
his shameful neglect. There was a curiously vague con-
ception of the stranger’s mission abroad among his ad-
mirers. I asked people why he had come to Australia, and
the answer was “He’ll show ’em!” or words to that
effect. Nobody knew what Hardie was going to show, or
whom he was about to show it to, but the opinion was
that the operation would be very beneficial to the work-
ing man. For my part, I welcome the Tilletts and
Manns and Hardies, since close investigation destrovsthe
Australian’s meek faith in the man from a far land, as
against the local product.
Charles Rasp, the discoverer of Broken
Hill, died a few weeks ago ; and George
McCulloch, who got the biggest bite of the new
find, has now followed. Rasp was boundary
rider on Mt. Gipps station; McCulloch was the
manager, and as one member after another of the
little syndicate took his profit, McCulloch bought
the shares. Then he became an absentee and a
big-pursed patron of Art—about the last thing
that would have been expected of him. Still,
though he was an absentee, he had some virtues :
knowing what a God-forgotten place Broken Hill
was, and how deadly lead-mining is, he helped
the Broken Hill hospital pretty liberally, and gave
Broken Hill’s little art-gallery a good start.
There are others—jackeroo Philip Charley and
tank-sinker David James, for instance who
apparently haven’t given a thought to the people
who swallow dust and tear out the metal.
Died the other day in a rabbit-burrow, at
Moonta (S.A.), William Thomas, aged 19. With
that red haste which is twin sister to delay William
crawled into a large burrow to fish out the rabbit
and thus save digging. He stuck ; he struggled ;
the burrow fell in ; and that ended the story.
Old John Brown is dead at last. He was
an ex-navy man, and 19 years ago entered Liver-
pool (N.S.W.) Asylum. He said he was born at
Canterbury (Eng.) in 1787. For several years he
lay on his bed in the asylum, unable to move,
speak or hear—or to die. His personal reminis-
cences, when he was able to reminisce, and his
huge stature impressed the asylum authorities
favorably in regard to his alleged age. A hunt at
Canterbury for his birth records was unsuccessful l
still birth records weren’t so methodically com-
piled 120 years ago as they are now; and, anyhow,
a lot of things might happen to the best of records
in 120 years.
Lionel Terry, who emphasised his dislike
of Ah Sin by shooting one of him in Wellington
some time back, announced that he would like a
“fortnight’s change” from the asylum, and faded
into the greyness of Central Otago. As he was
hatless and shod with sandals, his recapture
within a day or so was predicted confidently, and
the Dunedin detectives chartered a motor car with
light hearts ; but for quite a heap of weeks the car
continued to go to and fro over the earth in search
of clues. An enterprising firm seized a cheap
advertisement by exhibiting three pictures exe-
cuted by Terry while in Seacliffe asylum ; but this
enterprise was sternly checked by the medical
superintendent, who demanded the instant with-
drawal of the works of art. The doctor also wrote
to the local press that his late charge was a
courteous and talented gentleman, and that
dwellers who might observe a stately figure in
undress uniform bearing down on them need be
under no apprehension.
By The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant
An ancient Hawkesbury (N.S.W.) native, Edward
Parnell, died at Newcastle last week, aged S 4 years. He
was born at Richmond in 1823, and when a very young
man went to the Narnoi River district and settled upon
the station of his father, near Gunnedah. He afterwards
located in the Hunter district. In 1867 he took up Ids
residence in Newcastle, and resided there ever afterwards.
The first Parnell of whom we have note was a somewhat
historic character. About the year 1810 Daniel Parnell
wrote a respectful letter to Secretary Campbell, asking
him to obtain permission from Governor Macquarie to
establish a theatre. On Macquarie’s arrival Daniel was in
very poor circumstances, and was anxious to get up “a
theatrical temporary display,” which might lift him out
of his difficulties. Governor Bligh had authorised the
erection of a theatre, but the change of Government
consequent on the Rum Revolution put an end
to the speculation. Parnell winds up his letter to
Campbell with: “ I feel some assurance that your
acquaintance with the Muses will befriend at so critical a
juncture, by a representation to his Excellency favorable
to my application, confident as you may be in the assur-
ance that regularity and good order shall be the premier
objects of attention with Sir, etc., Dan'l Parnell.” The
application was evidently not granted, as permission to
build a permanent theatre was not obtained until
Governor Bourke’s time. So presumably “ Dan’l Parnell ”
remained buried in his difficulties. *
DITTYFUL BENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sing hey ! The political, dittyful Bent!
He’s huge and audacious,
He’s fairly pugnacious,
He’s full of a bluster that must have a vent;
For men’s merriment
He was funnily blent,
And vvas put on the throne as a Ruler, was Bent.
He’s full to his wonderful, thunderful brim
Of stories eternal,
Of humor infernal,
And sometimes he’ll cheerfully chant you a hymn ;
He’s burly of limb,
He’ll fight with a vim,
And he’s full up of biz to his wonderful brim.
He treads in his lumbersome, cumbersome way
In places forbidden,
And, though he be chidden,
He’ll always come up again, smiling and gay ;
In work or in play
He’s a beggar to stay,
And he’s got a good grip in his cumbersome way.
We smile at political, dittyful Bent—
But, rowdy or ruly,
He manages duly,
And cannilv minds how the money is spent;
And this ballad is meant
With a kindly intent—
For the land has done roorse than with, dittyful Bent !
QUEENSLAND.
A BENTITE.

214 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY,
A little lady you all know in this city was
once very unhappy. Everyone wondered why.
She had money—piles of it—a figure that men
used to fairly rave about—hosts of admirers—in
fact, was quite ripping. Only her complexion 1
Blotchy and rough, tanned by the sun—it was
wretched. That was her sorrow. Then a change
came about—everyone noticed it. Her skin lost
all its blemishes —it became soft, and smooth, and
beautiful, with that clear satiny look you see in
the skins of little girlies. And the lady is no
longer sad. She is the admired of all. Would
you like to know how the change was brought
about. Listen, while I whisper it. Valaze.
That’s all. Valaze. Has the little fable any
meaning to you ?* 1

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 17, 1907.
My Dear Arini,—
N.S.W. has known two great A.D.C’s.—Wal-
lington and Wilson—and of these the greater is
Wilson, who shortly leaves us. Does the average
onlooker realise all the qualities of tact, good-
humor, memory, firmness, tolerance, and high
diplomacy requisite for success at this invidious
job ? There are, roughly, 5000 people who go to
State Government House. Of these the ones
with sufficient sense not to expect the A.D.C.,
who has seen them once, to remember
them for ever after are in the minority.
No human being does, can, or wants to remember
any fair proportion of a crowd of 5000. It is part
of Wilson’s genius that he creates the illusion of
knowing everybody, and of hankering for a sight
of each fresh face that confronts him. This sort
of thing carries its own punishment often enough;
hostesses struggle for so amiable a young man,
and when they can’t get him (which happens
often enough, since there’s a limit even to an
A.D.C.’s capacity for being entertained) tend to
grow acid. But somehow no one grows acid
in connection with Wilson. He is that
marvel, an attractive man whose name
has never been connected with that of any local
female person, a D. 8.0. (and you wouldn’t know
from him that he’d ever smelt powder), a success-
ful organiser, actor, polite-letter-writer, and a
score of other things. In fact, he’s a prodigy, and
why he ever descended to A.D.0.-ing is one of
the mysteries of this hemisphere. Wallington
was a genius at his job of leading Governors by
the nose, and putting bits into the mouths of the
Buster-Crosses of Australian Society and then
driving them as he pleased. But his talent for
not seeing people of no importance or bank balance,
no matter how well he knew them, amounted to
so much genius that very few cared how soon he got
off the Southern Cross'premises. Besides, he looked
as if he had citric juice in his arteries instead of
blood, and the 4500 persons whom he never could
remember were always telling one another that
he had proposed eighteen times to a certain
millionairess.
The conclusion of the Wallace case will remove
a lowering cloud from the lives of many gay
young men hereabouts. This has been a drear time
when none knew what the evening paper (late
edition) might bring forth. A number of maidens
have made it their heartless practice latterly to gaze
with sympathy at their dinner or theatre partners
and ask in the hushed tone adopted in houses over
which the shadow broods, “ Hare you seen the
Evening Herald?” As who should say, “The
worst has occurred. Is it going to be rat-bane or
the harbor ? ” It was impossible for the man to ask
the girl if any terrible suspicion had at last fallen
upon him ; nor could he leave her right there, and
flee with a disordered visage to the nearest news-
agent and buy a paper regardless of cost. So he
could only sit and speculate miserably upon what
might be, and when, later on, he found that it
wasn’t, he would gladly have slain that girl till she
was quite dead.
Mr. Cricketer Braund must be gnashing his
teeth these test-match day 3. Either that or he
must be icily philosophical. When he goes out
to take his share in the cricket battle half a
thousand raucous young male voices are raised in
cries of “O, Len, dear, how’s Kiddie?” or
“Heard from her lately, Len?” “Boys,” as
Carlyle remarked, “ought to be brought up
underground.”
If the South Pole isn’t nailed down with a
million walrus teeth, I believe the new Antarctic
expedition will catch it. Talk about speed—why,
the Nimrod hadn’t been tied up much more than
24 hours to a Maoriland port before one of its
chief officers had pursued an auburn-haired local
product to the proposal point and persuaded her to
say “ Yes,” or else “ Yairs,” or perhaps “Yep.”
James Edmond, from Lake Te Anau (Maori-
land) —a belated contribution :
Te Anau.
This a land of calm.
Its peace suggests some old, slow, droning psalm.
There is no tram-car screeching on the shore.
No motor fiend is reaching for your gore.
The bobby passes not with heavy tread,
In boots that are enough to wake the dead.
’Tis the abode of peace,
Like places that we read about in Greece.
The people here dwell twenty miles asunder.
No newsboy rouses them to sudden wonder.
Nor wakes the slumbering echoes with a yell
About a Horrid Tale he has to sell.
There is no smell of Beer
In all the long-drawn aisles of leafage here,
And here the mountain tops are very tall,
And the sandfly is numerous and small.
It seemeth to be mostly afternoon,
Or the white hills are reaching for the moon,
Or else the bright red morning sun gets up,
And he whose horse has lost the Melbourne Cup
Behold, his language isn’t half so red
As that same sunrise. Here the winds are dead,
And yet the forest is a living thing,
And birds make all its deep recesses ring.
Just now and then the boat
Creeps in like a soft-footed ghost afloat.
There is no Care
In all these leagues of verdure rich and rare ;
And Worry has gone overboard and drowned ;
And Woe is lost, and shall no more be found.
Therefore, we’d settle down and be at peace,
As folks once did in storied isles of Greece,
Only we miss the papers and the cars,
And sometimes we grow weary of the stars ;
There is no tragedy to talk about.
We also miss our Great Australian Drought.
We cannot stay,
Because we are just ordinary clay.
We haven’t been supplied with sets of wings ;
We’re fitted for plain, ordinary things.
Therefore we scoot from Paradise amain,
And never hitherward shall come again ;
And yet the land is the abode of peace,
Like isles where Byron once got drunk in Greece.
Miss Ola Jane Humphrey was the fixed star in
the audience at Melba’s first concert. In her
recent flight through Paris and New York she
paused long enough to collect some frock works
of art. One was an inspiration in apricot and
coral, with an overdress of white net embroidered
with big poppies and convolvuli in mother of
pearl, fish scales and silver. Her kimona was
black, with gold embroidery and tassels, and her
sportive hair sported amber combs and a white
waving fore-limb of some bird. A revelation in the
way of motor costumes is her skirt and Norfolk
blouse of golden brown kid. Every inch of it is kid
—beautiful fawn baby-goat. With cap and long
boots of the same little billee or nanny, Ola Jane
used to let tidal waves, and suchlike wet circum-
stances, wash over her on the voyage down here.
In reply to the inevitable and profoundly
idiotic question, “Are you glad to be back?”
said Ola Jane : “Why, certainly, I ought to be,
but I’m not sure yet. You see, I left here only
eight months ago, and thought I might come back
in about eight years—if ever. But I feel as
if I’d been bounced back, and so quickly,
that I’ve got a little concussion of the brain.
There were alarms and excursions by speech and
wire and letter with Mr. Williamson ; I, hedging
and dodging from a verbal promise, because a
foreign aunt of mine, with a villa outside Paris,
had offered me a sparkler of a time, with inter-
ludes of rest at Nice and Monte Carlo and by the
Seine itself, and I really badly wanted to rest and
sparkle. Besides, there was the motherling in
New York —I had only a week with her on my
way here. A sudden sally on the part of Mr. Wil-
liamson, at Marienbad, caught me in Paris, when
his wifeand I were at dinner at Mr. Frank Gardner’s.
Say—l’ve seen millionaire and billionaire homes
in N’York and London, but never one so artistic
as Mr. Gardner’s. Why, Honey, he’s got pictures
and marbles and tapestries and ivories whose
namesarekept onfile in the Public Library archives
as if they were the treaties of the Great Powers !
And, unlike other of my countrymen collectors,
you can see he owns them—they don’t own him.
“ The Paris hats ? Well, Honey, this is one—
yes, you are right even if you are horrid. It
is a murderous assault on the whole scheme of
harmonious coloring.”
The huge hat was covered, crown and brim,
with flowers and foliage. A crimson hibiscus lay
prone against a pink rose, a gentian blue trail of
florality trespassed with violence on premises
occupied by a bed of mauve daisies. The artiste
looked meditatively at the millinery transgression,
and then headed into another huge circle of
velvet, with weeping willow feathers of marabout
and heron, and not weighing more than five
pounds. “Mauves and purples are my favorite.
Did you see that someone called the violet frock
I landed in ‘my astral shade’ ? They—no I don’t
mean the Fates when I say they—are wearing
high-necked gowns with the tight-fitting empire
backs and the indeterminate waist line. You
must have a figure like a boy, you must not pull
in your corsets ! If you do They will say you are
only a slug or a chrysalis, or something that isn’t
finished off perfectly.
“ My costumes in ‘ The Scarlet Pimpernel ’ are
not so very unlike the fashions of now. They
follow those worn through the book by Lady
Blakeney—the big hat with the waving plumes,
and the blue velvet robe, and the tall stick with
the bunch of ribbons. Then there’s the opera
gown, the short-waisted, classical thing, covered
with gold embroidery and the spray of scarlet
pimpernel in the hair. No, I won’t say ‘Lud
love you, sir,’ ‘odds fish,’ and ‘gadzooks.’ Mr.
Knight uses all the mild early eighteenth-century
swears that have to be sworn. I hope Sydney
will like me as Marguerite. Say, Honey, if you
don’t like me in it, don’t pretend to be dead, so’s
to be carried out. Isn’t that what Nero’s audi-
ence used to do when he sang on the stage ? He
sang so badly.”
Diabolo is beginning to wind its tentacles
around Sydney. One of the young Stephen boys
has put up a record of 750. But the demon won’t
cast out the Bridge devil—at least, not till Sydney
is cooler. A model form in a current fashion
paper proves the diabolo craze. Its braiding ex-
ploits the string, spools and sticks used in the
game.
Mrs. Charles Bright, widow of the well-known
journalist, has a book on the market, “A Soul’s
Pilgrimage,” which sounds good, though tired.
The will of the late Mitchell of the library
gives to his cousin, Miss Hose Scott, £5OOO. His
sister, Mrs. Merewether, who has a quiverful of
grown-up children and a large bank-balance, is,
after the Library, the largest inheritor. Mr.
Mitchell was always good-humoredly unsym-
pathetic towards Miss Scott’s connection with the
advanced women’s movements.
In this hot-bran-mash weather, when any exer-
tion save lifting a glass of iced lemonade is
anathema, working for a charity will be
counted trebly for righteousness. At Rand wick
Town Hall, last week, Mrs. Rutter arranged a
juvenile matinee in aid of Mrs. Allen Taylor’s
Thirlmere Home Fund. A feminine house (nary
a man) greeted the youthful performers. Miss
May Summerbelle had roped in Miss Linda Davis
and Miss Curphey, from the Tivoli, and the two
skilled ones put the high lights on the perform-
ance.
Miss Chandos-Pole (doesn’t she call it Shandy-
Poole?), who was here visiting the Northcotes
a while ago, is again in Australia. A niece of
hers —an outside-size, goddess-like young party,
Mrs. Sitwell by name—is stagnating on the Blue
Mountains.
A note from London says that Mr. B. R. Wise
is about again in good form, and he attributes his
second wind to his wife’s good nursing.
Mrs. Baker, a daughter of a well-known Maori-
lander, one-time Premier Whittaker, got into
Sydney by the Omrah on Saturday. Another
ex-Maorilander, MissMinnett, one of the smartest
frocked exiles resident in Melbourne, is in Sydney
for the Connell-Mitchell double wedding, when
two squatter brothers marry twin sisters.
Two very old families were united on December
5, when Olendon, eldest son of G. M. Kable, of
Orton Park, was wedded to Emily, fourth daughter
of Mr. Hutchinson, of Wattle Flat. The bride
was given away by her brother-in-law, Leslie
Piper. The original Kable came out in the first
fleet, and the original Piper about a couple of
years after. The Pipers went over the Blue
Mountains in the late ’2o’s, and settled at Allo-
way Bank, on the Kelso side of the Macquarie
River.
Possibly to prevent complications on their wed-
ding day, the twin Mitchell girls (of Neutral Bay)
dressed unlike —for the first time. They have
hitherto been chronically frocked, batted, shod,
and coiffed to correspond. And as they are as
exasperatingly similar as the firm of Dromio and
Co., none but intimate friends knew, without ask-
ing the lady, which was Marjorie and which Wini-
fred. If they had had the naming of themselves
they would probably have both been Marjorie
or Winifred. As it is, are their respective
husbands, the brothers Connell, quite sure that
they have married the right girl ? They may
have proposed to one thinking it was the other, or
to each thinking she was both, or to both in
succession thinking she was only one. But the
expert squatter can detect an unlikeness to its
fellow in each sheep’s face; so perhaps to the
Connells their brides have different ear marks,
even if they are to most people as same as two
coffee beans.
Talking of weddings : A triple alliance will
eventuate in Victoria this month. Three sisters
are to marry three brothers. There’s unanimity !
In N.S.W. there is a boom in doctors in the
matrimonial market. Six bachelors and one
widower are to take up the pale citizen’s burden.
Brennand, Coen, Diethelm, Gillespie Burford,
and Donovan are five of them. Miss Esme Mann,
of Randwick, who has a lovely load of money, but
who could afford to have none, so attractive is
she, is one of the brides-elect.
Miss Selina Anderson, of Parliamentary candi-
date fame, is about to take a consort at Wellington
(N.S.W.). She is a bright, capable woman, and
deserves all kinds of good fortune.
Dr. and Mrs. Worrall returned from globe-
girdling last week.
Mrs. Greig Smith, wife of one of the IJni.
teachers, who has started Sydney into a serious
business of founding a Norland Institute for the
higher training of children’s nurses, looks like a
prize doll. Not a trace of the iron jaw, with
which the serious business of life is often and mis-
takenly connected. Mrs. Mackinnon, the consort
of a N.S. medical man, hopes to start a babies’
boarding house at Manly, at a charge of
about 15s. a week for each young tiger. She
tells with truth and pathos of the sufferings
from heat endured by young children in the
country. Their mothers cannot leave their homes
to bring their offspring to the sea coast for an
extended time, but if there were an accredited
place in which to lodge Silken Hair, funds
could be spared to that extent. Mrs. Mackinnon
is the big-eyed young party who bore the name of
Eleanor Addison—suggestive of Marion Craw-
ford novels. Anyone with such a name is bound
to have high-class “ views.”
Melba and the heat—the heat that hangs like a
vast railway rug wrung out of hot water —are the
two topics affecting Sydney just now. But the
heat has no effect on Melba, and she has
some effect on it. For she is filling
the Town Hall in spite of it. Saturday night’s
audience was described by a man who knows
everyone as an “astounding audience.” It was
no bigger than a Butt audience, but pretty well
every well-known face in Sydney’s Church and
State and School and Society and Politics was to
be seen. Melba was princess-robed in pale blue
with an over dress of white net glittering with
silver embroidery. Her diamond crown keeps its
lustre, and her black hair its ebony.
When Sydney read (over its wheatmeal biscuit
and coffee) that Melba had walked nine miles
owing to the vice-regal motor breaking down at
Fitzroy Falls, Sydney said it wasn’t taking any
foxes in this heat. But Captain Wilson, Miss
Rawson, and the rest of the party have made
affidavits on the matter —so we accept those
nine miles as the Israelites did some re-
marks made from a burning bush. On Sunday
the untired star went to Manly with the Mariches,
and strolled on the sand, laughing at the surf-
bathing; then the party went to Mr. Marich’s
camp at Middle Harbor for lunch. In the evening
the Voice went up the North Shore line to
dinner, and got back at midnight, as untired as
when she began the day. To-day she went on an
all-day picnic to Narrabeen with Mrs. Burdekin
and a party.
On Saturday the Voice listened to another
voice—that of Miss Eva Champion. Spent half-
an-hour giving the youngster a lesson and advice,
and both the voice and the method with which it
is being produced passed their examination with
honors.
“Mario,” from Brisbane :
The Governor’s wife returned to town from Gabbinbar,
last week, to distribute the prizes at the Church of
England High School at Southport, where her own small
hopeful managed to gather in an award for general pro-
gress. Later, the determined lady attended the weekly
meeting of the Children’s Hospital, and her investiga-
tions produced a lively half-hour. First of all, she
inquired into the infantile mortality. The statement
of the resident medico that climatic conditions were
chiefly to blame did not seem to convince her, and with
that practicability which ever characterises her words she
suggested that if Brisbane was unhealthy for gastric
enteritis, the little sufferers might be shifted to the
Sandgate sanatorium, where fresh ocean breezes and
a purer air are available. This sensible view was,
however, met with numerous objections by the
medical staff, and her Ex. then let drop some
pertinent remarks to the effect that, if less time had been
wastedin haggling over small details, the new wards might
have been built, and the hospital authorities might have
been able to produce a better record. When she rose to
go, it was in an atmosphere charged with electricity.
After two race-days—mostly umbrellas, rubber shoes
and ill-temper—the Q.T.C. finished up its first summer
meeting in bright sunshine, and in the presence of some
lovely frocking. The golden-haired proprietor of the
local variety show was one of the best-turned-out women
present, in silver-grey, with a wonderful panel arrange-
ment of blue chine ribbon and Paris guipure. Pretty
Mrs. Cleghorn, wife of one of our big coal men, was a joy
to behold in a gown in which black chiffon glace, black
Maltese lace, and a ribbon-worked white chiffon
vest were skilfully combined with a droopy black hat.
A perfectly elegant leg, daintily stockinged, and finished
somewhere about the knee with a lace-frill, was one of
the sights of the day. It was the property of a dame with
a brown hem to her robe, and was revealed as she fled in
haste to the telephone call-box, to ’phone hubby that she
was one of the lucky 22 who drew £2O 17s. on Joker.
As I write yet another social bomb bursts beneath my
feet. Miss Fannie Collins, she of the pale , golden halo
and dark brown eyes, fourth niece of the firm of John
Collins and Sons, Logan squatters, is to marry De Burgh
B. (Burgo), the second and very popular hope of De
Burgh Persse, of Tabrogalba.
Women in politics are becoming a seething mass in the
land. At every turn the wild she-leaguer is to be
hurrying to meetings, distributing unreliable tracts, or
button-holing voters. If the Boodle Party doesn’t retain
office it won’t be for the want of female agitators.
There are only three topics in Brisbane these dog-days
—the weather, the test match, and “Captain Jones,”
who lies slowly recovering from bronchial catarrh in the
Victoria Hospital. Of the three, Jones stands easily first.
The half-hour-progress wires of the test match, sent over
to him by the Waily Tale, have had such a marked effect
that the invalid is now confident of Christmassing in
Sydney.
Speech day at the Grammar School, on Friday, was
productive of some wholesome talk and at least one well-
deserved compliment, when the Premier promised the
utmost support to the establishment of a Queensland
universitjq and said that when that dream was realised,
the life work of Mr. R. H. Roe (for 20 years headmaster)
should be recognised by the offer of a professor’s chair.
The applause damaged the building so badly that tenders
have been called for repairs.
The Buchanan dame and her chicks are back at
“ Enerton,” from Sydney, and we are promised a double
wedding in June. Mrs. Buchanan declares that when
this function is over she will flit for ever from a State
where Mary Ann has died out. Her chicks, however,
will have a try at keeping house for a young rising
medical husband, and a ditto ditto barrister husband
respective!}', and hope, by a politeness that approaches
servility, to get a Mary Ann apiece occasionally.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
Last Monday, at Brisbane, the strong arm
of the law hanged August Millewski, a man with
a scraggy beard and retreating forehead, chiefly
on the information of his wife and son, for the
slaying of Wallum Nabby. Two years ago Wall am
Nabby was tried on a charge of burning Millewski’s
barn, and acquitted. Johnnie Do, from some-
where in Asia, gave evidence against Nabby, and
it proved unhealthy for him. When Johnnie
Do’s body was found it was almost past recog-
nition. Nabby was tried for murder, and
again his luck stood by him, for he was
acquitted a second time, in spite of a solid, popular
conviction that he had done the deep damnation
of Johnnie’s taking off. Had Millewski done his
crimes on his own he might also have got off, but
when he went forth at night and shot Nabby in
his bed, he took his wife to carry the lantern,
and his son to consult about the burial of the
remains, and these relatives turned dog on him.
He died urging the public to burn Mrs. Millewski
alive, and complaining of the bad ventilation of
the cap that is put over the head of the hangee in
his last moments.
“ M.P.” supplies a nice, cheerful dose, to
be taken before bed on December 25:
The bungling in the hanging of the youthful George
Toffts, at Tamworth (N.S.W.), calls to mind several
instances of bad hangmanship at Melbourne gaol. In one
case old Rowley, chief warder of the ’sixties, did a pen-
dulum act. When the condemned man got his last drop,
the knot didn’t adjust properly, so Rowley put his arms
about the suspended man, and swung with him until his
neck was broken. Then there was Howard, strung up
for a stabbing affair. When cut down, he was found to
be still breathing. The Governor was for giving him
another swing, but a cooler subordinate checked the pro-
ceedings, whereupon the poor victim, who had doubtless
been listening to the discussion, gave a couple of sniffs
and snuffed finally out. Landells, a big, soft, fleshy
fellow, got too long a drop which—ugh 1 Phelan, a tough,
muscular fellow, didn’t get sufficient rope, and prac-
tically strangled. But you can rely upon getting
much better service in Melbourne now. A register
is kept showing your height, weight, type of physique,
etc. The governor, medical officer, arid chief warder
hold a confab, and the victim is allotted his share of drop.
It only remains for the hangman to adjust the rope
accordingly. The heavier the subject the shorter the
drop. The rope is soft-soaped and stretched all night.
It is attached to the top tier with a large stone on
the end to take out the “give.” The hangman’s knot is
so arranged that it self-adjusts below the ear the moment
the “ bolt is drawn.” Pressmen and other onlookers,
under present conditions, merely get a glimpse of the
suspended man, then a curtain falls, and the drama is
ended —at all events as far as the public is concerned.
Nevertheless, the performance is sufficiently disclosed to
admit of keen criticism by an experienced pressman. For
instance, when Deeming was hanged at Melbourne Gaol,
his descent and decease were most carefully noted by the
late Mr. Spooner, who afterwards went to S’Africa as a
Boer war correspondent, and died there. Spooner hadi
come over from Sydney to writeup Deeming "details for
the Nooze. He knew his business. He watched the con-
demned man in his downward flight, and summed up the-
tragedy in three little words —“ Not a kick ! ”

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, December 16, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Wonder if the grim Scotch ghost of “ Johnny
Wallace watches the divorce court and sets his
face in horror at the gilded glad guineas piling
up for the lawyer folk each artfully protracted
day ! His personality was strong enough to
make his ghostly presence felt. Johnny Wallace
was a great broad fellow, grimly grey, with a
peculiar limp and a heavy stick. He’d plunk,
plunk, plunk a forceful way down Oollins-street.
“That’s Wallace,” busy men would say when
they heard the plunk. Wallace made history in
the old Ovens District. For 20 or 30 years
there was no one better known than he. In
every little mining centre Wallace planted a pub
and a store —a money-making combination. His
hotel was always called “ The Star.” The Beech-
worth (nee Ovens) district is studded with the
“Stars’ of Wallace. They were like a well-
regulated line of ships, and the careful Scot piled
up money in heaps. Wallace was never out of
the saddle—he’d ride over every inch of the
Ovens country, drawing canny threads from
every point. Everyone knew Wallace and his
jumper. Always the same dress—a red and
white striped soft shirt, corduroy breeches, and
long boots—and the Devil’s own seat in the
saddle.
Wallace always rode to Melbourne on his busi-
ness trips, and, though he owned so many pubs,
never wasted a bawbee at a Melbourne bar.. All
his store business—hardware and such things—
was done through M‘Ewan’s firm. Wallace in-
variably carried his swag on the saddle; when he
reached Melbourne he would put up his horse in
the M‘Ewan stables, at the back of the stores, and
climb up to the loft with his bluey for his own
rest. That was his custom for quite 20 years.
Later years Wallace rushed fiercely into mining.
He discarded the “jumper,” wore ordinary
clothes, and abandoned much of his Scotch caution.
Mining and experiments and patents and big ven-
tures simply clutched the man, and the huge
fortune was riddled and eaten. His first wife
was the big, handsome daughter of millionaire
“Tommy” Monaghan, and the old man’s money
was bequeathed to her children, with his son-in-
law as sole trustee. “Johnny” interpreted his
duties as trustee as including power to “do what
he jolly well liked,” and in the glamor of mining
enthusiasm, with a bitten investor’s dreams of
Golconda, the fortunes he held in . trust were
somewhat impaired—not much impaired, but a
little. His son Charley Wallace, the divorce
petitioner, states his pile at not more than six or
seven thousand pounds a year.
During the last work, while Mrs. Wallace was
igiving her evidence, the court was crowded from
imorn till adjournment hour. The lady affirmed
quite heroically. For three consecutive days
■she was in the box under the verbal fire of
a K.C. and a quintette of other famous lawyer
men. And during all that time she never
lost her self-control for an instant. Occa-
sionally under special stress she blushed or
blanched ; but no tears nor hysterics. It’s an
interesting study—the effect of one woman’s atti-
tude upon many men. Day by day one may
observe by subtle signs just how much and in
which direction the listeners are moved. Also
such observation points out the instability of
masculine regard. In Mrs. Wallace’s audience
there is seldom a settled opinion for any length of
time.
An amazing divorce is promised to those who
love to learn exactly how sinful their brothers and
sisters are. The co-re. will be the centre of
attraction. He is a plump, popular youth who
has looked at the world in a supercilious way,
from a high official social-post.
The Northcotes, before putting the furniture
into summer pinnies, gave a children’s dance-
full regalia of fancy dress for the youngsters, and
an At-Home for fond Mommers and. some
Poppers. It was a joyous spree for the kiddies,
anyhow. The Governor Lady has a genius for
entertainment, and the party was neatly stage-
managed, with an eye to avoiding any congestion
or slump in the entertainment. Punch banged
Judy and heaped indignities on the baby for
half-an-hour in the drawing-room, whilst the
youngsters shouted their appreciation. The visit-
ing Talbottle dame got as much fun out of the
sins of Punch as the kiddies did. What a girly
face she has ! If it were not for the grey hair she
would look 20 in profile.
Ruby Madden, beauteous in a short frock and
her hair in a knot, looked seventeen as she
romped. Baby Beatrice might have passed for
an elder sister. The Madden dame, pale and
quiet, talked Exhibition with the grey Janet
Missus. Among the nippers, Mrs. M‘Donald’s
little Cupid wore little more than some glorious
amethysts and a tawny skin. Dodo Chenery,
Mrs. George Chirnside’s niece, the prettiest girl
there, was in all white, with tan riding boots and
shooting gear, and a stockwhip wound round her
lithe little body.
Bibron, the adored of the dancing maiden, has
broken his bachelordom. The girl who made
him happy was Miss Kate Charlwood, a hand-
some Melbourne demoiselle with a clever press-
woman sister. The honeymooners have departed
to Charlwood, at Dromana, for the summer. The
bride was offered her choice of a brand-new house
or a trip to Paris. In sensible the
lady chose the tangible bricks and mortar.
Mr. Maclellan, boss of the Big Store, Prahran
(Melb.), and a partner, presumably, in the Foy
and Gibson business, belongs to the muscular
Christian sect. He favors exercises for the body
and the soul, and runs a girl-employees’ guild at
St. Kilda. It consists of some 300 members,
ranging from four years to 40, though the 40 is an
exception. The other week, he took his flock
along to St. Kilda Town Hall, and gave an invi-
tation display, the full brigade occupying the
floor, under the supervision of strong man Weber.
“Mac” himself works the pianola, whilst the
girleens juggle sticks and dumb-bells, and gene-
rally endeavor to improve their health. In a
game of basket ball they bucked in like football
teams in a final. As a show of physique, the
exhibition was first-iate. “ Mac,” as an apostle of
guild, drapery and religion, talks to his following
like a' parson and a father, and at the same time
holds up the practical end of the show with cash.
Apart from this big guild, there are some 16
others helping to train the Melbourne mothers of
the future in the way they should shape.
The true moral of Victoria’s latest clerical sen-
sation is that a wise Judge may have a foolish
wife.
The Green Ball last week was voted the best
that Mrs. G. and daughter Florrie have accom-
plished. In the darkened Town Hall —with good
music and limelight effects—the chicks and chick-
lings toed their well-trained gambols to an adoring
house of relatives. All the popular comic opera
ballets came in for miniature copy. The boy, Harry
Lazarus, who led the ballet in the “Blue Moon ’’
Crocodile dance is a born contortionist, but the
hard work over-taxed him, and he fainted—in
masculine earnest —when he was through with it.
At his next attempt his father watched him from
the ballroom with the anxiety of a footballer
awaiting a catch, or an old Homan emperor select-
ing a fat senator for killing purposes.
More pleasing than the prettiest dances were
the “ Sandow Girl ” movements of Maudie
Millett, who reproduced Fanny Dango from
“The Dairymaids” with an array of children
behind her. Maudie is a well-built blonde,
verging on the ’teens, with more physical-culture
about her than was apparent in her adult Dango
model. She has a turn for low comedy mimicry,
too, and can give a faithful copy of Little Cliff, an
imported boy performer one time a large item in
the bill at Rickards’.
The Arral got a faithful and clamorous house
for her adieu on Saturday night. “ There’s posi-
tively no one here, my dear,” sniffed one Society
dame to a kindred late-comer, but the hall was
fairly full of people, despite the scarcity of the
Mighty. Remnants of German opera scattered
round the balcony. They are all reappearing sud-
denly ; Miss Heinze spread her voluptuous
charms one side, with the Pinschof maidens for
company. Mamma Wiedermann Pinschof kept
papa Von at a safe distance in the opposite balcony.
Why the family scatter ? Slap and a scarlet-
clad daughter had a corner, and the Carlyle
Smythes viewed from aloft. Arthur Collins sat
solemnly through the concert with his youthful
fiancee. Hunger was to sing—but mysteriously
did not appear.
Mrs. Blanche acknowledge her warm welcome.
At her second appearace a head-light of a red
rose hit the eye and roused a feminine murmur,
“She’s changed her dress.” Mrs. Arral made a
triumph of her singing in something from “ Tra-
viata ” at the finale. She would sing like a bird
if she had less “ trickiness ” and purer technique.
Saturday saw Miss Leslie Tope change her name
to Mrs. Tommy Murdoch. The alteration was
made in the council chamber of South Melbourne
Town Hall. Afterwards, a reception and dance
in the large hall permitted a picturesque oppor-
tunity for congratulations. The little lady was a
delicious arrangement of sheeny white satin, silver
and soft, beautiful lace, and a superb Limerick
veil. After a rather original honeymoon tour, Mr.
Tommy and his brand new better-half will settle
down to domesticated romance in Hobart.
The Carty Salmons have invested in a town
house Fairholme, at South Yarra. Once estab-
lished in that pleasant mansion, Mrs. Salmon
intends to play hostess often and extensively—
particularly to her husband’s Parliamentary
associates and friendliest constituents. (Being
an acute matron, she’ll probably include a few
unfriendly ones, who may be liable to conversion,
before next elections.)
Miss Grace Palotta was in Melbourne for a few
days last week, on her way to Westralia, Mar-
seilles, Monte Carlo, and “the little mother.”
She gadded about none, and but few of her friends
knew that she was in the city. Rumor says that
the delightful actress will be back in Australia
within twelve months.
Louis Esson, the singer of queer clever songs
about slum folk, dead gods, and other interesting
and more or less proper people, went off to Ceylon
in the Moldavia last week. From there he goes
through India, and on to Thibet, Burmah, China,
Java, and Japan. And all he sees, hears, and
smells in these strange places is to be detailed in
future numbers of The Lone Hand.
Other Moldavia passengers were Mrs. Bigjohn
Forrest, whose emperor installed her in her cabin
and fled back to the tariff discussion ; Mrs. James
Gibb, and the J. A. Wallaces. Mountainous Mrs.
Wallace, in a feathery hat, came in for close in-
spection from strangers, who mistakenly thought
they saw in her the hapless lady who stars in
“ the case ” of the month.
The air is laden with feline plaints just now.
The Cats’ League is scratching for funds. It has
been indulging in more cream and fish (of doubt-
ful age) than it could well afford, and has had too
many sprees this year. The tardy rebellion of
rural Cats, and the League’s journalistic joke, are
other reasons for its money worries. Country mem-
bers, who don’t participate in the management of
the paper, and know nothing of the pleasures of
dooming “copy” to print or the waste-paper
basket, can see no reason for encouraging its
expensive existence. And they, for the most
part, know no more of the league’s festivities than
is published concerning them. Consequently the
cats whose homes are miles from the central
milk-dish have begun to withhold the shillings
which used to make things easy for the
gay metropolitan branch. So the league
has had to appeal to the pleasant hum-
bugs who were elected on its vote a
while back. The pleasant humbugs pain-
fully proffer small contributions, and invite the
league to lessen its appetite for cream and fish
(of doubtful age). The pleasant humbug is often
quite unpleasant when he’s asked to be genuine,
especially when asked for a genuine cheque
negotiable at his bank. And the league has been
inspecting its receipt books, and therein found
new cause for worry. One matron, unpopular as
any successful person could be, and pompous as a
Court orderly, has been practically bossing the
Cats of late ; yet she is discovered to be the con-
tributor of but one half-guinea since the league’s
inception. Another officialess had parted with
the impressive sum of ss. '
Lady Talbot’s first public reception at Stoning-
ton was on Monday, and took the form of a
crowded garden party. The garden deserved an
afternoon’s admiration to itself, by the way. It
was one radiant blaze of scarlet and green, with
here and there a cloud of pure pale lilies. The
Lady wore a supple gown of grev-blue panne,
with interjections of metallic embroideries; and
looked young and comely. Evidently a respite
from local Sassiety is a boon to all conscientious
martyrs who are broiled officially on a grid shaped
E.R.
The Federal pair, with a trio of aides, hap-
pened during the afternoon. Her Ex. was robed
in a grey garment, which hid itself beneath a cor-
duroy coat. Otherwise the clothing was incon-
spicuous and familiar. Mrs. Madden was in her
white lace frock ; Miss Leslie in matronly black ;
and the flower of the flock bloomed in green and
white muslin. With them was Irene Marks
in an infantile gown of grey ninon, and a
huge and vivid lid laden with green velvet.
Mrs. T. M. Maclnerney emphasised her fine
eyes with a violet-blue dress to match them, and
Mrs. Ernest Brookes crowned an exquisite lace
robe with a howling hat in harsh purple tones.
Some folk put pathetic energy into the up-
holding of appearances—-even if the tottering
props have to be held together with a bent nail
and a bit of string. It was a’Bus—a Malvern
’Bus—on the day of the Talbottle strawberry
hunt. Something hot and fat and pompous and
gold-laced hurried from the train and hailed the
trundler. It took some minutes to ship the im-
portant cargo, which stumbled over its sword and
tangled its gold tassels and its military records in
the doorway. Then the sleepy car got under way
again, and all the gossipy matrons, with their
respectable shopping baskets, had a gapeful of
delight at the gorgeous apparition. Further
delight came with its putting of the “tuppence”
into the slot, to the watchful accompaniment
of the all-seeing Eye that gazes through the peep-
hole from the Box. Pomposity took more gaps
out of the time-table schedule in dismounting
at “ Glenferrie Road.” Finally the ’Bus rumbled
away. Gold-lace smoothed his ruffled plumage,
straightened the ramrod of his back, marched
round a corner and importantly hailed a hansom.
Hansom and gold-lace completed the few yards
of remaining road and accomplished the vice-regal
drive with imperial dash. The story reminds one
of the taking song with which Grace Palotta won
Melbourne’s heart in the wayback days of “ A
Gaiety Girl.” “If you can’t afford a hansom-
take a ’bus,” was the Palotta maxim.
Another periodical made its appearance last
week—a quarterly budget, called The Heart op
the Rose. Its advent was celebrated by a dinner
at the Pension Fasoli, and all the world which
wears astounding neckties and flowing hair
was bidden. The wife of that world,
as usual, stays at home to mind the
baby; but its best girl and sister put
on their greenery-yalleriest draperies, and sat
through the proceedings with much unction.
Most women are Bohemian with difficulty. The
room wherein the soulful gathering ate mncaroni
and garlic salads, was decorated gorgeously with
flaming, scented red roses, and Mr. Bernard
O’Dowd. All the poets, and painters, and respect-
able writers, and tolerable art patrons, and sculptors
who are available in Melbourne w'ere on show as
well. After they’d eaten largely, they listened to
the singing of soft and sentimental ballads, and
to the speeches of certain mutual admirers. Dr.
Wm. Maloney made some extremely graceful
remarks about everything. David Wright did
likewise with charming effect. Fred MacG’ubbin
gave a bowdlerised account of his innocent
adventures abroad. Then the poet O’Dowd
talked intimately of Hell and Heaven, and the—er—
Hesperides. No one understood him, but they
applauded him enthusiastically. You see,
mystics—and that’s what readers and writers of
the Rose must be—never need know what they
or the other chaps are talking about. Indeed, a
grasp of any subject shows a gross materialism,
from their point of view. For the rest, Thomas
Bakhap, our best authority on Chinese literature,
A. T. Strong, Mrs. Colles (who versifies as
“ A.8.C.”), T. P. Maclnerney, E. J. Brady and
another distinguished bard in prunella boots,
and witty Jeremias Josephi, relieved the nebulous
mystic gloom which hung over the occasion.
And publisher Lothian and Douglas Hart made
jokes.
From “ Rosna,” of Adelaide :
Those frugal people who have been holding off in the
hope of a popular-priced Butt concert, got what they
wanted in the end—in moderation. The Taits, having
achieved a fine for overcrowding in Perth, took discretion
by the fetlock, and hired the old Exhibition barn for
their final distribution of melody in Adelaide—prices 7s.
6d., 55., and 3s. The place was thronged, including six
rows of a specially-built gallery, and the audience went
off its joint and several head with enthusiasm. The
great Clara wore silver, a wonderful garment with a
flounce of lace worth the ransom of the Peishwa of
Poona, or the Maharajah of the Mahrattas at Satara, or
even the Ahkoond of Swat (at a push). And she carried
the indispensable bouquet of carnations.
Michael and Patrick broke in upon the guileless Andrew
Mack at the Theatre Royal, last week, and made him the
usual presentation between acts, tellinghim what a broth
of a bhoy he is and always will be. Miss Mae Stevenson,
leading lady to the co. is new to this village, and shows
herself a somewhat attractive and frocky little party.
In fact, even a Mack show is a blessed relief to a State
beset by troops of Church bazaars, and overrun by shoals
of strawberry fetes.
But now we have achieved a two-act opera. It is called
“The Rose Maidens,” and is bestrewn with earls. The
music is by a blind musician, O. Brown, and the words by
A. Bernat. It was performed at Norwood in a thronged
edifice the other evening, to the tune of great applause.
For their hilarious spree at the Theatre Royal on 21st,
the undergrads have secured the services of “ The
Players,” under Percy Walshe, as an antidote to their
own exquisite foolery. Fragments of “ The Lady of
Lyons ” will help lure the elusive mite and obolus and
drachma from the pockets of the populace.
Sassiety is getting into the habit of handing out the
buttered scone at a municipal kiosk, which has just
sprung up at the end of the Glenelg pier. The thing is
three-storeyed ; the eatables dwell on the ground floor,
and cards, smoking and other furious varieties of sport
may be pursued upstairs, while salt sea breezes are flung
in free of charge. Some energetic folk gave a huge
Euchre at this kiosk, t’other evening, the guests being
carted back to the city in drags after they had got tired
of asking one another what were trumps, and foolishly
taking up the left bower.
Apropos of gambling, I have been informed that there
are 60,000 people wandering homeless around Europe
through not leading trumps at the right moment, and
that there is £975,000 lying in the cellars of the civilised
world through the coin rolling down the grating after
somebody had “ tossed up.”
S. Aus. Rhodes Scholar, Roy Lister Robinson, has just
captured a B. A. degree and a Baroness Burdett-Ooutts
scholarship at Oxford. The youth has a brilliant record
behind him. During his Adelaide days he chased a B.Sc.
degree to the death, and ran to earth every available
School of Alines diploma. But, alas ! these brilliant jmuths
don’t come back. Adelaide’s first “ Rhodes,” Norman
Jolly, has joined the Indian Forestry Department. Yet
another local savant, E. Jordan, B.Se., who has been
teaching the young idea of Sc. Peter’s College to spell cat,
goes off to the U.S., as lecturer in Applied Hieroglyphics
or Musical Botany, at Leland Stanford University. He’s
a crack tennis player too, which makes it all the harder
to part.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

The U.S. A. has become convinced that Australia
badly needs the particular sort of tubing it produces.
Wherefore Mr. R. H. Herveyis here on beh .If of the United
Flexible Metallic Tubing Go., Ltd., pushing a new and
exceptional brand of the article. It is made from copper
or galvanised steel tape, in one continuous length, rolled
spirally, so that the edges are made to interlock. The
tape is so rolled as to form a groove, in which a packing'
is inserted. The tubing is from Jin. to lOin. internal
diameter, and its owners claim it will carry steam at all
pressures up to 3001 b. per square inch. I’s good stuff—
the sort in fact that, under a rational fiscal system, we
should have long ago been making for ourselves.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Of course it can’t be true that Philp, the
Queensland Premier who refused to resign though
presented with six consecutive no-confidence
motions, thinks of grabbing the chance to lift up
an old political pal to the Supreme Court Bench.
Certain fools of some importance are start-
ing a Peace Society in Australia, and propose to
preach the doctrine of disarmament to a country
which already has hardly anything to defend
itself with, save road-metal. If the Peace Push
would try its eloquence upon Japan, it would be
more to the point. If the lamb and the wolf are
to cksarm, it is always best for the wolf to disarm
first.
DEAKIN EXPLAINS.
DEAKIN (to Out-of-work): “It's like this. One
finds you a job, and the other sees that
you're paid for it.”
Ex-Minister Kenneth Mackay has been
gazetted as Colonel commanding a newly-created
“ Intelligence Corps,” which is “ to help the De-
fence Department in connection with topography,
the preparation of maps, information with regard to
the country, transport, and various other matters.”
The most remarkable thing about the new branch
of the Defence service, though, is that it is “ not
to be subject to military discipline ” ; which means
that if a spy manages to coax Colonel Mackay to
enrol him, and is caught giving wilfully false and
misleading information, he cannot be hanged or
shot. In return for that concession, these men
who are much too fine to be soldiers are to work
for nothing. Presumably the motto of the new
corps will be—“No paying; no potting.”
A Committee of N.S.W. Assem-
bly, now taking evidence, shows the
danger of letting in the private rail-
way co. Years back, before Sydney
knew what far-western N.S.W. was
stuffed with, Parliament let a co. build
a line to connect Broken Hill and the
S. Aus. border. Since then, in one year,
the co. has paid as much as 81 per
cent, in dividends. But as the State
can only take over the line by paying
for it on the basis of profits earned,
State purchase has been turned down
as out of the question (the State would
have to pay about 12 times the cost
of the line); so even the local Labor
party is now driven to barrack on be-
half of another syndicate, which pro-
poses to build a competing line. The
party has to either swallow its prin-
ciples or put up with freights which
mean 80 per cent. divs. ; so it swal-
lows its principles, and tries to look
happy—and can’t. Though why not
propose a State-constructed competing
line?
Any old lie is good enough for
the Brisbane Tory papers these election
times. On December 7 the Courier published this
from its Charleville correspondent:—
Many electors who were at the last elections staunch
supporters of Mr. Kidston have become disgusted with
his late actions in conjunction with the Labor-Socialists,
and will now support the Government candidate.
And there wasn’t a Kidstonite candidate for the
Charleville seat (YVarrego) last election, nor one
who looked anything like a Kidstonite !

When the House of Reps, had finished
struggling with the piano problem it fixed the
duties at 25 per cent, on British and 30 per cent,
on non-British imported pianos. As the German
piano, which the importer person sells at £5O or
so, arrives per invoice at from rather under £l5 to
rather under £l9, it will only, after all, pay some-
thing like £4 ss. to £0 duty. The ravenous importer
can pay that out of his 300 per cent profit without
turning a hair.
Things move a little, and even the watched
heretic burns at last. South Australia has con-
sented to the transfer of the Northern Territory
to the Commonwealth, and now it is up to the
Commonwealth to grasp the Lost Province with
both hands, to build in haste a broad-gauge trans-
continental line fit for swift express traffic, and to
get a great land settlement policy going so sud-
denly that this country won’t know where it is.
Customs Minister Chapman lately received
a deputation “in private.” It was a Jap deputa-
tion, come to make a small brown moan about
haying to disclose, per Customs declaration, some-
thing like the real value of its importations.
Heaven knows how this piece of compulsory
honesty affected the brown importer, assuming
that the brown importer is honest; but still Chap-
man promised, as usual, to consider the request.
Presumably that is all he will do; yet, if he
doesn t want to become the victim of unpleasant
insinuations, he will speedily drop the practice of
receiving private deputations. There are plenty
of foreign-trade parasites who will be only too
eager to jump at the chance of maligning the
Customs administration, and those secret deputa-
tions give them the very opportunity they want.
The Federal Statist has been wrestling
with figures concerning Tommy Tanna, the
Federal work of whose re-
patriation will be completed
by February. Some 1400,
who have been granted ex-
emptions, will remain in
Australia as a legacy of the
unwholesome trade in black
labor. The first draft were
induced to come to Aus-
tralia by Ben Boyd, who
used them in and about
Eden as shepherds. But
they took more kindly to
rum and riot, and were
repatriated. The greedy
sugar - grower, who had
visions of the slavery of
Uncle Sam’s cotton fields,
found room for Tommy in
Bananaland. This was in
1868, and blackbirding,
otherwise kidnapping, was
so rife that regulating legis-
lation was passed. Tommy
continued to be an article
of trade for 36 years. During
that term the 81 licensed
vessels employed brought to and fro no less than
60,000 Kanakas, at a cost of £618,000. Of that
number 40,000 were repatriated. No less than
12,987 left their bones in Australian soil. It was
the most shameful enrichment that soil ever had.
When the Commonwealth Government
notified the Hobart Golf Club that it wanted a
slice of the club’s links for defence purposes, that
cheeky body, seeing a fine chance to bleed its
country, demanded, between “haws,” £4500 for the
bit of soil. Deakin and Co. offered £1230, which was
a good deal more than the unimproved value of the
paddock. The club waved its cleek and said it
would see the Commonwealth and Australia’s
defence in a putt or a foursome or something before
it would abate one penny of its claim. After
Crown Solicitor Powers had worn himself out
arguing the point, he took the matter to the High
Court; and Justice Higgins speedily decided that
£1230 was a very fair offer, and ordered the club
to pay all Costs. Seeing that the Commonwealth
is actually buying that land in order to prepare to
protect the Hobart Golf Club and its links from in-
vasion and disaster, it would appear that they
grow choice samples of other things beside apples
in Tasmania—“ hide,” to wit. A stiff progressive
land-tax would do people like that no end of good.
TOO MUCH COUNTER ATTRACTION.
Why the Women's Exhibition could not last
another week.
The days of Reid’s rail-painting policy for
his unemployed are recalled by an incident which
has incensed the .Sydney Trades Hall. Some 50
“free laborers” have been engaged by the
Government to do some necessary painting at the
Newington Asylum at much less than the Union
rates of pay. The excuse of the officials is that
the men need money for the Christmas season.
Because they need money, in short, they get very
little. The same Government officers would look
quite hurt if it were pointed out that a lot of
people would gladly do their jobs at half rates for
the sake of some money at Christmas time.
“ Billy T.” : I clip this from the columns
of a Melbourne weekly, which is reflecting on the
recent coal strike. It appears to me as absolute
and perfect ignorance reduced to its smallest
possible literary dimensions :
It is strange how enthusiastic the poor can be on the
subject of the umvorthiness of the rich. Tne poor rich,
who make the workers possible 1
The rich who make the workers possible ! I defy
any other competitor to make so big an ass of
himself in so small a space. Think of the capi-

giving him a job at 20s. a week 1
The bluff of the Victorian Fat Landlords
met its match once while they w T ere wrecking the
Factories Amending Bill. Laborite Evans was
protesting against Harwood’s amendment to
exempt shops from the Minimum Wage clauses,
and said that “some of the biggest men in the
city would hang their heads in shame if the wages
they paid were known.” A few rash voices
called “Names! Names!” Evans promptly
looked at his notes, as though he were about to
publish the awful information, when yells of
“No! No! No names!” smothered him, and
Harwood withdrew his amendment in a hurry.
Premier Bent already has plans out for a
subdivision sale of the land whereon Melbourne
gaol stands. If Bent hadn’t the mania for land
dealing, he would lease those blocks, and the
rents would every year snip off something percep-
tible from the public debt. And the further it
went, the bigger would be the snip; for land that
will bring in £5OOO a year now may easily return
thrice as much in 30 years’ time. But then there
would be no beautiful surplus to splash around,
and Bent does love his surplus, and the splash he
makes with it is like a waterfall.
There is already talk about the identity of
N.S. W.’s next expensive imported (State Governor.
According to the precedent of the Canadian
Federation, Australia should be done with im-
ported State Governors, and should content itself
with cheap local Lieutenant-Governors, appointed
by the Federal authority ; but Premier Wade
yearns, as Carruthers did, for the figurehead from
England. The Commonwealth gets its figurehead
from England, and, if the State doesn’t do like-
wise, “ State Bights” will be all busted up. The
Carruthers push would import suburban mayors
from Eugland if it thought that “ State Rights ” t
w r ould gain anything by the proceeding.
Maloney, M.H.R., proposes to submit to
the Australian Parliament a motion as long as
himself. Among other things, he desires that the
roll of members shall be called at the beginning of
each sitting, at every call for a quorum, after each
adjournment, and at the close of each sitting.
Every member who is found to be absent when
the roll is called or when a division takes place,
unless such absence is caused by illness, is to be
fined £l. If this scheme were put into operation,
how would George Reid fare ? It would take all
those “ Big Notes ” that the boy gave him in the
bathing box to pay a month’s tines.
Talking of the State properties transferred
to the Commonwealth, Treasurer Waddell last
week told N.S.W, Assembly the old, unreliable
story that—
The Federal Government was not paying interest
charges on the loan moneys expended in the erection of
the buildings, nor upon the capital value of the properties.
During the last five years the Commonwealth has
returned to N.S.W. its statutory three-fourths of
the Customs and Excise revenue and an average
of £348,782 a year besides. That is enough to
pay interest on £9,005,000 worth of transferred
properties. Yet the mean, whining, little
Twaddell goes round complaining that he doesn’t
get any interest.
Wilks, of Balmain, isn’t the only Free-
trader who grabs all the Protection he can get for
industries in his own electorate. In the glass
duties debate Edwards, of Queensland, voted like
clockwork to have the imposts wiped out or re-
duced, until the item “scientific apparatus”
came along. The Government had placed these
articles on the free list, and the fact made
Edwards ramp. He presented a petition from
some Brisbane people, and furiously demanded a
duty. Then Page, who also had been doing his
little bit to butcher the glass industry, in so far as
the slaughter didn’t harm his State, pleaded with
Lyne to “give them a square deal, and help an
established Queensland manufacturer.” Then
Lyne gave Edwards the brassy laugh, and re-
marked that, as Edwards had voted all day to
destroy the glass industry, he could now have the
whole hog. But for the shameful lack of
patriotism shown by Page and Edwards in voting
to destroy all the industries of the other States,
they would 1 probably have got consideration for
their own industry, but the temptation to give
them the Freetrade they had been demanding for
everybody else was too strong.
“ N.” on strikes :
The comfortable world at large fails to perceive the
growing and terrible significance of strikes. A new era
began with a comparatively little one, at M'Oormick’s
Agricultural Implement Works, Chicago, in 1886. It led
to some loss of life, in resisting the police. Therefore a
public outdoor meeting was held, presided over by the
mayor. It passed off in good order, and the mayor went
away. Then somebody, never discovered, flung a bomb,
which exploded in the midst of a large company of
police, killing several and wounding many. A great
number of anarchists were arrested. Ultimately
four were hanged in 1887. Three were kept jit
prison till 1893, when Governor Altgeld released them,
declaring that there had been no evidence even against
the men who were hanged. It is remarkable that
three chief leaders in the London Dock Strike, 1889—
Mann, Tillett, and Champion—met recently on one Mel-
bourne platform. This strike succeeded through a sub-
scription of £30,000 from Australia. It may just be
recalled how the so-called Australian maritime strike
of 1890 originated with shearers up the bush.
In 1892 was the Broken Hill strike, also that in
Carnegie’s Homestead Steel Works at Pittsburg (U.S. A.).
A peculiarly momentous affair this. Carnegie and Co.
brought in a sort of little army of their own, formed of
Pinkerton’s detectives, to protect non-unionists. The ’tecs
were armed with rifles. The strikers also took up rifles.
A downright battle ensued, with seven killed, and about
30 wounded. The Chicago Pullman car strike (1894)
not only demonstrated again how easily a civil war could
be kindled, but brought the pinch on the general com-
munity. The 24 railroads centring in Chicago were held
up. Next came the pinch with the coal, through the
intensely dangerous anthracite strike of 1902, settled In
Roosevelt, almost by force, as one may say. Yet none o'
these terrors were intrinsically so serious as the Victoria)
railway strike, because that was directed against a
Government, and success appeared to hang in the balance
for a day. I have been informed, upon authority t.la
value of which I cannot tell, that Premier Irvine was not,
the cold, frozen, glassv-eyed man in that case, and that, he
would have given way but for Bent, Railway Minister,
and Attorney-General Davies.
DIABOLUS— THE DEVIL.
Diabolo, it is reported, has invaded the Vatican.
Cardinal Merry del Val, who is an enthusiastic sportsman,
has ordered a diabolo set.
And a very good game for the church , too.
The Victorian Legislative machine is only
a two-speed car; it must either crawl or make a
frantic rush. It being desirable to get the
Licensing Act Amending Bill passed before the
annual licensing courts sat, Bent dashed down
the measure on the Assembly table on November
26, and it was rushed through and sent to the
Rich Landlords early on the 27th. The Land-
lords took one swift glance at it, and sent it back
on the 28th. Governor Talbottle was just leaving
for Gippsland, but Jenkins went in pursuit with
his tail streaming in the wind, and caught him at
Malvern. Between there and Caulfield an Ex r-
ecutive Council meeting was held on the train —
Bent, Talbottle, and Boyd being present —and the
measure was signed. Then the lawyers looked at
it, and said they saw about 14 holes each big
enough to let a herd of elephants pass through,
with Costs.
THE PRIMARY PRODUCER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He’s the backbone of the country, and it’s generally
known
He’s its muscle and its marrow, and its sinew and its
bone.
He’s its legs and arms, and whiskers ; he’s its eyes and
ears, and nose,
And the hardened shiny substance on its fingers and its
toes.
He’s its heart and lungs and liver; he’s the blood that
floweth red,
And its skin and teeth and stomach, and the hair upon
its head.
Tho’ I know it’s sacrilegious for a person to express
The contrary opinion in the columns of the press,
Yet I’d like to ray these farmer chaps can’t honorably
claim
To be the Prime Producers, and get credit for the same.
For I’ve never met a squatter yet, nor tiller of the soil,
Who made his own machinery or implements of toil.
He doesn’t make'the boots he wears, his pants, or shirt,
or hat,
Nor does he grow the stump-jump plough, or little things
like that.
His horses are not shod by God, nor did kind Providence
Produce the wire-wove netting that he puts around his
fence.
And the busy hands that cleared his lands I know were
not possessed
By disembodied spirits from the regions of the blessed.
I’ve never been where drills are seen suspended on the
vine,
Nor do I know where waggons grow, or bags, or binder
twine.
’Tis understood, tho’ God is good, lie left some things
undone,
And I’ve no doubt at all but that the harvester was one.
And the patent separator—tho’, of course, He knew
its use,
Was left for some “ consumer ” in the city to produce.
The farmer hops into the shops for what he may require ;
Perhaps a rule, or other tool —some nails or screws, or
wire. ’
With swift intent, his men are sent, like blazes, to the
town,
To find a handy tradesman, when his “cutter’s” broken
down.
There’s not, in fact, about the farm, a thing that wasn’t
made
By a primary producer in his own particular trade !
W. A.
TOM BEASLEY.

“ THE LIGHT OF THE WORLD.’*
The sensation caused by the exhibition of that famous
picture, “ The Light of the World,” by Holman Hunt,
which is still fresh in the memory of the public, has in-
duced A. Saunders, the well-known jeweller of 809 George
Street, Sydney, to specially procure from his London
house a large number of “Garden of the Soul ” prayer
books, with an embossed reproduction of the famous
picture in sterling silver on the cover, as depicted above.
This reproduction, when compared with the original, was
admitted by experts to be excellent. The size of the
book is 3fin. x 2|in., gilt edges with fine, clear type, and
is morocco leather finished. The price is 7s. 6d. and can
only be obtained from A. Saunders, 809 George Street,
who will send it post free to any address. We would
suggest one of these unique prayer books as an appro-
priate Christmas gift for a friend.
