SPORTING
NOTIONS
Some 15,000 people yielded a£sso “gate ” first
day (Friday) of first test match at Sydney Cricket Ground.
Visitors’ innings opened direfully. Skipper Fane, whose
win of the toss was England’s sixth successive correct
call, had only made two before being caught by Trumper
off Cotter. Gunn (the Notts, pro.), who had been stowed
away in wadding somewhere, and whose presence in Aus-
tralia was unknown to a big section of cricket goers,
followed and provided a huge surprise. Young went
back to the pavilion with the figures two for 18. Already
lightning bowler Cotter had demonstrated excellent
form ; two wickets for 14 stood to his credit. But, by
and bye, the averag-e broke up badly, especially
through his treatment at Gunn’s hands. That bats-
man was also the only one who attempted to paste Arm-
strong. At luncheon the board read three for 106. Time
to resume had passed ten minutes before the actors in
the show appeared again, and subsequent inquiry
elicited that one Australian had struck because
some notice on the door of the room was re-
moved by an association official. The innings closed
for 273 —towards which Gunn brought 119 (embracing
twenty fours, and accomplished in 2£ hours of sparkling
and varied play), before being caught by Hazlitt off
Cotter. All Gunn’s strokes were heavily charged with
power and capitally timed, and when the ball didn’t
go direct to someone in the field it was an almost sure
four. Hutchings and Gunn put on 73 ere the for-
mer left, and Braund and Gunn were together 80min.
for 100. One of Cotter’s catapultic deliveries introduced
a sensation by smashing Barnes’s off stump, and Cotter
pocketed £5 (the gift of an advertising firm) when five
wickets for 95 were opposite his name. Cotter (six) and
Armstrong (three) did the damage. England’s unpro-
mising beginning was followed by a grand effort, then a
complete collapse—mainly through the wicket becoming
sun-baked, and more suited to Cotter’s tactics. The
Britishers lost two for 18, and three for 200, and remain-
ing seven for an additional 73. The Australians’ begin-
ning was worse than that of- t’other side. McAlister and
Trumper opened, and McAlister went pavilionwards,
caught for two, with the total at four ; but at stumps-
draw 50 had accumulated without further loss—3l of
them off Trumper’s bat.
Thousands looked ruefully at the condition of
things when Trumper (bowled off the pad) only added 12
to his overnight score, and full total was 72; but when
Hill and Noble got together they piled up 92. Hill showed
the beauties of the game; albeit the side’s captain
(Noble) notched quite as quickly. Noble was operating
lhr. 18min. for 37, and hit four fours, but his play lacked
the polish of the second N.S.W.-S.A. innings. At 50 Hill
had won an advertising £l, and for every subse-
quent run he got 6d. Thus were the bowlers hit for
“ bobs ” and “ two bobs,” according to the crowd,
till 87 showed. Then Gunn caught Hill luckily, as Braund
did Noble before, and a perfect innings ended. The S.
Australian’s compilation was built up in 2hr. 26min.,
and included eight fours. The two left-handers of the side
(Hill and Ransford) had made exactly half (111) the total
runs so far. Play was dull and weighty, and compul-
sorily so, as every run had to be battled for hard. With
Trumper, Noble, Hill, and Armstrong gone, and the
score 222 for six wickets, every succeeding bats-
man faced the high-strung conditions, and re-
solved to take as few chances as possible, but
they were all done with just inside five hours from the
start of the innings, and with the total 300. Fielder
bowled remarkably—a perfect length—and was never
really mastered, though 33 runs were against him before
he got one wicket. The Englishman’s “ off theory ” had
the Australians troubled frequently, and ably aided by
men in the slips, he bagged six for 82—four of them
caught beautifully by Braund. Turnstiles showed an
attendance of 32,000, and the treasurer reported the
day’s takings as £1470.
•** * *
Happenings of Monday made it plain that the
Britishers are not a power as a batting combination, also
that they have a palpable caudal appendage. With only
three out for 218, nine succumbed for 293, leaving the
side in almost exactly the same position as on Friday,
and, but for fairly heavy rain at night, Aus-
tralia's prospects looked at lease healthy. Some-
where in the neighborhood of 12,000 people watched
cricket that was dreary, though here and there specially
good Strokes stood out from the weighty monotony.
Cotter’s bowling gav.e little trouble, as a rule, but Arm-
strong forced respect always ; eight of his 13 overs were
maidens, and only eight runs were scored off the others.
Rhodes had 59min. of life, during which he notched 29 in
a quiet, unobtrusive sort of way. Fane’s 33 were put
together in lhr. 44min., and but one four figured in the
list. Hardstaff (already dubbed “Hardstuff” by “hill”
patrons) toyed with Cotter’s deliveries, and put some
sorely-needed vigor into the play, though but for Cotter’s
absolute neglect to grasp an easy situation Hardstaff’s
innings (which closed at 63, made in lhr. 38min.)
should have ceased at 47. Hardstaff brought great power
to bear in his strokes. The Gunn-Hardstaff partnership
added 113. Gunn showed the same brilliancy and sturdy
shoulder that his side’s initial innings disclosed, and but
for a difficult catch (missed by McAlister at 47) he never
gave the field a chance. The day ended with the English-
men 266 to the good, and Braund and Fielder
at the wickets. Up to a late stage Cotter
had sent down 37 overs before getting" a wicket
(Gunn’s); he had one for 72 and two for 83, and
101. Noble bagged a second wicket in nine overs—four
of them maidens. Two of his three catches were par-
ticularly good. Saunders had the batsmen guessing
often, and four for 68 says much for his work. Con-
cerning the job set the Australians on Tuesday, the
perambulating cricket record tells that in only one test
match has 300 been made for a fourth innings, and then
when Australia scored 316 for six wickets against
Maclaren’s eleven at Adelaide five years ago. As regards the
story of that Tuesday, it is brief and painful. The English-
men made another seven, and closed with 300—exactly
the record of the Australians in their first innings. Then
Australia swam to the wicket. Trumper made three, and
was bowled by Barnes. Hill made one, and was bowled
by Fielder. Macartney was out for nine. Noble was
holding the fort with 27, and Armstrong was with him on
the raft, and keeping up his end of the shipwreck with
17, when the rain finally washed the game away for the
day. The Bulmtin then went to press.
* * * *
“Adelaide”: This peculiarity of S.A. cricket
becomes almost uncanny as the seasons go by, and still it
never fails. Somewhere, somehow, S.A. wins a match
every season—and never more than one. Almost always
the win is gained against all probability—for the Adelaide
Oval has not seen the S.A. team win a game this century !
This time, N.S.W. has suffered. Before that, for four
seasons Victoria went down always in Melbourne.
Before that, it was N.S.W. again, and when for two
separate years it was neither of them, an English team
came along to get a surprise and a beating. But not since
1894-5 (the season after its solitary holding of the Sheffield
Shield) has S.A. won more games than the one in any
season.
* * * *
The Fijians disappointed with the poorness of
a swimming display at Waverley Club’s Carnival, and
gave a flat contradiction to the hair-raising tales told of
their alleged marvellous performances in the water.
Hundreds of Sydney schoolboys could give them points
and a bad beating; but they made some amends at
cricket. Filing on to the Sydney Cricket Ground the
team showed the physique of wrestlers and the vanity of
women or Roman emperors. Muscular shoulders and
arms, powerful necks and chests and hips were gaily
bedecked with beads, flowers, seaweed, and other inde-
scribable things, while bunches of flowers ornamented the
doormat-like heads of several. The preliminary war-cry
was not a circumstanoe to the ordinary every-
day whoop of a healthy Rugby fifteen. As the
afternoon wore on, sections of the general adornment of
each were discarded until the littered field suggested the
emptying out of a theatrical property room,' and some
stood up clothed only in the briefest of white knickers,
and the already-referred-to thick woollv mops. Fijian
fielding was keen indeed—three or four scooted after each
ball—and returns were usually accurate and speedy.
Every dismissal was the signal for something in the
nature of a war dance. Nine wickets for 410 caused heaps
of leather-chasing, and the N.S.W. innings (finished next
day) totalled 440. The brown men’s effort produced 132,
and when those figures had been brought into existence
by N.S.W. with one wicket down, the match ended.
Ratu Pope is the top-rung bat of the combination—he
belts them well on both sides, and drives capitallv. Ratu
Kadavu has good defence, and Liceni is the 'mighty
smiter of the lot.
* • * *
The abandonment, last week, of the match
England v. an Australian XI. at Brisbane looked a decided
stroke of luck for the Britishers. Australia had only two
wickets down in its second innings, and was then 186 to
the good. Possibly t’other side might have pulled through
—many more unlikely things occur at cricket now and
again— but IS6 would have been a big start on a rain-
soaked wicket, and Heaven only knows what the start
might have grown to if the match had gone on.
* * * *
N.S.W. side to meet Victoria on Boxing and
succeeding days has been chosen. Among the notable
missing cricketers are Blaxland, Hopkins, and Diamond.
Diamond’s latest form justifies his exclusion, though he
did make 138 and 210 in inter-State matches last year,
but Hopkins’ 60 odd against S. Australia should have
opened the door wide. The limelight of big cricket will
be on Sid Gregory once more, and R. N. Hickson has the
opportunity hundreds of admirers have been clamoring
* * * *
Selected second eleven of N.S.W. to meet
Queensland reps, includes junior Whitty, of whom much
is expected. Left-handed, his delivery is high, but with-
out apparent effort, and, for a log-break bowler, he gets
in eye-opening pace from the pitch.

From Sydney Evening News of Monday last:
“ The racing pony Rose Amber dropped dead this
morning. A post-mortem revealed the fact that the
intestines were partially eaten away through the use of
certain drugs.” A volume in a few words.
* * * *
What backers of horses have to endure has
rarely been more tellingly evidenced than in True Light’s
running, at Moorefield, last Saturday week, and Rosehill
last Saturday. True Light won the big Moorefield event
so easily that he became a strong tip, and was largely
backed in doubles for A.J.C. Summer Cup on Boxing
Day. Consequently, Rosehill Handicap looked a cer-
tainty for him, and big parcels of coin were unloaded into
Ikey Mo’s satchel till better than 5 to 4 was unobtainable.
But the nag badly disappointed his armv of backers, and
the race went to Royal Star (a 12 and'ls to 1 chance).
This steed had previously won twice in Victoria, and has
6st. 121 b. in Villiers Stakes (next Saturday), 6st. 101 b. in
Summer Cup, and 6st. 71b. in Tatt.’s Cup (New Year’s
Day).
» * * *
Melbourne Age published the other day a long
letter from “ Owner and Trainer,” protesting against the
.action of the V.R.C. in refusing to register Jack Wren’s
racecourse at Ascot. The letter breathed the spirit of
Wren’s “ fight ’’ advertisements, and was full of fine demo-
cratic concern for those poor toilers of the turf who live
by providing “sport” for hard-swearing citizens. It
presented the writer in an attitude of melodramatic
appeal to the amphitheatre, with the limelight flooding
him, and rugged candor written on his countenance.
“ We are owners and trainers battling for bread and
butter—it would be cowardice to attempt to disguise
that fact,” he said, patting himself on the back for his
brave confession of the obvious. “ Racing is my
trade—l know no other—and is it not natural
that I should earnestly desire to see opened
up a wider and richer field in which I might
follow the trade to my own advantage, and to the
detriment or injury of no other man. . . . Horses are to
the favored few but expensive play toys ; to us they are
bread-winners, and it seems to us to be an act of injustice
that man and horse should be compelled to remain in
idleness when thousands of pounds are being competed
for at our very doors”—meaning that once a yeaA £2OOO
stake is put up for a pony race at Wren’s Ascot meeting.
The bitter cry of Wren’s barracker included a lot more
“guff,” utterly at variance with what was said at an
“Owners and Trainers’” social gathering night awhile
ago, when one of the speakers complained Chat a large sum
of profit made out of privately-owned race meetings was
lost to the Turf, whereas it would go to benefit owners if
it went into the treasuries of genuine racing clubs.
As a matter of fact, all proprietary meetings—whether
for “ponies” or full-grown gees—are undeserving of
encouragement. The proprietors personally fatten on
the success of the show. A racing club adds its surplus
to the stakes, but the individual Boss adds his surplus to
his banking account. And as for unregistered meetings
in their other sporting aspects, they are bad in principle.
The Boss of the show is the employer of the handicapper,
judge, starter, and every paid servant on the ground, and
when, as in Wren’s case, he is also (through an agent)
the boss bookmaker, his control of the business is prac-
tically unchecked. To say that Wren’s pony meetings
are run on business-like lines—that glaring malpractices
are punished, and the management is smart and brisk—
is not to offer a satisfactory reason for registering his race-
courses. If they were more decently and honestly conduct-
ed than the meetings of the V.R.C. or A.J.C. the objection
to the principle of personal proprietorship would not be
lessened. Whether the races be thoroughly sporting events
or despicable robberies of the uninformed public, it is the
paying public who provide the bulk of the money. The
larger the amount given in stakes the greater the chance
of honest racing—the better the sport. The support of
the main body of occasional racegoers—as distinguished
from regular followers of the game—is essential to the
biggest race meetings, and it is not conceivable that tens
of thousands of people would again and again pay 10s.
for admission to the grand-stand at meetings controlled
by a Boss who places the bulk of the profits to his own
credit.
* » * *
A third-rate gee-gee named Avalon was gathered
to its fathers the other day, and this matter of no conse-
quence was recorded in Melbourne Age, at the length of
a full quarter column, under bold cross headings A
Notable Racehorse—Death of Avalon.” To the large
majority of Age readers the quarter-column obituary
notice of Avalon would seem a tribute to its achieve-
ments ; to many followers of racing it looked simply
absurd; by a few “in the know ” it was accepted
as an advt. for the animal’s owner. Whereby hangs
an interesting suggestion. The 12-year-old Avalon ran in
183 races, mostly petty events, and won 23 of them, being
unplaced in 118 others—as it appears from Age statistics.
“Like old Proceeder, Avalon had a second in the
Champion to his credit, being beaten byßobadil in 1899,”
said the historian. As a matter of fact, the highly in-
ferior animal started at “ any odds ”—5O or 100 to I—in a
field of four for the champion race aforesaid, and was
beaten by eight lengths. The race took seven seconds longer
than the time limit, and the added money was accordingly
reduced by one-half. So much for Avalon’s one classic
achievement. The explanation of the “Notable Racehorse”
yarn lies apparently in the following portion of it—“He
won the Sunbury Cup a few weeks' ago, and his oivner,
Mr. C. Lenny, on that occasion proudly recalled the fact
that this was the twelfth cup (!) the horse had won during
his remarkable career. Some days ago he was sent to his
owner’s establishment at St. Kilda for a spell, which it
was thought would recuperate him for the rigorous ordeal
of the approaching Autumn campaign.” The owner, Mr.
C. Lenny, at whose St. Kilda establishment the aged gee
passed out, is the “ Squash ” Lenny, whose connection
with “sport” was touched upon in The Lone Hand’s
exposh of John Wren and his law-breaking career. And
this uncalled-for certificate of sporting merit awarded to
“Squash” Lenny by a powerful newspaper has to be
considered in connection with the Argus’s respectful
interviewing of Lenny’s old partner, “Mr. Patrick Board-
man,” at the time of the surrender of the Melbourne Tote*
and with the all-round boosting of John Wren’s name*
which has of late years been so bitterly discouraging
to law-abiding citizens.
* * * *
The luck of it! Well-known crack pony Blue
Diamond was sold at auction in Sydney last week for
£255, and next day won Kensington Handicap (70 sovs.)
and attendant bets up to, it is stated, £4OO for her new
owner. Early this year Blue Diamond, as Imogene, ran
second to the crack northern-owned nag, Garches, in the
New England Cup.
* * * *
Maoriland bookmaker Cleland, who owned
Apologue till after he won Melbourne Cup, complains
that success in that event isn’t all beer and skittles. He
was “ considered fair game by all the cadgers and loafers
in the country.” Personal and written applications for
monetary assistance were without end. One letter
asked for £2OO. The writer had, he said, mortgaged his
house for £lOO and lost the lot on Apologue in the Caul-
field Cup.
* » * *
Whatever the cause, Sydney pony racing—
never a losing venture for promoters—has made marked
progress lately. It comes from a reliable source that
Kensington “gate” last Wednesday was a record, and
entry-fees made up considerably for amount of prize-
money disbursed. Existing clubs look with disfavor upon
the coming into the business from the beginning of next
year of the newly-formed Victoria Park Club, which will
cut down the list of the year’s racing days allowed each club
to 16. Pony-racecourse companies are looking to Mo to
make up the deficiency, and he has been notified of a 50
per cent, increase in fees, and the likelihood of others of
his calling being licensed to bet. Estimated that without
the proposed additions to the betting ring, the increased
tax will bring £920 more annually to each club, thus
swelling the amount exacted from pony “books,” for the
privilege of increasing their bank balances at the expense
of punters, by something over £3680. As might be ex-
pected, Ikey Moses has his back up, and vows (fearful
alternative) that he will go to work first. However, that
threat notwithstanding, the clubs’ latest levy will be paid
and the odds shortened still further to make up for it.
* * * *
It was to be expected that the chosen of 28
N.S.W. bowlers should be defeated by the selected 75
Victorians in Melbourne, especially as playing conditions
there are widely different from those of Sydney; but the
visiting side onlyfinished 11 down in the first match, and
won the second—one to the good—after a tie. In the
circumstances, the proper thing was to leave well enough
alone, and let the same team fight the third “leg” of the
rubber; but emergency men (who had been onlookers
so far) wanted to play, and, though absolute strangers to
the conditions, three of them took the places of others
who had done well and had gained the required experi-
ence. The result was that N.S.W. went under, bud bv
five only.
* •* * *
On Saturday last the Burwood lonas journeyed
to Parramatta (N.S.W.) to do battle for the Western
Suburbs Junior Rugby supremacy. The semi-final the
week previous, between Parramatta Oaks and Liverpool,
had ended in a riot, so on this occasion all parties were
studiously polite at first, and held their caps in their
hands while addressing each other. The game started,
and Parramatta Oaks promptly scored a free kick. An
awful roar from the visitors’ barrackers. “Lie down,
you dogs! ” howled a Parramatta Oaks man, in
reply, and then a fragment of a bottle took him, and he
curled up like a fly on a hot stove. Palings were ripped
from the fence, and the engagement became general.
Amidst howls of fury the Parramatta Oaks dashed into
the fray, hitting, kicking, biting, and swearing.
Lemonade and other bottles were used freely, and blood
literally ran. The police charged. They were powerless
for a time, but biffed steadily away, and getting the
visitors together, fought a way to the train, where the
lonas were embarked and despatched for home and
medical treatment. Someone recently wrote that riotous
football meant clean football. Rugby up Parramatta
way is in a perfectly dazzling condition according to this
doctrine.
■* * * *
As usual Sydney Flying Squadron monopolised
whatever battle and sudden death were lying around on
Saturday afternoon. The first excitement was caused by
Desdemona and Australian running ashore at Bradley’s
Head, which gave first and second places in the first heat
to Eileen and Zena respectively. Having qualified for
the final, Zena promptly capsized, and was still keel up
when that race started. The second heat was won
by Oweenee without any striking episodes, excepting that
Violet bolted, crossed Mona’s bows, and got disqualified.
The final saw a great struggle between Oweenee, which is
a mere skiff, and Mona, which put up a good run to
Taylor Bay. A jibe saw Oweenee have a narrow escape
from capsizing, and already one boat had filled in the
endeavor to keep up the pace ; but the skiff slowly gained
on Mona, and beat the romantically-named craft'by half-
a-second right on the line. Eileen and Australian were
third and fourth.
* * * *
Eight yachts started under the Prince Alfred
Club’s burgee for the Basin Cup race. The course was
from Sydney to Broken Bay and back, a distance of 44
nautical miles, and a succession of catspaws followed by
long calms played havoc with the prophecies of the
knowing ones. Herreshoff was minutes late starting,
and chased the fleet down the Harbor on a fair nor’-east
wind. Nearing the Heads it died away and left
most of the fleet drifting helplessly in the neighbor-
hood of South Reef. Then Herreshoff, bringing - along
the tail end of the breeze, splashed in among
the fleet and drifted helplessly too. Another stir in the
atmosphere saw the squadron scatter. Some went
straight up the coast, others followed Rawhiti far out,
where the big yacht raked sea and sky for the wind that
was not there, and they drifted slowly northward, won-
dering if Lion Island or nightfall would appear first.
Spasmodic puffs and deceptive catspaws kept their tails
up, and it soon became apparent that whatever wind
there was was being caught by Rawhiti, Fleetwing,
and White Wings. They had started level, time
allowances to be adjusted at the finish, and at
seven minutes 12 seconds past three p.m. Rawhiti
struggled round Lion Island nine-and-a-half minutes
ahead of her great rival Awanui, which bad hugged the
coast. Then Awanui, White Wings, Fleetwing, Heather,
and Culwulla followed in that order. Herreshoff dropped
out. The wind freshening a little, something like a run
started for home, but the wind died away again at Long
Reef. Awanui led the way inside with Rawhiti next,
and White Wings, Fleetwing, Heather and Culwulla
following in that order. When the time allowances were
adjusted it was found that Fleetwing had won, with
Rawhiti and Heather second and third.
La Carabine, once Rupert Clarke’s crack yacht,
sailed away in the sunset one day last week, a much
chastened and altered vessel. It has been disposed of
to a South Sea Island trading firm. It has now been
denuded of all its fittings, the state rooms are turned into
a hold, and an ugly Plimsoll mark has been painted on its
pretty sides. Instead of Youth at the prow and Beauty
at the helm, its principal cargoes will henceforth be
copra and niggers.
* * * *.
Hitherto regarded as a sprinter, pure and
simple, Cecil Healy surprised the bulk of the crowd at
Leichhardt Swimming Club’s Carnival on Saturday by
winning the 500yds. State supremacy (the first champion-
ship decided this season) from start to finish, and com-
pleting the distance with a heap of reserve in 6min. 23
l-ssec. Healy looks like filling the gap left by the going
over to professionalism of Dick Cavil], and the death of
Barney Kieran, for, like Cavill (and Kieran in a lesser
degree), he can sprint with the best, and will probably,
ere the season closes, get much nearer Kieran’s world’s
record of 6min. 10 3-ssec. for the “ five hundred.” Healy
used the “ crawl” stroke on Saturday, and turned weli.
Previously his great failing was inability to round smartly
from the basin’s end.
Assisted by the novel spectacle of the simul-
taneous release of 5000 carrier pigeons, more or less
Melbourne Bicycle Club made a valiant effort to recover
the lost glories of the Austral last Saturday and the week
before. The chiffony and frilly crowd was there all right,
some 10,000 in all; but, alas ! they were mostly in the
M.C.C. reserve—otherwise deadheads—and the gross
takings for the two days would hardly exceed £750, if.
indeed, they reached that sum. That means that
the Austral, once worth £5OO, £lOO and £2O to the
three placed men, only panned out about £9O,
£45 and £l5 ; and, as the total prize money
reached £4OO, the club won’t have much left for refresh-
ments after it has paid for the ground and the exes. As
a spectacle, the pigeon flight was a trifling circumstance,
as the birds merely rise in the air and depart hastily, like
the sparrow, or any other pest. Considering the glassy
nature of the grass track, the racing was fair, but to re-
tain popularity bike-racing must guarantee clean sport,
exciting finishes, and high speed—otherwise the thing is
a mere procession of bikes round a paddock, and there is
little joy in it. The first two requisites materialised
on both Saturdays, but the third couldn’t, by
reason of the flat grass circle, wherefore the only
delight was in watching the finishes. The Austral heats
had left in only A. J. Clark, H. Thomas, L. O. Meyer, and
10 other mostly unknown men, and the handicapper had
re-handicapped them as he thought fit. Ikey Mo, in a
surreptitious, modest sort of way, insinuated his desire
to lay 3 to 1 Scott and C. Brown, 4 to 1 Thomas, 10 to 1
others, but very few accommodated him. He gauged
the riders poorly, anyhow ; for though Clark and Thomas
made a game effort to catch the front markers,
they decreased the gap of 200yds. but little, and
chucked it at the sixth lap. Then A. C. Colvin, Meyer,
and E. Birch broke away from the bunch and rode to the
tape in that order. Meyer put in a strong sprint at the
finish, but Colvin stalled him off. Winner, who started
from the 150yds. mark, is a Gippsland youth of 20, and
had previously no notable success to his credit. He
trained on a country road. Time for the two miles,
4.25 2-5, the record for the grass race being still A. C.
Forbes’ 4.19 3-5, from the 30yds. mark, in 1900. Of the
once world-renowned Austral Wheel Race, Ichabod may
now be definitely written.
* * • *
The last race on the first day of the Austral
meeting, the five-mile Burston Handicap—was a quaint
circumstance. With two laps to go, only five of the score
of starters were still plugging away—Hehir, Eavies,
Horder, Plunkett and Millard. As the bell rang, the latter
dropped out, but before he had dismounted Plunkett
skidded on the dry grass, and the other three fell over
him in disorder. Millard promptly got speed up once
more and pedalled in an easy winner. Hehir, though
badly damaged, found his machine good and sound,
and bogged in as best he could, following Millard
at some distance. Horder, whose machine was in an
even worse condition than himself, picked the wreck up
and made a valiant attempt to carry it round to the tape,
but Dobie, last Warrnambool Road Race winner, who had
chucked it up quite a while back, pedalled round smartly
a time or two, and beat the cripple on the post for third
place. The other invalids were assisted off the course
making violent remarks about high speeds on grass tracks.
* * * *
The anticipated clever battle between hard-
punching and tough-hided Sid Sullivan and skilful Tom
Jones, for “fedder ’’supremacy of N.S.W., at Sydney
Gaiety A. C., Saturday night last, was only clever during
three or four rounds. Sullivan treated Jones’ superior
science with contempt, and now and again landed a blow
that compelled respect, though once or twice Jones stood
up and mixed the fighting, to the great delight of a strong
Jones gallery. Some heavy thumping in the fifth round
made Jones wilt, and he used his knee treacherously three
times without suffering more serious punishment than a
caution on each occasion. Another similar breach during
the sixth, and the boxers were ordered to their corners,
when, amid howls and yells in all keys and from all
quarters, Jones elevated the wipe. Crack middle-weight
Ted Nelson was, as referee, badly bothered several times,
but no professional pug ever did figure satisfactorily in
that position.
* * * *
Aunger and Dutton, would-be motor over-
landers, have already had to take to the railway through
the crass stupidity of those who had the depositing of the
supplies of petrol. At Coward, 550 miles north of Ade-
laide, they discovered that the fools in charge had left
the petrol in the sun, with the result that the tins had
burst and the fluid evaporated. So within the confines
of civilisation the desert traversers were stranded for
want of petrol and had to proceed 167 miles by rail to
Oodnadatta, where the next supply awaited them. The
stupidity of some people defies the gods themselves, and
people who leave petrol in the Centralian sun belong to
the class which wrecks the enterprises of Divinity.
THE GREATEST ORGAN IN THE WORLD.
Most people think the greatest organ in the
world is in the Sydney Town Hall. Such, how-
ever, is not the case. The greatest organ is
located in each of our own bodies, and is the liver.
When it gets out of order our whole system
becomes deranged. We are dull and heavy, and
life is a burden. The climate of Australia and
the tendency to over-eat and over-drink are the
principal causes of this great organ getting out of
harmony with the rest of the system. Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills reach the liver as no
other remedy does, opening: the clogged ducts,
causing the bile to flow, and aiding in the digestion
and assimilation of food.

▲ND AT ADELAIDE AND BRISBANE.
The only exuberance Buttan Singh, the
black-and-tan wrestler, ever displays is when he
wins a bout, and then he expresses his emotions
by leaping high in the air. Each leap would clear
an ordinary dinner-table. The other day Buttan
had an action for damages against John Wren in
a Melbourne court, and throughout the trial he
sat like a chocolate ornament. The decision was
given against the wrestler, but somehow Buttan
got it into his small, hard, Asiatic head that he
had secured a verdict of £25 damages, and he
started to leap. When he had jumped the wit-
ness box and the lawyers’ table, and was on the
point of bounding on to the exalted seat of the
Judge, he was seized by the Law and others, and
held down while a friend explained the situation.
Then Buttan relapsed into the stolid chocolate
ornament again.

BILIOUSNESS * HEADACHE REMOVED
THE EFFECTS OF A BAD LIVER.
The brilliant work of Pawlow and his pupils has brought
to light many important facts concerned with the action of
the liver. His investigations go to prove that in many
diseases the battle is lost or won according as the safety-valve
functions of the liver rise and fall. The consequences which
follow liver derangement are very extensive. The symptoms
are pale and sallow complexion, headache, a bitter taste,
coated tongue, depression of spirits, languor, cantankerous
moods, insomnia, loss of appetite, flatulency, acrid eructations,
irregular bowels, usually constipated, and pain between the
shoulder-blades. To a sluggish liver is to be ascribed the
mental deterioration, the loss of memory, and gradual extinc-
tion of the intellectual powers. When liver trouble arises
from any cause take a dessertspoonful of Kutnow’s Powder
in the morning before breakfast. The action of this refresh-
ing corrective is materially hastened by a cup of hot tea
taken about ten minutes after ; in twenty or thirty minutes
the desired effect is attained, and the entire system is
cleansed and invigorated. You can try it free of all cost!

Be careful to avoid spurious substitutes, which frequently cause
irreparable injury. The genuine and original Kutnow’s Powder is
strongly recommended by all leading physicians for the stomach,
liver, kidneys, and bowels. Insist on getting Kutnow’s, which
you can obtain at all conscientious chemists throughout the world.
Examine the package (shown in the reduced illustration) and see
that it bears the fac-simile signature, “S. Kutnow and Co. Ltd.,”
and the registered trade mark, “ Hirschensprung, or Deer Leap.”
You will then avoid imitations, and know

FROOTOIDS
For Headache, Indigestion,
Constipation, and Bilionsness.
■ 000
The immense number of orders for
Frootoids, sent by post direct to the Pro-
prietor, is convincing proof that the
Public appreciate their splendid curing
power over the above-named complaints.
They are elegant in appearance, pleasant
to take, and, what is of the utmost
importance, are thoroughly reliable in
affording quick relief.
Frootoids are immensely more valu-
able than an ordinary aperient, in so far
that they not only act as an aperient,
but do remove from the blood, tissues, and
internal organs, waste poisonous matter
that is clogging them and choking the
channels that lead to and from them.
The beneficial effects of Frootoids are
evident at once by the disappearance of
headache, the head becoming clear, and
a bright, cheery sense of perfect health
taking the place of sluggish, depressed
feelings, by the liver acting properly, and
by the food being properly digested.
Frootoids are the proper aperient
medicine to take when any Congestion
or Blood Poison is present, or when Con-
gestion of the Brain or Apoplexy is pre-
sent or threatening. They have been
tested, and have been proved to afford
quick relief in such cases when other
aperients have not done any good at all.
It is of the utmost importance that this
should be borne in mind, for in such
cases to take an ordinary aperient is to
waste time and permit of a serious ill-
ness becoming fatal.
Frootoids act splendidly on the liver,
and quickly cure bilious attacks that
“antibilious pills” make worse. Many
people have been made sick and ill by
“antibilious pills” who could have been
cured at once by Frootoids. People
should not allow themselves to be duped
into contracting a medicine-taking habit
by being persuaded to take daily doses
with each meal of so-called indigestion
cures that do NOT cure. Frootoids
have been subjected to extensive tests,
and have in every case proved success-
ful in completely curing the complaints
named.
The ordinary adult dose of Frootoids,
of which there are 72 in a bottle, is 2 to
4 —more or less as required—taken, pre-
ferably at bedtime, when constipated,
or at the commencement of any other
disease requiring an aperient, as an
auxilliary with the special medicine
necessary for the case. A constipated
habit of body will be completely cured
if the patient will on each occasion,
when suffering, take a dose of Frootoids,
instead of an ordinary aperient; making
the interval between the taking of each
dose longer and the dose smaller. The
patient thus gradually becomes indepen-
dent of Aperient Medicines.

Canada’s Immigrants.
“ W.” writes :
In the last eight months, according to cabled statistics,
216,865 immigrants went into Canada. In the 1906-7 year
there were 250,000 ; in 1905-6, 189,064 : in 1904-5,146,266 ;
in 1903-4, 130,331 ; in 1902-3, 128,364; and in 1901-2,
67,379. Apparently for 1907-8 there are going to be four
times as many as there were six years earlier. And the
London Daily News makes this a text for a sermon which
“ vehemently assails Australia for having failed to realise
that population is the basic note of great, enduring
States.” But something else besides mere numbers must
be reckoned when Canada’s vast increases of population
come to be considered. An immense proportion of the
increase is brought by a European syndicate, which
receives £1 per head for every immigrant which it
is able to induce to leave for Canada, and the
result is that the quality of the new population
is steadily deteriorating. The United States, which
still attracts immigrants in a vastly greater num-
ber than Canada does, have lately found that they are
recruiting their people more and more from the less
virile and more illiterate Eastern European races, and
less and less from the superior Western Europeans. In
1882 the United States received —from Western Europe,
563,174 ; from Southern and Eastern Europe, 83,637. In
1905 the numbers were—Western Europe, 215,863 ; S. and
E., 808,856. There was an enormous falling off in the
better class, and an enormous increase in the worse class,
of new citizens. Canada is beginning to share the same
experience. Of its 250,000 immigrants for 1906-7, only
91,263 were British and Irish, and the remainder were a
very miscellaneous lot. Last May, a big batch of Galician
immigrants arrived at Winnipeg clothed in sheepskins.
A sheepskin may be a cosy sort of dress, and suited to the
climate of the frosty Dominion ; but if too many of the
sheepskin variety of immigrant come into a country
the danger is that the people already there may
have their wages forced down to the sheepskin level.
One of Canada’s immigrant colonies is filled by the
notorious Russian Doukhobors—the crazy religious vege-
tarians who periodically abandon their farms and march
to Winnipeg to convert the world. They went in a body
in 1902 ; and in 1905 they went forth naked, and had to
be arrested and gaoled until their reason returned. Their
last outbreak was in July, 1907, when 50 men, women and
children set out to find a country “ where the sun always
shone, and work was unnecessary.” Jesus was leading
them there ; and, meantime, they lived on wild straw-
berries and such bread and onions as they could beg'.
Does anybody want Australia to be stocked with lunatics
like these? Moreover, it was recently asserted (London
Times, August 30) that there were 8000 Japanese in British
Columbia, and that 2500 more were “ expected within a
few months.” A single steamer, the Monteagle, brought
900 Hindus to British Columbia in September, and two
other steamers were “ due shortly ” with more black
citizens. The population of Canada increased by
li million in six years; but what a piebald
increase it was! The leader of the Canadian Oppo-
sition, Mr. R. L. Borden, is now campaigning on a
good democratic platform, which includes the more
effective public control of railways and similar monopolies
(the Pacific Railway practically runs Canada), the
nationalisation of telegraphs and telephones, the diversion
of unearned increment in land value from private lauds,
and a more effective promotion of home industries bj 7
Protection ; and this programme has as its first plank a
more careful selection of the sources from, which immi-
grants shall be sought, a more rigid inspection of immi-
grants, and the abolition of the bonus system except in
very special circumstances and for the purpose of obtain-
ing particularly desirable classes of settlers. In fact,
Canada begins to fear that it is becoming a dumping-
ground for “citizens” who will be bad workmen, cutters
of wages, and corrupt electors. There is no “ basic note
of great endurance” in that sort of person. Canada
is regularly held up before Australia as a State to
be envied because its people are increasing so
rapidly. The lesson to this continent is something
different. The immigrants whom we want are not
the riff-raff whom we can obtain at £1 a head, but the
sturdy Europeans of the Nortli and West, who will be
attracted by our conditions of life and la.bor. Unlock,
by taxation, the lands that the grabbers have monopo-
lised ; make Australian workshops boom by a Protective
tariff ; and then the pick of the world will want to come
here. What we are looking for in Europe is its best, not
its worst ; and Canada, like the rest of America, seems to
be getting a dangerous quantity of the bad along with
the good.
And, apropos of this, the new Canadian Immi-
gration Act, passed last year, is now in full
operation. It is built on the lines of the
Australian and American laws, but appears to
be a lot more stringent than either of them.
And yet the English papers quote the prin-
cipal provisions without either favorable or un-
favorable comment. Tlie Act, amongst other
things, shuts out the following :
(1) The feeble-minded, idiots, epileptics, insane, or
those who have been insane within five years, the deaf-
and-dumb, dumb, blind, or infirm (unless the infirm one
comes with a family which gives solid security for his
support).
(2) Those with a loathsome or dangerous disease.
(3) Paupers, beggars, vagrants, people who are likely
to become a public charge, criminals, and prostitutes.
If a prohibited person gets ashore, he may be shot
out any time within two years afterwards. The
Cape Colony Act is stricter still. It has, in ad-
dition to most of the Canadian provisions, prac-
tically the same education test as we have ; and
in addition to all the rest nobody is allowed to
land unless he has at least £2O on him. Yet there
has been no yell, either in Canada or Cape
Colony, of outraged anguish such as our Tory
politicians and press raised here when our Act
was passed. It seems as if Australia has the mis-
fortune to possess a unique brand of Stinking-Fish
—something peculiarly its own, as is the platypus.
A few specimens might be trapped and preserved
for future reference.
A Typical Misrepresentative Australian.
Quaint fact it is, but indubitable, that the send-
ing of G. Reid to London as High Commissioner
is being canvassed with much hopeful enthusiasm
in importing circles just now. There is no one
anti-Australians would rather see represent Aus-
tralia abroad. And with good reason. G. Reid
has stood always stoutly for their cause. He
was the main exploiter of the “Hatters”
lie, which did more, during its cur-
rency, to head off desirable immigrants
from these shores than the Big Drought; also
he was the politician who for party ends did not
scruple to paint Australians as murderous people
with a mania for pushing ship-wrecked mariners
over cliffs; and later he helped the yarn, now
blazoning to Australia’s detriment in the British
and American papers, that a sovereign will purchase
locally only 15s. worth of goods. That G. Reid has
bottled his venom against tariff and democratic
legislation in the last two months is condoned
by the majority of his supporters. Recognisedly
it were foolish on his part, while the appointment
hangs in the balance, to alienate Labor or Protec-
tionist sympathy. It’s a depressing circumstance
—because insulting to their collective intelligence
—that Nationalists are understood to be blind to
manoeuvres of that sort. Primarily any Australian
Commissioner will need to be Australian in
spirit; he must be at one with the general trend
of feeling throughout the Commonwealth ; and
he must have the confidence of the bulk
of the people in the country on whose behalf he
speaks. It is a noticeable circumstance in con-
nection with G. Reid that he fulfils none of these
conditions. He is a belated Freetrader in a
land which is mostly Protectionist; a State-
Frighter amidst Nationalists; the leader (and
the discredited and distrusted leader at that) of the
forlorn Anti-Sosh band which is regarded through-
out the Commonwealth as a mild joke. G. Reid as
Australia’s London representative would be about
as humorous as J. Neild as Commonwealth Com-
mander-in-Chief.

Absorbing natural history item from.
l T!, SK number
beast, or rather a numoer gha a we ll-fed banana,
with on’^pair” of wiigs of the moth family, and a subsi-
with one pair oi Of h Qf the brand affected by the
diar> brac ®> “" )h , ck beetle. Most of these birds are
”some°o them mot so" They seem to be harmless,
but disp ay a “Jimmie Pannikin 11 disposition when
writer’s pen. The female insect (it might be
the male for all I know) wears a couple of dainty aigrets,
2Mn long in its hair. The male is afflicted with the
Methody face, as per Norman Lindsay, which it vvears-
the not Norman— immediately m front of its com-
Mned anatomy, the same being made up, for the most
part, of body and legs. Aboliars will doubtless have
met these" creatures whilst sitting on the gates in the
“West of Beyond,” but they are apparently unknown
here, for on showing them to a local naturalist, he said,
after a close inspection, “ Cripes ! ” (This is Benallese for
“ something unique.”) They fly in flocks, numbering from
one hundred to one, but always in the same direction—
viz., two points east of south. If they meet an obstacle in
their flight] they will not deviate from their line, but will
rise till they go over it, be it bush, tree, building,
telegraph-pble Or elothes-prop. The engineer on the
works here computes their rate of flight at about one
mile an hour, and, after calculating the number of
trees, hills and other obstructions between Benalla
and the sea, reckons they Will reach the coast in
time to see the first Australian-made cruiser steam round.
The question is : Are these a new pest ? Or, if not, what
are they? It they fly continually in one direction, how
do they provide for the propagation of their species ? Do
they lay their eggs further south and send them round by
train, or what ? It strikes me as forcibly as one of Jimpan’s
anathemas that they are something new, and require the
earnest attention of our prominent Abo. experts.
“Cornstalk”: How’s this for a neat
dodge ? A stockowner, in a small way up North,
determined to down a pastoralist in the local shire
class for best 2-tooth merino ewe, so he fattened
up a fine-looking 4-toother, had a dentist remove
the extra teeth, entered the veteran in the 2-tootli
section, and won the prize. .
“Philo”: “Aimee” is mistaken in
quoting Blayney (N.S.W.)as the bleakest town on
any line. Oberon is. Oberon is an insignificant
township somewhere on the top of the Blue
Mountains, and all the cold draughts in the world
amalgamate there. “ Aimee ’’ reckons that Blay-
ney doesn’t know how cold it could be because it
hasn’t really tried yet. Blayney is no sport, any-
how. Oberon couldn’t possibly be any colder
than it is, but it is always endeavoring to be
colder than it can be, and to enhance its reputa-
tion by adding a fraction of a freeze to its own
record. And occasionally it succeeds.

WHY DRUGS FAIL
AND ELECTRICITY CURES I
DRUGS cure acute diseases because the latter are of poisonous origin,
and the poison in drugs counteracts or neutralizes the poison
in the body. They fail to cure chronic diseases
or make strength, as you who have tried them know. Chronic
ailments are caused by depletion of the nerve tissues, lack of recreation,
of fresh air and of exercise, the non-assimilation of food, and often by
excesses in eating, drinking and numerous other excesses and violations
of the laws of Nature which result in weakness of the various organs.
It goes without saying that the taking of poison into the body will not, cannot
cure these conditions, for poisons do not give nourishment.
What must be the means of cure? It certainly must be something which
replaces and reinstates the lost or wasted vitality. It must be a natural element of
the body, not an unnatural one. No one will claim that poisons are natural elements
of the human body, for it revolts every time they are taken. An experience of
more than twenty years with drugs and Electricity has convinced us that
Electricity alone, without any other material aid, will replace and renew lost energy
and vitality. Thousands of grateful letters from cured people tell us that drugs
failed to cure, and that Electricity, as given by the new improved

Business, Robbery, etc.
Westralian timber merchants engaged in the
sandalwood export trade are fearful lest the Chinese re-
form movement, now going strong at Singapore and
thereabouts, should spread to China. It is the custom
of the Chinese to burn sandalwood to gratify the spirits
of their ancestors, but the Chows of Singapore have
decided to save the money and let their ancestors scratch
for themselves. Should the great aggregate Chow decide to
do likewise, the sandalwood export trade will be almost
ruined. This wood is also employed for making boxes,
and for coffins for affluent Johns, but that won’t keep the
business going. #
A journalist recently took a tour through the
Maoriland Mercantile and Bankruptcy Gazette, and was
astounded to find that, in spiteof the Advances toSettlers
Office, farmers were borrowing sums as low as £l7 at 20
per cent, interest, while for furniture loans nothing less
than 20 per cent, w'as even considered. In some cases the
rate soared above that, but 20 per cent, seems to be
regarded by the Christian Gentleman whose object is to
Aid and not to Squash as a fair thing And now sundry
newspapers are remarking that the only e ®® ct .'J e Yf'LL
suppress the usurer is to gaol the brute. The ideais v y
simple. Fix a maximum interest rate by law, and tor
every pound taken in interest above the maximum let t le
shark do a week in prison. And if he tries to dodge the
law by calling the extra interest b nus, or by purchasing
the goods and selling or hiring them back to the borrower,
let him still do a week in prison for each £, and seven
days solitary for his ingenuity. #
Pericles and Lycurgus, of the green peas and
icecream barrow, are having a bad time with the Bure
Foods Act (Vic.). They have received a circular stating that
Icecream must not have more than 14,000 000 micro,
organisms to the cubic millimetre, or words to that
effect. “Me give' uppa de icecreams. No time to
counts, and de micker-organisimo—l do not know him it
I see him." ~
“Quartpot,” from Ingham (Q.) : The C.S.R.
Co. runs this district. The Ingham Council, having got
into trouble some years back, handed over the tram to
Lucinda Point, under a slipshod agreement by which the
co. carries passengers and mails for nothing, the council
collecting fares from passengers. Co., however, refuses
to wait for mails at Lucinda, and on a recent Monday
evening, although it was known that the mail-boat would
arrive from Townsville at 7 p.m., the tram left at 5.30
p.m. so about 20 passengers had to “doss” for the night.
Mails did not reach Ingham till mid-day on Tuesday, the
result being that places outside were delayed a week in
their correspondence. Some time back the council moved
to get control of the line again, but the Octopus was too
strong, and the movement fell through. The postal
authorities should certainly take steps now'. Kates for
goods are very high, and discontent is general, but the
growl is very low, for the Monster has long eais and
sharp claw's. » * *
From the land of Monkey tricks : “There are
dark days ahead in Japan, and the Japs are not going the
right way to light them. Their one idea is to get money,
and get it quickly ; consequently, some of the goods they
are sending abroad are rubbish, which may' take in a
customer once, but not twice. A short time ago a Japan-
ese firm unloaded a tremendous amount of J. and B.
Coats’ ’ cotton on the Chinese. When the Chows came to
use the pirated stuff they found that the reels were solid
bits of wood with about 12ft. of cotton on them . This is
only one instance among a thousand. The latest case is
an infringement of Chambers’ trade mark—the Japs have
been turning out Chambers’ school books wholesale.
* * * *
The Union Steamship Co. intends to be the
first down south co. to own a “turret” cargo-ship. An
occasional one or two of these craft have crept into Aus-
tralian ports, and they haven’t improved the shipping
landscape. The upper part of them is built very much
after the ordinary pattern ; but at or about water-level
they bulge out, so that their holds are almost globular—
“turret’’-shaped, in fact. On account of these wings,
their decks are feet away from the wharf. They are slow
but steady, cheap to build, and their stowage capacity is
enormous —about twice as much as you would it to
be, looking at the part of the ship that stands out above
the water. # #
The fish agents of Sydney have their own ideas
about the law of supply and demand. If the public gets
cheap fish to-day, the agents say, it will probably want
cheap fish to-morrow ; so they would rather pour a sur-
plus down the nearest drain than let the public have the
food at a reduced price. A while ago there was a very big
haul of small mullet in the Harbor, and 100 baskets were
landed at Woolloomooloo Bay wharf; but when the fisher-
men interviewed the agents the latter said they liaa
their usual supply on hand, and refused even to send
carts to take the surplus into cold storage. Most of that
catch had therefore to be either thrown back into the
Harbor or given away around the jetty.

“ Philo ” : Saw a bumptious joker neatly taken
down by a Sydney tram-conductor. He boarded the tram
with a friend at'King-street, and, handing up a shilling,
requested the conductor to take for two. When asked
how far he desired to go, he replied ‘ To hell, and
winked extravagantly to his companion. The conductor
handed him two fourpenny tickets and fourpence change.
“These are not what 1 want,” said the funny man.
“ Well, we only go as far as Botany,” replied the matter-
of-fact conductor, and went on collecting' fares. The
ruined humorist alighted at the Haymarket.
“ Trag.” : Just came back after spending a real
good time in one of the outlying districts attached to
Newcastle. There I dispensed mixtures, pills and
powders for a local medico. I came across some hard-
faced, good-natured people in this part—which has been
the case in all the mining towns I have visited. Patients
who called on the doctor were ordered to bring a
bottle, in case the medico should prescribe a liquid.
Some came on spec without the bottle, expecting pills.
Those persons usually got an empty from Bung in the
same street, for 3d. The people in the little township
drink medicine like whisky. One day, the doctor said to
me, “ Always give them something.” It was hard to kill
some of them. Well or ill, they all came along. They nearly
all asked for cough mixtures, or something strong. In
nine cases out of ten they would bring along a whisky,
brandy or sauce bottle, to carry away their remedy. On
one occasion, I gave a big buxom lady a mixture in
appearance much like water. Before leaving, sue
reckoned it was not like the original. Next day,
she called and told me she had by accident
broken the bottle, and asked for something like the
original. She reckoned what she got from the last dis-
penser would cure any pain if taken in large quantities.
I concocted something* with the sparkle and color of
<r o od whisky. Siie smiled, thanked me, and often
called again. Something, to warm the palate, I found,
was in most cases a certain cure. There was a big de-
mand for headache powders, and clients took large doses of
cough mixtures as sleeping draughts. Liniments they
usually wanted in large quantities. They often asked me
to put an extra dose of ammonia in. Everything had to
be full strength and far above normal. I never neglected
to tell them to rub it well in, and to keep the bottle well
corked. Some day I shall again call at this dispensary',
just to inquire if the people are still going strong and
rubbing the stuff in hard.
* * * *
“ Polstan ” : It’s a trick of some grain mer-
chants to fault a number of bags of grain in a consign-
ment on account of alleged bad condition of the sacks.
The pirate re-bags the grain in sound, second-hand bags,
and charges the vendor with labor and cost of new sacks,
and keeps the old bags. Any sack that will carry wheat
will make a good chaff bag, and a dozen would be worth
3s 6d. Sometimes a victim kicks, and demands the old
sacks back. When that happens the pirate sends along
a dignified reply to the effect that it isn’t customary to
return the rejects. Mostly the victim subsides, although
if he knew enough to blot himself when he gets wet, he
w'ould threaten proceedings. Then the merchant-shark,
rather than have his profitable little side-show exposed,
would disgorge in a fearful hurry'.
* * * *
“Nomad”: I’m an insurance agent. The
other evening I was bailed up by one of those no-license
chaps. He knows me pretty well, and being evidently
enthused right up to the sticking point, opened fire with
“ Say ! are you going to vote no license ! Well, 1
said, “I hardly know. To tell the truth I m not very
much interested in the matter.” “What? he shrieked.
“Not interested in thousands of homes being wrecked _
thousands of wives and children starving— thousands—
I saw right here that I was in for it, and proceeded to
strike the first blow. “Yes, old man,” I interrupted, lam
interested in those very matters. No man worth calling a
man should stand by and see homes wrecked and women
and children starving. Now, how old are you ?
three,” said the no-licenser, wondenngly. Well, said
I, “ it would cost you under £2O a year to insure tor
£lOOO, to be drawn when you’re too old to graft, or for
vour wife and children if you go under before that. Now
I know you’re not insured, and leaving out the thousands
of homes belonging to other people that are being
wrecked, and the thousands of other people’s wives and
children who are starving, just you insure yourself and
look after your own little lot first. What ? Too busy
just now' to save your wife from starvation ? Well, pop
in any time you’re passing, and we can have a chat over
that no-license. Good night.” He hasn’t popped yet.
I don’t think he will pop. Yet I want to insure that
man.
“ G. 8.” : One firm, which was not in a moder-
ately recent financial smash, got in out of the wet because
the boss’s raffish son paid more attention to private
theatricals than to business. Years ago the firm did a
good deal of business with the person who is financially
gone, and the old man had the faintest possible suspicion
that all was not as it seemed with his customer. He
mentioned this to his raffish son, who was thinking
vacantly of dogs and ballet girls while he yawned in the
face of some simple mechanical work that the office-kid
might have accomplished. The son said he would find
out something about the position, and the old man
scoffed ; he had found out many things about his son,
but his son had never been known to find out
anything save girls. Nevertheless, the doggy youth
presented his father, a few weeks later, with a memo of
the exact size of the customer’s overdraft, and on the
strength of that the old man cautiously got from under.
And the son says that he would have got the memo much
sooner, only he waited for a specially gloomy day so that
his get-up as old Smith, peering into the ledger-keeper’s
den at the Bank of Jerusalem and asking how his account
stood, might not have to endure too close a scrutiny.

“Sr”: Such a nice young lady called at our
place, the other day, and left a book for me to read. As
it wasn’t washing day, I read it. It was entitled, “ Saved
from Dishonor.” It was worse than pickled melodrama.
It was crowded with murders, suicides, and accidents,
and every second page was red with human blood, same
as the snow at Hohenlinden after the furious Frank and
fiery Hun had left off. It ended with a chapter wherein
the heroine was about to be unjustly hanged, and, at the
psychological moment when the hangman had tied the rope
round the alabaster interference between her head and her
shoulders, and was about to ring' the door-bell of Heaven,
the chapter abruptly ended with (“ For the continuation
of this thrilling story purchase the full instalments from
our traveller ”). Father had the frontispiece for his shav-
ingf paper, I wrapped up some sandwiches with the
last chapter (the sandwiches went bad), and the remaining
murders lit the morning fire. About a week later the nice
vounglady again called. Had I read the paper? Yes. Would
I buy the 58 other instalments at 3d. each, and obtain
coupons in their cash prize system, and find out what
happened to the heroine. No. “ Will you kindly return
my book? ” she next asked. I went inside and didn t find
the book, of course. The way that young lady went on
was a caution. Next day, a bell-toppered, be-frocked
male came and threatened law and police and gaol ana
insecticide and aggravated dog-tax if I didn t give the
book back, and said the easiest way out of the difficulty
was to purchase the series. I didn’t and I m not in gaol
yet. Is this another dodge employed by the people who
publish these threepenny “dreadfuls” to get on their
musty stock? For my own part, I hope the heroine was
hanged and is suspended even unto this day.

THE RULING PASSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O’Fizz is as silent
A man as you’d meet,
A man you’d not note
As he went up the street;
His trousers are frayed,
And his coat shabby-grey,
And you’d never believe
He has Prospects to-day ;
But wait till you whisper
Of Copper a while,
And you’ll find that he’s just
On the verge of a Pile.
M'Thirst is a Scot
Who is certainly dense
In the densest of ways,
Though he’s keen on the pence ;
There’s no reason to think
He has aught in his head,
For his talk is inane
Till you try him with Lead ;
Then there glistens a light
In his dull eye of blue,
And there’s a fortune
Awaiting him too !
And Ward is a Yank
Who is getting quite old,
And who’s fond of a cocktail
To keep out the cold ;
His cheek is of parchment,
He’s fleshless and thin,
But he almost grows portly
If once you say “ Tin ! ”
And you’ll find he has Riches
Galore in his view,
And he’s just a few rods
Behind Rockefeller too!
Appearances mostly
Are prone to deceive ;
It is sad to mistrust,
It is sweet to believe ;
If Delusion is riding
My poor fellow-man,
I pity his folly,
And smile —as I can —
For you see I’m a bit of
A Croesus myself,
If Silver goes up I’ll
Have millions in pelf!
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER FLAM.
THE WILD CAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sunset time at “ Dingo Diggin’s ”
(Felisferox generis)!
And I stood on Mother Higgins’
Pub verandah, wrapt in bliss. t
Ruby-red the sun was lying
On a ragged mountain peak.
“ It’s the blooming dust a-flying,”
Ventured Micky from the creek.
All the barren plain was flooded
With a wealth of liquid gold ;
Yea, the very hills were studded.
Gemmed with treasuries untold.
And I turned around, ecstatic,
To the oldest r#sident-
(How he lived was problematic,
Where he lived was in a tent).
“ See,” I cried, “ yon golden glories 1 ”
But he spat, and turned to town,
And remarked, with several “ gories
“ Blarst the stuff ; it don’t go down ! ”
VON KOTZE.


MICK SIMMONS

The Night-Relief.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HAVE you never heard the Night-Relief
Marching along the lonely coasts—
Waves that mourn for mermaids’ grief,
Their pathway crossed by pallid ghosts ?
At every port, with a challenge deep.
They take and they leave a squadron white
To guard the bars while the steamers sleep,
To keep them safe from the waves of night.
At all the ports are the White Patrols,
And the Night-Relief must make the rounds,
Where the channel-bell on a sandbank tolls ;
Where the hills drop sheer to the silent sounds,
Have you never heard the Night-Relief
Its tramping march—its challenge brief ?
'Neath the quiet moon the White Patrols
Have swaggered and swung along the bar
That lies between the wide-set moles,
Where the drowsy, crimson port lights are.
There is never a breeze since the sun went down.
Seaward away the sea is glass,
With only a ripple towards the town
Where the tugs that toil in the darkness pass;
No wavelets beat on the silver sand.
Ashore there is scarce a fluttering leaf.
Till a slow wind blows on the heated land,
Bringing the sound of the Night-Relief,
Marching in harness black and green,
To ask of the guards what they have seen.
The White Patrols that challenge them
Hear the countersign, and reply
“ Never was such a diadem
On the brows of night as burns in the sky.
Scarcely a sound have we heard since dusk,
Nor has aught stirred since long ago
When a black-fish blew with a scent of musk,
And a whistle, such as the steamers blow.” . . .
The sentries change, and the new-set guard
Are warned to remember the thing they saw
As they marched along—a ship held hard
Struggling still, in the Night Waves’ maw ;
Then the Night-Relief, with a last “ goodnight,”
Leaves the port to the watchmen white.
A gale has blown since the hour of noon,
And now that the cloak of night has come
In the streets of the quiet town, leaf-strewn,
Are only men who hurry home ;
But out on the bar a dreadful fight
Is raging between Night Waves that leap
And the cheery, sturdy watchmen white
Who drive them back with swinging sweep—
Parrying lunge and reckless charge
With valor born of their firm belief
That somewhere out on the ocean’s marge
Is coming the welcome Night-Relief—
All black and green their strong ranks roll
To double the ranks of the White Patrol.
Have you never heard the Night-Relief
Marching along the silent coasts,
When there’s scarce a breeze to stir a leaf
Of the creepers on the verandah-posts?
There’s never a silver shoulder-strap,
Or a band of gold in their grim array ;
No drums to clatter, and throb, and tap—
No bugles blown as they march away,
On their rounds to relieve the White Patrols.
Hear the challenge 1 The gruff reply !
The channel-bell at its moorings tolls,
Rocked in their wash as they swagger by.
Marching in harness black and green,
To ask of the guards what they have seen.
MAORIIiAND.
WILL LAWSON.
The Shepherd Kings of
Canterbury.
HELEN LEIGH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN ancient Canterbury, the Canterbury of horse-
back and waggonette, the traveller from
Christchurch toiled the best part of three
days across the wilderness of wind-swept plains
that lies beyond Rakaia before a curve in the road
led round a terrace and showed the woodlands
and green fields of Rangihape Station nestling in
a basin of the hills. It was a beautiful sight, and
on eyes made weary with a journey across that
howling wilderness it burst like a gleam of
Heaven suddenly revealed. So the Leigh family
changed the original name, which had been that
of some out-of-the-way English village, to Rangi-
hape, which, being translated, means “ The
Heavens Opened.”
The fringe of forest that had once grown
over the terrace on which the homestead stood
had supplied the timber for the station buildings,
the homestead itself, and also the posts for miles
and miles of fencing. When it was exhausted
from a timber-yielding point of view it was a
vast park, in which the masses of convolvuli
climbing over trees and shrubs made ideal shelter
for sheep.
The family consisted of the old people, two
daughters, Helen and Geraldine, and Roland and
the twins, Arthur and Harold, mere children.
Old man Leigh grew suddenly tired. He had
founded the place, built a fine homestead, stocked
it with first-class sheep and cattle; and he became
so obsessed with admiration of his own handiwork
that he could only sit, with a tobacco-jar on his
left hand and a cask of home-brew on his right,
and admire it.
So Roland Leigh came home in a hurry from
the college at Christchurch, where he was finishing
his education, and started out to manage the place
in his father’s stead. He learned amazingly, and
in two years was considered a smart pastoralist,
and in five years he was the most scientific squat-
ter among them all.
Lie had imported a scientist to teach him
chemistry, and study with him the relationship
between soil and fine wool. Also, he had im-
ported quite a little colony of European gover-
nesses to educate his sisters, and those two
beautiful girls had grown up on the Rangihape
Station, with the wonderful health and vitality
that belongs to the first generation on those
breezy plains, and with all the accomplishments
that are taught in the best seminaries of England
or France.
Roland Leigh was an ambitious young man.
He went on piling up the acres and the bank
account, but, unlike many of the squattocracy,
every acre of land that he grabbed he treated
scientifically to make it carry more and more
sheep. Roland used to boast openly that his
object was to make his family the wealthiest
in all Maoriland, and, perhaps, if a bachelor
Governor came in place of Hori Grey, to marry
one of the girls to him. As for himself, he
intended to become a great statesman and devise
some means of turning the rivers that rolled
through Canterbury to the generation of power,
so that in the long run, when the squatters and
their sheep stations bad passed away, as he held
they must pass, Canterbury would be the manu-
facturing centre of the southern hemisphere.
The squatters to whom he expressed these
views used to smile and inquire who was going
to wipe them out. They could not pass unless
someone pushed them. “We are the neo-
Barons,” said Greville of The Wilderness, and
when Otago and the North swamp us in the
House, we’ll vote with the rifle, and this land
will remain ours and our children’s for ever.”
“Well, the Barons of England w'ere stronger
than we, and somebody pushed them over the edge
of the board, or they fell over, or something. Any-
how, where are they now ? ”
“Well, some of them are here,” said Master-
ton, waving his hand round the lawn at Rangi-
hape, where the tennis tournament was in full
blast.
“ This insane feudal craze will be our ruin, you
see if it isn’t ! ” snorted Leigh of Rangilmpe.
* * * *
The homestead at The Oaks was rocking in the
wild nor’-wester that was blowing away cornfields,
rooting up plantations, and overturning churches
(the nor’-west wind in Canterbury is always pecu-
liarly destructive on churches) when Mrs. Collier,
as she watched her beautiful trees crashing down
in the moonlight, suddenly gasped at the sight of
a lady riding up the carriage-drive. The girl
clung to the pommel, and swayed in the saddle,
and looked as though she were in the last stages
of exhaustion.
“ It looks like Helen Leigh, but she’d never
ride all this way by herself, and through a nor’-
wester, too.” _ 0
Mr. Collier hurried out, and catching the girl
in his arms as she tried to dismount, half-carried,
half-dragged her inside.
‘ ‘ Give me shelter. Roland has turned me out! ”
“ Oh, this is too ridiculous ! He can’t turn
you out. It is as much your place as it is his.”
“ But he threatens to shoot me, and, in his
present state of mind, he would do it. I’d have
stayed, and called out the men, and fought him,
but Geraldine howled, and begged me to go ; so I
threw myself on Saladin, and galloped away.
I’m sorry now ; at least half the hands would have
followed me, and the shooting would not have
been all on one side ! ”
“That’s more ridiculous than the other
absurdity,” said Mr. Collier. “ I’ll ride across to
Rangihape to-morrow and talk sense to Roland.”
“ No, don’t do that. I’ll stay here a week,
and if in that time he doesn’t write or show his
hand, I’ll go back and talk sense to him in a way
he cannot ignore,” said the dark-haired, brown-
eyed Helen, her thin, exquisitely-chiselled features
quivering with passion.
‘ ‘ Well, dear, you come and have something to
eat now —they’ve laid it in the morning-room. I
never thought Roland would have made such a
fuss.”
“ Says it has wrecked all his ambitions —seems
he wanted to marry us to a Governor, so he said ;
and besides, he says it will be thrown at him
when he enters Parliament.”
“ Oh, rubbish ! What does a gentleman want
in Parliament anyhow ? It’s a dreadful waste of
time.”
With the dawning came Roland Leigh to take
his sister home, on condition she would disclose
the name of the man. Helen was adamant, and
announced her intention of returning to Rangihape
in spite of Roland. Roland made fearsome
threats. Helen laughed.
“ It’s no good talking like that, Rangihape,”
said Collier. “ She’s your own sister, and is just
as full of fight as you are. I feel certain that if
you stick out she'll try to force her way in. She
says half your people would follow her.”
“ They would, too, if it came to a row ; but it
won’t. I’m not fool enough to let it go so far.”
“ Well, give in gracefully while you may. Why,
you're acting as though we were shopkeepers, or
some such scum. Damn it, have some reason,
and come in and have breakfast. Much damage
done on the road ? ”
“All right—yes, the country’s blown away.”
In six years time, but for the presence of little
Esmeralda, Helen’s lache would have been for-
gotten ; but she resolutely refused to part with
her child, and was not ashamed of her. And she
had no reason to be ; for the child was more
beautiful than the mother—and that was saying
much.
The squatters had no objection to a love-child,
and the presence of Esmeralda in the world and
at Rangihape did not prevent Helen receiving
offers of marriage from various squatters. All
these offers she refused with quiet determination.
Then they wove romances around her. She
was devoted to the father of her child, but he was
a married man, and she had vowed to wait for
him, unwed, at Rangihape till he should be free
to claim her. But no one could give the slightest
indication of whom this well-beloved individual
might be.
The Hon. Westby Valentyne, who had
accepted the position of Surveyor-General of the
Provincial District of Canterbury, was bound to
be, if he lived long enough, a full-blown earl.
Just at the time Esmeralda was five years old he
arrived at Rangihape while superintending the
geodetic survey of the Provincial District.
Esmeralda was staying with friends beyond the
Rangitata. So during the three weeks that he
stayed at Rangihape and became hopelessly
infatuated with Helen Leigh, he never understood
that the Esmeralda they talked of unceasingly was
Helen’s daughter.
One day he asked Roland’s permission to pay
his addresses to his host’s sister, and received it as
a matter of course. But when he asked Helen to
