marry him she turned her dream-like face away,
and refused.
He took it very quietly, and rode away into
the fastnesses of the Southern Alps for six weeks ;
then he called in at Rangihape again and had a
chat with Roland.
Roland Leigh’s great weakness was that he
could do anything rather than fail. The prospect
of marrying his sister Helen to the man who
would one day be Earl of Humber brought out
his weakness in its worst form. It was such a
splendid opportunity to retrieve the disaster of
six years ago.
The Hon. Valentyne proposed, and —as usual—
was coldly rejected by Helen. But Valentyne
was sufficiently man of the world to know that,
although she refused to marry him, the girl was in
love with him.
He had a yarn with Roland, and again rode
away into the fastnesses of the Southern Alps for
six weeks. In that time Roland deceived his
sister with a tale that he had explained about
Esmeralda to Valentyne, and he had agreed that,
providing she would marry him at once and give
him six months to accustom himself to the idea of
Esmeralda, he would adopt the child as his own.
But for the time her name was not to be men-
tioned. And Helen consented. His offer to
adopt the little girl as his own had won her heart
right over.
* * * *
That wedding was the most gorgeous show
Canterbury had ever seen. The Wool Barons
were there in all their pomp and their strength,
and Rangihape could not accommodate them, so
tents were erected in the great park. There were
coursing matches, several days of shooting, Cale-
donian sports, and horse racing. At nights there
were Chinese lanterns, torchlight processions, and
music. On the day of the wedding Helen walked
over a rose-strewn path to the little station church,
built of gray boulders ; and when she drove away
on her honeymoon three hundred mounted men
fired a royal salute, and a guard of honor saw her
safely out of the county.
Alter her honeymoon was over, she went to live
in Christchurch. A strange change came over
Roland Leigh. He became frighttully irritable,
and whenever he left the homestead, if only for a
few hours, his first question on his return was :
“ Any word from Helen, Geraldine?”
Geraldine grew worried. Why did her brother
always ask for news—did he expect bad news?
But his manner was so grim that she dared not
ask him. The only person towards whom he had
not changed for the worse was little Esmeralda,
who was broken-hearted at her mother’s depar-
ture, and was fretting herself to a shadow.
Down at an old out-station, close to the ter-
races of the roaring Rangitata, was a ruined
garden with wild strawberries in it. The previous
year Esmeralda’s birthday picnic had been held
among the flowering kowbi groves behind the old
out-station, and she and her mother had wan-
dered into the desolate garden and found those
wild strawberries. And now the little girl re-
membered those strawberries, and desired them.
* * * *
The waggonette rolled clear of the plantations,
and sped down the Main North Road to where
the track branched off to the old out-station.
Esmeralda saw a cloud of dust approaching.
“ I wonder if it is anybody with a letter from
my mamma ? ” she said wistfully.
“ We’ll wait and see, darling,” said Roland,
pulling the horses off on to the tussock beside the
road, where he let them graze.
The cloud of dust came closer. It was evi-
dently a hired vehicle of some sort, and must
have come a considerable distance. There were
no vehicles for hire round that part of the country.
Esmeralda, standing up on the seat, holding on
to her uncle’s head, suddenly broke out with a
shriek of delight:
“Oh, uncle, it’s my mamma—it’s my mamma!”
As the vehicle drew up, he sprang from the
waggonette with Esmeralda in his arms.
“ Oh, mamma, have you come back to me ? ”
said Esmeralda, with a sob.
“ Yes, darling, and never to leave you again,”
said Helen, hugging her frantically.
“ So, Roland, you lied to me. That man
knew nothing of Esmeralda, and, when I men-
tioned her, he thought that I had gone mad. So
I told him of her, and he raved. He called me
names, Roland ; and he called Esmeralda a
name. I rushed for a pistol, but he had gone.
I took a hammer, and I pounded every bit of
jewellery he had given me to fragments. I
smashed the great opal portrait of myself in my
wedding dress ; and I wrecked the silver toilet
articles, and the Chippendale furniture in my
boudoir, that he gave me for my own. I tore to
fragments the dresses that were paid for with his
accursed money, and came home in not one gar-
ment he had ever seen me in. And I’ll kill him
if he comes here after that name he called
Esmeralda. And it’s all your fault, Roland —just
because you didn’t tell the truth.”
And Roland Leigh went down on his knees to
his sister, and begged for forgiveness.
West by Valentyne rode up to the back gate of
Rangihape Station the next evening. He put his
horse into the loose-box himself, and then walked
up through the kitchen-garden, across the tennis
lawn, where he met the most beautiful child he
had ever set eyes on.
“Is your name Esmeralda?” he said, gently.
“Yes, I am Miss Esmeralda,” said the little
lady, affably.
He picked her up in his arms and kissed her.
Esmeralda was not accustomed to this sort of
tiling, and struggled.
“Oh, you can’t get away from me,” said the
man pleasantly, carrying her towards the front
carriage drive, presumably on his way to the front
door. But the poor child thought he was going
to carry her away down the drive, and shrieked
for her mother.
Helen heard the cry. She saw the man with
her daughter in his arms, and she recognised her
husband. She swept out, grasping in her hand a
little pistol. When he met her eyes sullen anger
blazed up within him, and he spoke harshly.
“Put my child down,” said Plelen. “How
dare you ? ”
The Hon. Valentyne laughed aharsh, ugly laugh.
“ What do you mean by making me a public
show ? How dare you break up my home and
walk out of it, leaving me to be a laughing-stock !
How dare you, you vile woman ! As for Esmer-
alda, I’m ”
Bang !
The Hon. Valentyne lurched forward, his arms
tossed up, his head back between his shoulders.
* * * *
Canterbury was paralysed, and wondered, in a
dazed sort of manner, what would happen next.
At least one woman had been hanged in the colony
for killing her husband, but she was not a lady of
the Canterbury squattocracy. Still, the Hon.
Westby Valentyne was too big a man to be killed
with impunity, and, although the Law usually
allowed the Wool Barons to settle their own legal
squabbles in their own peculiar fashion, it was felt
that something was sure to happen.
The Law never faltered. It sent a detective
with two mounted troopers to arrest Helen Valen-
tyne on a charge that she did feloniously kill and
slay, etc., etc.
The hungry eyes of the police party had just
been gladdened by the sight of Rangihape,
nestling in the arms of the mountains, when it
was confronted by an armed party, under a tall,
burly man, whose check knickerbocker suit,
silver-plated bit and stirrups, and gold-mounted
hunting crop, proclaimed him the squatter. He
ordered them out of the county at once.
“ You don’t seem to understand, sir,” said the
officer, “ that I have the Queen’s warrant.”
* ‘ If you bring the Queen's warrant to Rangi-
hape I’ll bury it with you in the glacier up in the
gorge there ! ” said the squatter, waving towards
Mount Somers.
“ You don’t mean to say you defy the Queen’s
warrant ? ”
“ I do—you will have to storm Rangihape
homestead before you arrest the Hon. Mrs.
Valentyne ! Every man on the place will die
with me before you do that. These are some of
them. Ask them.”
The officer looked inquiringly at the men.
“ You bet your life,” they snorted.
“ How many men like these can you put up,
Mr. Leigh ? ”
“Just as many as it requires to protect my
sister. You see all this was my fault, and I’m in
honor bound to protect her from the results of my
action.”
“Well, sir, I admire your spirit, but kindly
allow me to point out the uselessness of it. Sup-
posing you have all the district behind you—well,
you’ll be declared rebels, and Governor Grey will
try first with the local forces ; and if you defy
all the rest of Maoriland, behind the Governor,
my dear sir, looms up the armed force of the
British Empire. Be reasonable, Mr. Leigh.”
“ Well, come what may, I fight to a finish.”
‘ ‘ Any good waiting a few days to see if you
change your mind, sir ? ”
“ No, none whatever !”
* * * *
Three attempts to storm Rangihape homestead
by the mounted police had been repulsed, and
there were dead men scattered among the shingle
ridges. Hori Grey was hurrying from the North,
and a proclamation declared all who aided and
abetted Roland Leigh to be rebels, and threatened
death and confiscation. Then the squattocracy
fell away, and left Rangihape to fight his own
battle. He had tried to make a dash to the sea-
coast, but had been driven back into the Rangi-
tata gorge, where a pitched fight had resulted in
somewhere about a score of men, mostly troopers,
being killed and wounded. After that Roland
fell back on his homestead, preparatory to taking
up an impregnable position among the fastnesses
of the Alps. Then Helen Valentyne did some-
thing. Tired of the slaughter, and realising the
hopelessness of the struggle, she tried to treat
with the Law. She offered to surrender if her
brother were pardoned. The Law would make
no such compromise. She must surrender uncon-
ditionally, and trust to the Law’s mercy. Secretly
she saddled Saladin herself, and leaving a note
for her brother, stole out and galloped off to the
Police Camp, and gave herself up unconditionally.
The astounded police saddled up, and made off with
their prisoner through the sunset that filled the
Rangitata gorge with tints of purple and gold.
Darkness fell upon them while they still struggled
through the shingle and between the groves of
sweet-scented cabbage trees. They camped in a
patch of yellow-blooming kowhi to wait for the
moon.
It came at nine o’clock, and with it the tramp-
ling of many horses. From the shingle-bars in
front and behind burst a spluttering fire, and in
five minutes the police hadn’t a horse left.
“ Will you surrender the lady ? ” a voice called
through the roar of the swirling waters and the
crash of rolling boulders.
“No, damn you !” shouted the angry officer
in charge.
Again the spluttering fire, and the police, lying
on their faces among the kowhi trees, saw the gray
ring of the squatters and their men, creeping from
shingle-ridge to shingle-ridge, closing in for a final
volley. Although the police were trapped they were
in the best position, and those squatters were evi-
dently in a desperate hurry. Perhaps, even then,
Hori Grey was coming with his veterans, and the
police lay still and shot coolly and effectively, till
that ring widened out again.
At last the moon went down behind the mighty
terrace, and gloom filled the gorge. The roar of
the river and the grinding of the rolling boulders
drowned all sound of steps among the shingle,
but the attacking force could be seen by the light
of the stars drawing closer.
Then there was a sudden rush. Shots rang out,
and the police, forming close, poured in a volley.
The squatters recoiled, and did not charge again.
Far down the river-bed strange lights were Hash-
ing, and as the dawn broke suddenly in the east
the squatters streamed away up the sheep track
that scaled the northern terrace, and vanished into
the tussock. Then across the shingle that
gleamed like silver in the rosy morning light rode
Governor Grey and his mounted riflemen. On
the top of that impregnable northern terrace a few
scattered horsemen watched his arrival, and then
vanished. The Governor’s party collected the
dead—the squatters had carried off their wounded.
Roland Leigh’s body, riddled with Snider
bullets, lay at the edge of the clump of kowhi,
where he had fallen while leading that desperate
charge. And it was all so unnecessary, for a jury
acquitted Helen Leigh on the ground that the
pistol went off by accident.
